THE BED PAGE.
“Modern Pen Drawings.”
THE “special winter number of The Studio ”
issued under this title does not replace or
supersede Pennell’s “ Pen Drawing and
Pen Draughtsmen.” It is less critical;
and the criticism that there is rarely appears as
good as Pennell’s. Its object, writes the editor,
Charles Holme, is “to give typical and varied
examples of contemporary pen-and-ink drawings”;
and though some well-known names are missed,
the volume is very interesting, and brings Pennell
up to date by force of recent examples. At the
moderate price (55.) it is within the reach of every
pen-artist and amateur.
Probably most critics will agree with the
writer for the American section, Ernest Knaufft,
that though the art of pen-drawing has been well
continued, has changed its modes and processes,
the last teri years show little, if any, improvement
on the best work before that period. The ex-
amples on this page reproduced (naturally losing
much of their effect) have been chosen for their
interest rather than for their skill; but the skill
shown in these and in other drawings of The
FENCERS, by F. H. Townsend.
Studio’s volume is not such as to silence
the man who praises the past. It is to re-
gret that so little is known of Australian
work in London that this country goes unrepre-
sented.
“Modern Pen Drawings ” contains British,
American, French, German, Belgian, Danish,
Dutch, Finnish, and Swiss examples. Italy and
Russia, Norway and Sweden, go (with Austria
and Australia) unrepresented. The British sec-
tion has an intelligible but not an artistic pre-
dominance. A survey of examples brings the
opinion that the British fail comparatively, not in
technical skill, perhaps not in sentiment, but in
imaginative power. A touch of heaviness, wooden-
STUDY, by Albert Sterner.
ness, seems to influence the British art : it
does not dance like the French, or fire
like the German and Belgian. The American
keynote seems to be unsurpassed dexterity : the
Americans seem to lie between British and
French, the British between Americans and
Dutch. The best German work (perhaps through
Jewish admixture) does not seem to hold the
heaviness commonly attributed to the German
temperament: it is at once art and inspiration.
The Danish and Finnish work approximates to
the German. The half-dozen Swiss examples
disclose no evident characteristic.
Here is the essence of the expert opinions col-
lected in the book. Of British drawings J. M.
Bulloch writes that—
We are only on the threshold of the new school, with
its bewildering ingenuity and its beautiful technique, for
the army is being recruited from the ranks of creators
who, twenty years ago, would have found their dreams
attainable only, in the many shades of the canvas or the
water-colour. In short, the discovery of the young school
is this : that, in the manipulation of simple blacks and
simpler whites, you can get the colour of the world itself,
and get it at first hand. ... We have the curious para-
dox that art has become more possible through mechan-
ism ; that freedom has emerged from the unalterable
conditions of scientific laws as they -apply to the material
of mechanical reproduction. . . . However you hare to
account for it, the fact remains that we are in the pres-
ence of a series of artists whose work in pen and ink
has been unprecedented in this country.
But Bulloch does not compel conviction.
He perpetuates the fable that
Mr. May was compelled to adopt his method
of elimination of the superfluous when work-
ing in Australia under defective conditions
of printing ; and instead of escaping from a method* he
has developed it into an art by itself, brilliantly par-
simonious.
Arid he does not accurately express the fact when
he-writes that
S. H. Sime is indebted to Beardsley, hut he has an out-
look all his own, saner, and therefore sadder, in all its
essentials.
Sime’s sanity is ’mere commonplace; to call him
“ sad ” is to thoroughly misunderstand the striking
results he achieves by deliberately superposing
Beardsley’s methods on his own and as far as pos-
sible engrafting Beardsley’s outlook on his own.
Ernest Knaufft, on “American Pen Draw-
ings
The modern school of illustrating dates from 1876 . . .
by 188 Q Abbey had become the leading illustrator in
America . . . and when, in the middle of the eighties,
process-engraving came into vogue, and Life, which had
been started in 1883, brought before the public the draw-
ings of O. D. Gibson, the climax Of modern illustration
was reached.
Foremost among American illustrators is Edwin A.
Abbey. . . . Next to Abbey must be named Charles Dana
Gibson. . . . William T. Smedley would rank with Mr.
Gibson if his work were always executed with pen and
ink, but of recent years he has used wash entirely. . . .
The mantle of Edwin Abbey has fallen upon the shoulders
of Albert E. Sterner, who is almost the sole representative
of sentiment in illustration. His technique is not so ex-
pert as that of Abbey. He is, in fact, an uneven draughts-
man ; his figures are sometimes out of drawing. But at
his best he far transcends the average illustrator, and we
find the genuine ring of art, the fine poetic feeling,
dominating his productions. ... In recent years there
has been little development in American illustrating—the
tendency is mainly toward using wash in place of pen-
and-ink. Technically, almost all the illustrations of to-
day are good, but there is little that is dis-
tinctive or that promises to become
classical.
Under/ “French Pen-Draw-
ings,” Gabriel Mourey laments
that nowadays so many books
are illustrated which are not
worth illustrating ; so that “ the
art of the book,” in the best
sense, does not receive deserved
attention.
One who knows, and has good reason
to know, book-collectors well, said to
me, the other day, quite seriously, “ Print
with nail-heads on candle-paper the re-
cipes in the * Cuisinifere Bourgeoise ’ ; limit
the edition to ten copies—not- one more—and offer
this masterpiece of typography and literature to the
true amateurs of rare books. You will sell them
more easily for three hundred, if not five hun-
dred, francs apiece than for twenty francs, for the
bibliophile who makes a career of his hobby cares for
nothing that does not cost money! ” . . .1 feel con-
vinced that the only means whereby the general standard
of art publishing in France can be raised' lies in the
abandonment of the old formulae of anecdotal illustra-
tion, and in the resolute substitution of decorative
designs such as are to be seen in England, in America,
and in Germany.
Which denotes a preference very different from
Ernest Knaufft’s taste for poetic sentiment.
“German Pen-Drawings”—Dr. Hans W.
Singer—
The number of artists who devote themselves exclu-
sively to pen work still remains small. This is probably
the case everywhere. To-day, even more than formerly,
the artist who works with his pen is an illustrator, either
of books or of periodicals, and he adjusts his technique
to the peculiarities of the photo-mechanical process by
which his drawings are reproduced. . . . Foremost
among the artists who create pen-drawings pure and
simple is Max Klinger. ... I think it would be difficult
for anybody to-day to predict exactly where Klinger will
be ranked in a hundred years’ time. But I also think
that whatever fluctuations the estimate of his painting,
his sculpture, and his etching may undergo, nothing but
praise will be found at all times for his remarkable pen-
drawings. . . . Greiner’s skill in the handling of his peri
is quite astonishing, but his imaginative powers are at
times unequal to the task he imposes upon himself. . . .
The greatest of all our illustrators, Menzel ■ . •
The “ Wodki,” here reproduced, is by Gebhard,
One of the most gifted artists in Finland, whose verve
as a draughtsman knows no bounds. He literally plays
with his pen as with his brush, and produces the
strongest effects with astonishing- facility. Moreover, he
is perhaps the only Finnish artist possessing the real
humour and the lively imagination of the good carica-
turist.
Only the glasses spoil “Wodki” as a splendid
illustration of the fine old English verses, “Jolly
Good Ale and Old.”
W. E. Henley (quanto mufatus !)
has collected his rhyming twaddle
about the Anglo-Boer war, and
has produced in “For England’s
Sake” a booklet fully worthy of
the subject. The work
has one merit —brevity.
There are nine new
pieces and three from
former books—two
dozen pages in all,
As “Prologue”
Henley gives a
■fi Vl/-4 1 AV
fine lyric, ex-
pressive of
the longing
for a life of
action and
of the
loathing of
stagnation.
He wrote
it ten years
ago, and ap.
parently it i s
now inserted to
prove that Henley
is really tli e true poet
who is -more or less
worthily praised in the
twelve pages of press cut-
tings which pad out the pam-
phlet to a passable shilling’s
worth.
Among the. anti-Boer pieces it is
hard to find even single lines with a trace of
poetic merit in them. A Lawson-like descriptive
verse in “Our Chief of Men ” is remarkable for
its comparative value, but the second half of the
couplet drops to level of the commonplace
By the dismal fords, the thankless hills, the desolate,
half-dead flats
He has shepherded them like silly sheep, and cornered
them like rats.
That is the best that Henley can offer. There is
no poetry in the miserable African game of grab.
Henley and Swinburne afford indirect evidence of
the moral triumph of the Boer cause. And the
former has done all he can to show how- deep has
been his fall. Remember his fascinating little
song to the tune of “Over the hills and far
away”—
Where forlorn sunsets flare and fade
On desolate sea and lonely sand,
Out of the silence and the shade
What is the voice- of strange command
Calling you still, as friend calls friend
With love that cannot brook delay,
To rise and follow the ways that wend
Over the hills and far away ?
Now Henley gives another piece with the same
refrain ; and begins
Sons of Shannon, Tamar, Trent,
Men of the Lothianx, men of Kent,
Essex, Wessex, shore and shire,
Mates of the net, the mine, the fire,
Lads of desk and wheel and loom,
Noble and trader, squire and groom,
Come where the bugles 1 of England play
Over the hills and far away !
This is endurable newspaper work ; but Henley
has not been content with such unattractive medi-
ocrity. He tries to rival Kipling in the slangy
music-hall .style,. and one must confess that Kip-
ling—whose most popular verse is notable mainly
for vulgarity sweetened with a dash of heroism—
never wrote anything worse than the invitation
to swashbuckling “ John ”to “ Storm along. It
is painful doggerel, as poor in technic as it is false
in its concluding statement. One falters over
such lines as these : • . ' y
“ You had only to cart ’em some thousands
of miles ; .
So you fell to your work with the calm-
est of smiles,”
merely to reach the ridi-
FANTASIA, by Varan d’Ache.
“ Storm along, John ! Storm along, John !
Not in the best of the years that are gone
Has the star which is yours thus .tremendously shone!
Storm along, storm along, storm along John !”
Which suggests a collaboration of ancient Pistol
and the bellman.
But “ The Man in the Street ” (written in 1892)
is the slime of the pit. Here the very soul of
jingoism is bared in all its folly and arrogance,
its narrowness and its thirst for blood. How is
this for complacent blackguardism ?
Hands in your pockets, eyes on the pavement, where in
the world is the fun of it all ?
But a row—but a rush—but a face for your fist. Then a
crash through the dark—and a fall;
And they carry you—where ? Does it matter a straw ?
You can look at them out of your pride ;
For you’ve had ybur will of a new front door,
And your foot on the mat inside.
And for violent idiocy bow’s this ?
And if, please God, it’s the Rag of Rags, that sends us
roaring into the fight,
O, we'll go in a glory, dead certain sure that we’re utterly
bound to be right!
Frank, at any rate. And this, one gathers, is
the creed of the declining Henley : that brutality
is the first of virtues ; that England is infallible;
that God is a British jingo and will always help to
cast out the foreign scum ; and that to die selfishly
fighting for the mere love of strife is better than
to pass calmly in an honourable peace.
The title-page of “ For England’s Sake” bears
a quotation from Shakespeare. The self-respect-
ing reader may add it to another: —
I dare do all that become a man ;
Who dares do more is none.
B.

Various Verses.
The Slain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I followed in an awful dream,
With no desire, or hope, or plan,
The winding of a silent stream
That through a shadowy woodland ran.
No voice of leaves above I heard,
No voice of gladness or distress,
There was no song from any bird
To stir that dreadful silentness.
And as that gloomy path I trod,
I found within a place remote
The body of a fair dead God
With marks of fingers on his throat.
Who slew that Being all divine,
And from iiis eyes the life-light stole ?
Ah me, the finger-marks were mine,
And mine the murder of my soul!
N.s.w.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Our Parting.
Was it but yesterday that you were here
Beside me, where, by stretching out my hand
To touch you, I could, panting, draw you near;
And kiss you till I fill’d my heart’s demand ?
Kiss’d you —dear God ! Our sacred souls were
met
In converse that no words have ever told.
And ne’er till death can you and I forget
The heart-beats that a silent kiss can hold.
Bathed in the smiles of noon I saw you stand,
With passion’d eyes and cheeks aflame with love,
Till lo ! a crimson glory lit the land
With radiance to the vaulted skies above.
Till with a fear I snatched you to my heart,
And held you where my lips could touch your hair
Could feel you mine—no mystic thing apart
From me—no dream-love of the magic air.
Could it be yesterday ? A thousand years
Would hardly span the gulf that lies between
Our last stol’n meeting and to-day. Vain tears
Are they that fall when Fate doth intervene.
Deep from my fainting heart a cry is wrung
As dim descried I see my lonely life —
A broken harp with every string unstrung,
A thing of discords and unlovely strife.
S.A.
ALAN.
The Visions.
In sweet hours of idleness,
By the wave or ’neath a tree,
Come two lovely lady-dreams,
And they pay their court to me.
“I am Love ! ” “ And lam Fame ! ”
“Love and Fame! ” they smile and sing,
“ If you ’ll be our servitor
Men will greet you as their king.
“ Sing of Love and roses red,
Sing of Love and have no fear,
Speak my praise in golden words
So the world may pause and hear.”
And the other says, “ With blood —
Blood and far-off battle-flame,
Make a wonder in men’s eyes,
And that wonder —call it Fame.”
So in hours of idleness,
By the wave or ’neath a tree,
Come two lovely lady-dreams,
And they win my soul from me.
But, at midnight, when the wind
Weaves a woeful melody,
Come far other shapes, and they—
Yea, they fright the soul in me.
Pallid faces in the night,
Eyes that cry a voiceless woe,
Bid me rise and go with them,
And I needs must rise and go.
Come I to the saddest scenes,
Fire and famine, sin and soil,
And the heart of man that sobs
In the serpent-grasp of toil.
Whisper they : “He holds the key,
Power has he to ope the gate ;
Bid him sing of death-in-life
And the nightmare of our fate.
What to us are Fame and Love,
Scent of roses, clash of swords?
We, whose ears are choked with groans,
Spurn the silk of chosen words.
Write of wounded hands and hearts,
Soiled and sunken womanhood, _
Wealth that walks with head in air
Knee-deep in a slush of blood.
Write of faces burnt and seared
By the savage furnace-glare,
Write of men who move in gloom,
Yearn for love and light and air.
Write of robbed Humanity
Ruggedly, with savage pen, f
Give the God-in-man his chance,
Freer scope and wider ken.”
So they pass and leave me filled
With a fierce desire to fight
’Gainst the callous hosts of Wrong
In the vanguard of the Right.
But I scarce can wet my pen,
Scarce a note of scorn can raise
When they come, my lady-dreams,
And I needs must write their praise.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

Our Home
was built nearly four years ago upon a rock, out of
which water is oozing at all times of the year, and
while the foundations below the P Si B Damp Course are always wet, the
walls above are always dry. As the foundation over which the Damp-
course was laid is 1J" wider than the wall, that portion which projected
was covered with cement. In one place this cement has been accidentally
removed, thus exposing tire Dampcourse, which shows no signs of
deterioration whatever.
The Fowl Houses
as well as * portion of oar home
proper, was rooted with P A B
Rubereld Reefing, nearly four year* since, and appears perfectly good
now, in fact, during the recent heavy rains and gales, when 68 miles an
hour were recorded by Mr. Russell, and during which our Tile Roof was
quite inadequate to keep out the storm, our P & B Ruberoid Roof was
perfectly watertight. This roof cost less, and is cooler than the
iron roof over the scullery.
We Always Keep
a roll of P & B Ruberoid about the place
for general use, it is such a handy mate-
rial to handle, my wife can handle it as well as she can a piece of carpet.
The Ruberoid gets a bit dark after a while, so we have painted it with
P& B Red Ideal Paint, to match the tiles in colour. We are now using
it as a Ceiling under the iron roof of the scullery, which make* the room
much cooler.
P&B ruberoid costs much less than galvanised iron, and is cooler.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Perse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 23, 1901.
The Freetectionist M'Millan and the
Protrade Bruce Smith.
These two bygone Freetectionist Treasurers
of N.S.W., W. M‘Millan and Bruce
Smith, have now both declared themselves
in the Federal Foreign-trade interest, and
their advent lends some extra excitement to
the conflict. They have both stated violently
that they are not only Freetraders now, but
that they have always been such, and a
deeply-embittered B. Smith added that any
one who said he had ever been a Protection-
ist was an Ananias of the deepest dye.
And to this The Bulletin replies that both
M‘Millan and B. Smith, when Treasurers
in N.S.W., were one of two things. They
were either persons who believed in Protec-
tion but denied their faith, like the unstable
and treacherous Peter that the bird crowed
at in Judsea, for reasons best known to
themselves. Or they were really Free-
traders, and really believed Protection to be
injurious to the country, but deliberately
and meanly and treacherously set. to work
to injure the country—also for reasons best
known to themselves. And it advances as
evidence the Protectionist tariff levied by
Treasurers M‘Millan and Bruce Smith,
and leaves the public to judge :—
M'Millan and Bruce Smith Protective Tariff in
Force in N.S.W. up to 1891.
Protection to
Import duties. Excise duties, local Pro-
duct.

* * * *
The Bulletin assumes in all kindness
that M‘Millan and B. Smith were weak-
kneed brethren who thought they believed
in Freetrade, but found it too brutal, dis-
astrous and unclean a policy to put into
practice when they were in office and had
the opportunity. So they paltered with
their consciences by practising a very large
measure of Protection (just as Reid and
Carruthers and Joe Cook did on a smaller
scale after them), and denied that they did
it, and tried to persuade themselves that
they didn’t do it, and grew purple with in-
dignation when their own Protectionist
tariff was disinterred from the recesses
of Coghlan’s “ Statistical Register ” for 1890
and shown to them. Probably M ‘Millan is
the best Freetrader of the crowd. He and his
kind constitute the greatest, richest, and
most disastrous vested interest in N.S.W.—
the great importing interest which lives by
buying a shilling's worth of goods in Eng-
land and charging Is. 9d. or so for it in
N.S.W. If local industries grow up on a
large scale to supply the people with work
and good wages, and to furnish them with
some better employment than stirring up
with a hopeless plough the top-dust of the
barren inlands, and dragging the dead cow T ’s
bones out. of the family supply of drinking-
mud, and otherwise “going on the land,’’then
the importing class to which M‘Millan
belongs is killed dead. That class has
to swear that it believes Freetrade is for the
best interest of the country whether it be-
lieves it or not. It has to believe it. It can
no more afford to think otherwise (even
though it does think otherwise) than a king
can afford to regard republicanism as the
best policy, or a Pope can afford to declare
his leanings towards Protestantism, or an
Archbishop of Canterbury with £15,000 a
year can afford to openly turn agnostic. The
M‘Millan class can no more have an honest,
unbiassed opinion about the fiscal question
than a hangman with no private means can
about capital punishment. And when
M‘Millan, the agent of a purely British
firm, advises Australians as to the best
policy whereby to make Australian industries
grow great and rich and prosperous (so that
they will ruin the M ‘Millan importing busi-
ness) he is just in the position of a Roer
general giving unbiassed advice to a British
general how to conduct the war. And all
the gang of parasites who owe their political
importance to the votes and money and sup-
port of the great foreign importing firms—
the traitorous Australians who live by selling
their country to the foreign enemy camped
in our midst—are in much the same situation.
* • * *
In short, the importing aristocracy of
N.S.W. is in the unfortunate position that
it has to believe (whether it believes or not)
that it is to the country’s interest to
crush out all manufacturing industries
that interfere with its import business.
If it can’t believe, it has to say it does.
It has to find or invent statistics to prove
that it isn’t the curse it really is. When
M‘Millan was Treasurer of N.S.W. he
protected many local industries, presumably
because he knew that Protection was good
for them and would make them grow. The
Bulletin doesn’t think so badly of him as
to suppose he practised Protection knowing
it would injure the country, and merely to
save paying an income-tax. But he didn’t
protect the local softgoods manufacture, and
it didn't grow to any extent worth mention-
ing, and the vested interest of the great
M‘Millan importing firm therefore re-
mained unimpaired. And M‘Millan is
still a Freetrader, which is natural, for his
importing business still lives on the policy
which crushes and ruins local industry. But
The Bulletin does him the credit to’believe
that he isn’t so bad as he seems to think he
is, and that if he attained office to-morrow he
would again prove himself more humane than
his savage and disgusting creed. The fact
which utterly damns Freetrade is that not one
of its advocates has ever believed in it suffi-
ciently, or been sufficiently unpatriotic and
brutal and unfeeling, to really and wholly
practise it when he got into power. All the
cries of the irresponsible M‘Millan and B.
Smith when they are out of office, about the
advantages of Freetrade, count for nothing
against the fact that the responsible M‘Mil-
lan and B. Smith in office were pretty good
Protectionists all the time. They are in the
position of the Calvinist preacher who thinks
he believes generally in the damnation of
unbaptised infants, but who isn’t callous
enough to damn any specific infant. New
South Wales has never yet had Freetrade.
Apparently it never will have it while it
trusts to M‘Millan and B. Smith and Reid
and Carruthers and Joe Cook. And
when it gets the real article and finds out
for the first time what it has been demand-
ing, it will probably pursue those persons
with guns.
* * » •
Apart from the frantic declaration of
M ‘Millan and B. Smith that they were good
Freetraders—who practised Protection—
there is very little satisfaction in the recent
utterances of either of them. M ‘Millan
declared wildly that living was 20 to 40 per
cent, dearer in Victoria than in N.S.W.,
and that Protection was the cause. As a
gentleman who lives by importing he is
bound to think like that even if he doesn’t
think it, w’hich is a difference that is more
different than appeal’s on the surface. Yet
N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan declares that
the inhabitant of Freetrade N.S.W. is worse
fed than the average inhabitant of the Pro-
tectionist States, that he lives no better,
AND THAT THE COST OF LIVING IS
MORE IN ALLEGEDLY FREETRADE
N.S.W. THAN IN THE SIX PROTEC-
TIONIST STATES OF AUSTRALASIA.
On page 412 of. the “ Seven Colonies ” for
1899-1900 Coghlan declares that the N.S.W.
inhabitant gets 10381 b. of food per annum,
while the average inhabitant of the six Pro-
tectionist States gets so much more
that he raises the average for the whole
of Australasia to 10791 b. per inhabi-
tant per annum. In other words the
denizen of the allegedly Freetrade State,
which is crushed by the dead weight of the
great importing monopoly of M ‘Millan and
his class, gets 10381bs. of victuals per annum,
while the people of the six Protectionist
States average about 10901bs. eachperannum.
Further, Coghlan sets forth on page 417 of
the same work that N.S.W. gets 217 gallons
of alcohol annually for every 100 people,
while in the six Protectionist States the
supply is so much larger as to raise the
average for all the seven States to 241 gallons
per 100 people. Also he states on pages 419
and 420 of the same book that the standard
of living is about equally high in all seven
States, but that everything is so much dearer
in Freetrade N.S.W. that it costs
£‘39 14s. lid. per inhabitant per annum there
to live up to that standard, while the
six Protectionist States apparently feed,
clothe, and amuse their people equally
well (we have Coghlan’s assurance
that they do it equally well) for about
£36 per inhabitant yearly. A.t all
events they do it so much more cheaply
than it is done in N.S.W. that they reduce
the average cost of living for the whole of
Australasia to £36 19s. sd. annually per
head.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Statistician is not an impor-
ter, or a Parliamentary candidate sup-
ported by the importing interest, but a
State official with no axe to grind. As no
Freetrade Government has ever accused him
of issuing unreliable calculations, he is en-
titled to some small credit. And he sets
forth distinctly, on pages 419 and 420 of his
“Seven Colonies,” that food, liquor, cloth-
ing, rent, and practically everything that a
human being can want to use, costs more in
Freetrade N.S.W. than in the six Protec-
tionist States of Australasia, while at the
same time he declares that the people of
these Protectionist States enjoy as good liv-
ing as do the inhabitants of N.S.W. He
sets forth that the Freetrade State is the
dear State—not the cheap one. And
The Bulletin puts this independent
authority against' the assurance of
M'Millan: —who lives by Freetrade and
can’t possibly be an unbiassed witness when
defending the policy on which his affluence
mainly depends—that things are otherwise.
It further sets against M'Millan’s assurance
that clothing is from 20 to 40 per cent,
dearer in Victoria than in N.S.W. the fact
that N.S.W. imports more drapery, blankets,
wearing apparel, &c., from Victoria than
from any other country on earth—Great
Britain only excepted ; and also buys from
Victoria great quantities of hats, boots,
gloves, furniture, &c. It can’t surely be
on account of the excessive price that it
makes all these purchases.
* * * *
The visible reason why the Protectionist
States should be cheaper places to live in
than N.S.W. is that they have in a much
greater measure thrown off the yoke of that
remorseless shark and commercial parasite,
the importer. The importer is an individual
who charges exorbitant profits when there is
no local competition. When local com-
petition becomes serious in any branch
he necessarily cuts down profits in
that branch to the uttermost till he
has killed the new Australian industry
and turned those dependent on it out
to starve. Then he puts prices up again.
While his prices are low in one branch of
his business in order to kill off some local
industry, he lives on the profits of other
departments of his comprehensive trade.
This is pure business. The importer has to
be an enemy of local industry in order to
live. And he has to believe, in order to live,
that the Freetrade system which makes his
disastrous existence possible is a blessing
to the community. * In this hard world
people have to believe strange things in
order to live.
• • * •
Furthermore, M‘Millan and B. Smith
declared that Victoria’s “burdensome sys-
tem of Protection ” was maintained in order
to find employment and sustenance for only
100,000 persons. But, as already shown,
Protection has evidently supplied' Victoria
with cheaper food and clothing than N.S.W.
enjoys, and a comparison of the wages paid
in the two States (as set forth on pages 967-
1013 of Coghlan’s N.S.W. “Statistical
Register,” and on pages 602-606 of the
official “ Victorian Year Book ”) establishes
that the Victorian, on the whole, gets the
better wages. Therefore it might seem to
any rational being, who isn’t an importer or
other interested party, that if Protection, in
addition to these blessings, finds sustenance
for one person, let alone 100,000, it is amply
justified. Also both M‘Millan and B.
Smith got off the old gag about the Protected
State, after 30 years of Protection, being no
more prepared to do without Protection than
it was 30 years ago, though why any State
should want to do without a policy that gives
it cheaper living, better wages, and the means
of supporting, as M‘Millan admits, 100,000
extra people, passes human comprehension.
M‘Millan himself has had food for 50 years,
and is no more prepared to do without food
than he was 50 years ago, and he has been
importing for at least 20 years, yet is ne
more prepared to do without importing
than he was 20 years ago. And further,
both M'Millan and B. Smith pointed out
that N.S. W. exported more than its
neighbour (because it used less of its
produce in its own manufactures), and im-
ported more (because it manufactured less
for itself), and they asked the public to ad-
mire this great system of hauling things up
a plank on to a foreign owned ship, and
hauling other things down a plank off a
foreign-owned ship, and being a great public
lumper on a wharf, as the summit of human
greatness, and the beginning, middle and end
of human happiness. And on top of all,
they pointed out that Victoria, with 87,000
square miles of land, didn’t hold quite so
many, though very nearly as many, people
as N.S.W., with 310,000 square milts.
* * * *
* And, finally, M'Mili.an, the great im-
porter, urged the people of N.S.W. to stand
by the policy which keeps down local indus-
tries, and thereby brings lots of business to
the M‘Millan importing class, and retains
■the M‘Millan vested interest in a
profitable state. Also, M'Millan, the Free-
trader, said he was in favor of raising
£8,000,000 by customs duties, but these
duties were to be carefully arranged so that,
they would only take money from the people,
and increase prices, but wouldn’t find work
or wages for anybody, or raise up any indus-
try, or in any way interfere with the import-
ing business or the great importing vested
interest. Also, he said he favored an excise
duty on locally-grown sugar (the importer
whose head-quarters and profits are in
Britain naturally believes in taxing Aus-
tralian rather than British goods), and post-
age on newspapers, both of which he had
a good chance to impose in the years
when he was Treasurer of N.S. Wales
if his practical faith in Freetrade
had only been strong enough. And B
Smith, the representative of the legal fra-
ternity—the most strongly-protected class
on earth—agreed in a general way with
M'Millan. And between the Freetec-
tionist gentleman and the Protrade gentle-
man, both of whom protected N.S.W. in-
dustries with such vigour when they held
office in a Ministry which shuddered at the
idea of doing such a thing, it appears that
the country may yet be saved.
Chaos and the Kanaka.
TJp in the happy Piebald Land Brisbane Courier
is demanding in a bitter and desolating shriek
that the Fiscal Issue, and everything else save
only the Nigger Issue, shall be dropped at the
approaching Federal elections, and that the Pie-
bald State shall go solid “On the one question of
saving its blessed Kanaka. But while it declares
that Queensland, alone among all the Common-
wealth States, shall fight the election on the sole
question of preserving and encouraging its nigger,
it also claims (in its leading article of 4tli Feb.)
that Queensland is the one State of the Common-
wealth which has systematically discouraged its
nigger. In fact, the Courier’s Kanaka attitude,
and many other people’s Kanaka attitudes, are
getting to be so mixed that it is difficult now to
say where anybody is. In its leading article of
15th Jan. the Courier declared that the Queens-
land sugar industry could get on without black
labor, and pointed out that there is now more than
double the area under cane that there was in
1891, while the Kanakas, instead of correspond-
ingly increasing, has actually diminished. The
inference is that more than half the Queensland
sugar-crop is now raised without nigger labor.
The Courier maintained that all the crop could be
raised by white labor, if only the change was
made gradually. Then Premier Barton pro-
mised to make it gradually. He said that the
Asiatic would be shut down on immediately, but,
while the Kanaka would not be allowed to in-
crease (he isn’t increasing in any case), his extinc-
tion would be done by degrees, and with all due
regard to the Queensland planters’ necessities.
Whereupon the Courier cursed Barton by all its
gods, and its Joss and its Confucius, for promis-
ing to do exactly what it had demanded he should
do. It now declares that Queensland is already
restricting colored labor more than any other
State, and it yells for Barton’s scalp because
he is going to ruin Queensland by doing what
the Courier says it is already doing on its
own account. All these things are a good deal
tangled up.
* * * *
And the Courier, after declaring that the
Queensland sugar industry doesn’t require Kanaka
labor, rakes up the old yarn that the attempt to
do without the islander in the late 80’s was a
failure and only proved the indispensability of
Tommy Tanna :
We may point out that the moving spirit in this legisla-
tion was the same Queensland statesman who, after an
abortive effort to abolish Kanaka labor, was compelled to
restore it in 1892.
It is at least time that this useful old lie was called
upon to drop down dead in some convenient place.
For Griffith was not compelled by any public
necessity, or by anything at all save his desire to
angle for the Fat Man vote, to restore Kanaka
laborinlß92. It was somewhere about 1885 thatthe
planter’s supply of Kanakas was put under severe
restriction, with an intimation that it would
presently cease altogether. The ■ planters, who
really thought their Polynesian ivas essential, took
alarm, and the cane area in Queensland began to
decrease. But the decrease lasted a very short
time, and then, with its Island Labor supply
growing less every day, the sugar industry started
to expand again rapidly. Between the period
when the democratic Griffith restricted the
Kanaka supply and started to abolish the nigger
by instalments, till the year when the new Tory
Griffith restored the Kanaka traffic, the area
under cane in Queensland was as follows
1885 .. .. .. 59,186 acres
1886 54,010 „
1887 51,815 „
1888 47,340 „
1889 49,741 „
1890 50,922 „
1891 50,948 ~
1892 .. .. .. 55,520 „
The industry had been growing steadily for four
years when Griffith restored the Kanaka trade.
It had got over the shock of losing its Tommy
Tanna. It had to a great extent solved the prob-
lem of raising sugar without that murderous Poly-


nesian. The kanaka was restored, not to prevent
the industry being killed, but to prevent it pass-
ing out of the hands of the big slave-driver and
into those of the small farmer. The success of
Griffith’s white labour scheme is Barton’s full
justification ; and that was practically what the
Courier itself said on January 15, until it slewed
round suddenly and started saying the other thing
on January 26.
* * * *
But there are other and even more elaborate
complications about the Federated Kanaka.
Away back in 1899, when Sydney Tele-
graph was a howling, cursing, raging Parochialist
organ, it published articles declaring that Federa-
tion was merely an elaborate device for flooding
Australia with black labour. For instance, this
demoniac yell is from the Telegraph of June 15,
1899:
All that is required to complete the scheme is that
N.S.W., on the 20th June, shall sign the bond, and
thereby pledge her lands and revenues for the future sup-
port of the most gigantic monopoly in the whole civilised
world, which monopoly would have as its natural corol-
lary the wholesale introduction of cheap colored labour.
Once the deed is signed, the greatest boom on record will
be in full swing on the London Stock Exchange, and
influential Australians (residents of Adelaide and Mel-
bourne for choice) will have magnificent opportunities of
becoming directors of great corporations, whose head
offices will be in London.
As George Reid turned Federalist soon after this
it seems, by the Telegraph’s showing, that he
and Barton equally joined in the Black Labour
conspiracy. Yet when Premier Barton an-
nounced that he and his Ministry would abso-
lutely and wholly shut out Asiatic coloured
labour, and that they would also abolish Poly-
nesian coloured labour by degrees, he went about
as straight as a man could go for a White Aus-
tralia. Reid himself acknowledged this, and
merely complained that he didn’t get sufficient
credit for being the inventor of the resonant term,
‘ ‘ White Australia ” —a term, by the bye, which
he didn’t invent.
* * * #
And about this same time (30th January)
Retd’s own special organ, the Sydney Telegraph,
published a furious article by one F. Wegg
Horne, declaring that nigger labour was neces-
sary in the North, and denouncing Barton’s
proposed abolition of it as a Protectionist device
of the most foolish and objectionable kind. _ There
was no doubt whatever expressed in this out-
pouring that the Federal Protectionist Govern-
ment was wholly opposed to coloured labour, and
again and again the Protectionists were objur-
gated as the party that intended to obliterate the
valued and indispensable Kanaka. And though
the Freetrade party, of which Reid and the
Daily Telegraph are such prominent upholders,
was not put forward as the pro-nigger party,
the writer, by fastening the. alleged guilt
of the anti-Black Labour policy on the
Protectionists, made the implication reasonably
clear. Yet after the Freetrade leader, Reid, had
complained of Barton for allegedly stealing his
anti-nigger policy (thereby distinctly implying
that Barton had an anti-nigger policy, stolen or
otherwise), and after Reid’s organ, the Sydney
Telegraph, had published its bitter shriek of
30th January denouncing Barton and his Pro-
tectionist party for its anti-nigger policy, the same
Reid organ, in a leading article of 14th February,
furiously declared that Barton was in favor of
Black Labor, and denounced him for that. Some
extracts:
It is something to the credit of the Government that
they recognise this and accept pro-Kanakaism as a logical
part of their vested interests conserving policy. ....
Practically-speaking, pro-Kanakaism for an indefinite
period of 3'ears is the same thing as pro-Kanakaism for as
long as the protectionist policy of the Ministry holds
good.
* * * *
So the situation may be summed up as follows :
(1) The Freetrade Brisbane Courier curses Barton and
his Protectionist Government for being wholly against
Black Labor, and urges the people to support Reid,
whom it puts forward as a man who will probably support
Black Labor.
(2) The Freetrade Sydney Telegraph (Reid’s special
organ) publishes the article of F. Wegg Horne, on 30th
January, denouncing Barton and his Protectionist Party
for wanting to abolish Black Labor.
(3) The Freetrade Sydney Telegraph, on 14th February,
publishes a leading article in which it denounces Barton
and his Protectionist Party on the alleged ground that
they want to preserve Black Labor.
(4) The Telegraph doesn’t say which side Reid is on,
nor does it denounce him for being on either side.
(5) Reid doesn’t say which side he is on himself. He
declares that Barton borrowed his “White Australia”
cry, which, without committing him, helps him to pose
as an anti-Black Labour man. At the same time, he
doesn’t repudiate the Courier’s recommendation of him
to the electors as the man who will stand by Black
Labour. In State politics he is known as a man who was
supposed to be hostile to nigger labour, yet allowed the
Hindoo influx into Northern N.S.W. to go on absolutely
unchecked.
(6) Meanwhile the Courier says, on 15th January, that
Queensland can do without the nigger, and on 26th
January that it can’t do without him.
(7) And, on top of all, while the Freetrade Telegraph
denounces Barton, as a sort of side issue, for favoring the
Protectionist idea of taxing imported sugar, William
M'Millan, the second or third boss of the Freetrade
party, advocates taxing local sugar, and neither he nor
that other leading Freetrader, B. Smith, seem to commit
themselves either to a Black or a White Australia.
Amid all this confusion, where does the Kanaka
question really stand ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Protected Freetrade Paper.
The Sydney Telegraph had a tremendous attack
of pious fervour, the other day, when it broke out
into a scream about “The Immorality of Protec-
tion,” and got this deafening top-note off its
chest: —
In our issue of yesterday we published an excellent
letter from a correspondent under the above heading.
His contention was that principle should be regarded
more than apparent expediency in the framing of laws
for public government; that the opinion of a majority
can never make right that which is morally wrong. “The
money which any man has earned (which is his own),” he
wrote, “ he has the inherent right to spend where and
how he can do so to the best advantage, subject always
to the claims which may be made upon him or it for the
purpose of government, or, in other words, for the pur-
pose of needful State revenue.” This is a true statement
of the ethical relation of the Government to the indi-
vidual. There is no moral justification possible for such
an act of legislation as empowers a section of the com-
munity to take for its own benefit, by means of Protective
taxation, any portion of a man’s earnings. Such an act
is unjust and absolutely opposed to the Federal spirit—
the spirit of fraternity and equal dealing—about which
we heard so much while Federation was yet “in the air.”
Yet, “ though the money that a man earns is his
own,” about £50,000 a-year is taken from the men
of N.S.W. in order that the Daily Telegraph and
such-like hypocritical prints may be carried gratis
through the post. And a section of the community
(the section that reads the Telegraph) takes for
its own benefit the earnings of other people
in order that they may get the Telegraph without
paying for the carriage of it. And the Telegraph
is aided and bolstered up by this protective taxa-
tion ; so long as a penny Brisbane, Adelaide or
Melbourne paper, even if it is posted in
NSW., is charged postage in N.S.W.
while the local penny paper goes free, the
latter is protected to the extent of 50 per cent, and
its circulation and profits are thereby much
increased. And the daily Freetrade paper loves
Protection (for itself) with such a great love that
„o NSW. Ministry has dared abolish this Pro-
tection, lest the enraged and unprotected * ree-
trade press should blacken its character till it was
<< written out of office.” These latter are the very
words which one Sydney Freetrade newspaper
man used to a Minister who was suspected ot an
intention to cease protecting the Freetrade press.
The Freetrade papers denounce with exceeding
great fury every kind of Protection except
the one they enjoy themselves, and about
it their silence is something incredible,
wherefore these Protected Freetrade papers
stink in the nostrils of every decent and intelli-
gent citizen. The Protected Freetrade Tele-
graph clutching with both hands at its 50 per
cent, bonus and groaning bitterly in its soul about
the “ Immorality of Protection ” for other people,
is a spectacle for the gods. And the Protected
Freetrade S.M. Herald and Evening News and
Launceston Examiner and Perth Morning
Herald apd many others all share the same
hypocrisy.
Too Thynne!
Thus A. J. Thynne in Brisbane Courier :
It seems a weak and unreasoning thing to do to strangle
an industry which employs more than two-thirds of
liberally-paid white labour and less than one-third of
fairly-paid coloured labour.
The two-thirds is probably obtained by reckoning
all the Japs and Chinese employed in Northern
mills as “white labour.” Further, Thynne has
the cold-blooded audacity to assert
As Queensland has led the van in the exclusion of un-
desirable coloured labour she may also have to take a
part in preserving intact the cardinal principles of a
democratic Federal Constitution.
The idea of Queensland, the mongrel province
par excellence, taking the lead in excluding the
alien is particularly good. Probably Thynne
imagines that the other States banded together
and forced Queensland to swallow the piebald
hash against her will. Probably Thynne
also imagines that the Queensland blackbird
fleet is employed in preventing the Polynesian
rushing in to the Queensland plantations.
In fact there is no saying what such a
man may imagine. The concluding stroke re
Bananaland’s coming forward to intact
the cardinal principles of a democratic Federal
Constitution is really superb, coming as it does
from a member of a Government that has done
everything in its power to choke democratic pro-
gress for years past. It may be safely asserted
that if the Q. Govt, nominees are returned to the
Federal Parliament they will infallibly be a
stumbling-block and a hindrance in the way of
Australian advancement.
——
The Railway Disaster.
Australia is thinking of the most recent N.S.W.
railway disaster, which has killed seven persons,
and permanently or temporarily injured many
more. On 15th inst., a train from Sydney to
Hurstville —a suburban station nine miles distant
—ran off the line between Sydenham and Tempo,
after completing between three and four miles of
its journey. The engine and first carriage were
wrecked. The other carriages fortunately remained
on the line, stopped by the automatic action of
the brake when the couplings broke.
The Bulletin does not pretend to know for
certain how the disaster happened; but, under
similar conditions, with the. assistance of Bad
Luck, it will undertake to cause a similar disaster
at any time.
The conditions are, first, the line; second, the
train ; and third, the time and place.
The Hurstville line is the Cinderella of Syd-
ney suburban lines. It is “a working-men’s
line ”; working-men are the residents along it;
there are very few “first-class” travellers. So,
in comparison with the aristocratic line to Strath-
field, the pet of Sydney railway suburbs, it is
worse built and worse maintained. It is not so
well laid —the rails are merely “dogged ” down to
the sleepers, instead of being properly braced by
“chairs”; it is not so well ballasted; and it is
not so well looked after. From an expert’s point
of view it is an inferior line, just as the Strath-
field line is a superior line. It is not fit to run
heavy trains over, at frequent intervals, at high
speed. This is the First Cause of the disaster.
Second cause—the train. A 39-ton engine of
the suburban “tank” type is not fit to draw a train
of eight carriages, to contain 100 passengers each,
at a high speed over an inferior line. The Hurst-
ville line is a working-men’s line ; and the return-
ing workers crowd the trains as the day closes.
The 5.55 train is alleged to have originally
consisted of six carriages, reducing the
risk. The traffic authorities are cramped at
Redfern (perhaps the primary cause of the
disaster is the inadequacy of Redfern station);
and could not get more trains out at the
busiest time of the day. So what did they do?
They added carriages —first a seventh; then an
eighth—the last straw that broke the little engine’s
back. A train, fully loaded, too heavy for the
engine to manage—that was the Second Cause of
the disaster.
The Third Cause —the circumstances. How
did the disaster happen, when similar circum-
stances have been met thousands of times without
disaster ? Probably in this way. The train started
a little late ; owing to the inadequacy of Redfern
station trains frequently start two or three minutes
late between 5 and 6 p.m. Speed was probably
accelerated in order to make up time. The Bel-
more junction points were crossed at a high
speed, and the train oscillated. Then—the
brakes were applied to this 39-ton engine with
its huge load. The engine stopped first, and
the momentum of the load lifted it, still oscillat-
ing, from the rails. That, mathematically, had
to happen—with decreased speed at the head
of a long and heavy train before the brakes could
reduce the momentum of the tail. Then—there
may have been a loose rail; probably there was ;
probably some of the Railway Dept’s. cheap
makeshift bolts had been loosened. Once the
light engine was shifted from the rails, with the
heavy train tumbling it on, the least irregularity
of pressure would cause a deviation of direction
sufficient to throw the engine off. Once off—
anything.
So, in all probability, very simply, very shame-
fully, the disaster happened.

The Victorian Old Age Fension Muddle.
Almost the last utterance of George Turner in
his capacity as a purely Victorian statesman was
that it would be necessary to reduce the amount
of the Victorian Old Age Pensions and grant
fewer of them. The bill under present conditions
will run to £300,000 a year, and George Turner’s
Government reckoned on only half that sum.
Having regard to the experience of Maoriland,
and allowing for the very much larger population
of Victoria, and for certain extra benefits not
included in the Maoriland scheme, The Bulletin
confidently reckoned the Victorian bill at
£400,000 a year, and how Turner made his
incredible mistake passes comprehension. Any-
how, Turner is out of Victorian politics now, and
Premier Peacock has to face the difficulty.
Assuming that the State can’t spare £300,000 a
year out of its present revenue and with its pre-
sent expenditure, he has the option of various ways
out of the financial pit. He may pay the
pensions out of loan money as N.S.W.. is going to
do; the N.S.W. Old Age Pensions will probably
cost £400,000 a year, but as the State uses £BOO,OOO
a year of loan money for ordinary expenditure
purposes the good old loan pays for them twice
over. At the same time Victoria has kept com-
paratively clear of this infamous public swindle in
the past, and it would be a pity if it sank
at this late hour to George Reid’s degraded
financial level. Another way is simply to levy
enough extra taxation to meet the country’s
obligations and redeem the country’s promises.
This would doubtless mean a fight with the House
of 48 Rich Landlords, but if Premier Peacock
won’t fight in such a good cause then he is about
the poorest wet-rag that ever passed for a
democratic statesman. Or if the country finds
it necessary to break faith with some of its
pensioners, it might break faith with the richest
ones instead of the poorest, and cut down the
huge allowances to Andrew Clarke and others
who can stand the shock. Victoria has two pen-
sion lists. One, of £300,000 a year, is for the
benefit of about 15,000 worn-out old pioneer
citizens of such unimpeachably good character
that they surmounted all the rules and regulations
for the prevention of pensions embodied in
Turner’s Act. The other pension list, of about
£320,000 a year, is for the benefit of a few hun-
dred ex-politicians and ex-public servants. The
first lot are very poor people, who have enjoyed
their very humble pensions for two months; some
of the second lot are very rich people, who have
enjoyed very large pensions for 32 years. If Vic-
toria must needs go back on one crowd, in
order to avoid further taxing its gang of land-
lords, it is a cowardly, wicked, crawlsome thing to
select the very poor and feeble and aged for the
victims. But, as a matter of fact, the Victorian
revenue has increased so very largely this
year that there would be abundance of money
and more than abundance to pay the Old Age
Pensions—only the cash is wanted to pay for
contingents, and to liquidate the bill for butcher-
ing Boer fathers who never did Australia any
wrong, and burning Boer farms, and turning-out
Boer mothers and infants to perish on the veldt.
In the early days of the war, when the blatant
Fat Man of Melbourne was babbling at gluttonous
banquets about the sacrifices he was prepared to
make for the Empire, The Bulletin prophesied
that he would take all possible care, as usual,_ to
pass the sacrifice along, and he is evidently doing
it. The rich man refuses to tax himself one extra
copper for war purposes, despite the enthusiasm
with which he stood up and gave several dozen
cheers for himself in admiration of his own disin-
terested patriotism and his readiness to pour
out his goods and chattels like water in the
good cause of Jingo. The Sargoods and Em-
blings and Cornelius Job Hams and other
rich members of the House of Bloated Landlords
didn’t fight personally, and didn’t pay personally
for their own personal Jingoism. And now the
newly-granted Old Age Pensions are to be in a
great measure withdrawn, so as to save money
to cover the bill for their “loyal’’fury. Already
instructions have been issued to the magistracy
to further overhaul the characters of the pen-
sioners, and to try and find excuses for withdraw-
ing many of the pensions already granted ; also,
when Parliament meets, it is proposed to make
an all-round reduction in the amount of the
allowances. Still, if the proposal does eventuate,
Parliament has an alternative. It can call the
Peacock Ministry a miserable gang of hypocrites
and backsliders, who truckle to the rich, and
break faith with the poor, and therewith fire it
into oblivion.
Our Black Brother.
It is truly an advantageous thing to be a nigger in
Westralia. For instance, here is how they hang
the guileless aboriginal in that pleasant land of
slavery. The nigger accused of murder is brought
in by the police, probably having had to tramp
hundreds of miles on foot. The police also bring
in the witnesses against the nigger. Nobody
brings in the witnesses for the nigger, so there
never are any. The police are paid so much per
day for the keep of the witnesses against the
prisoner, and as they make a good profit out of
payment they have a direct cash interest —a pretty
large one, too—in getting up cases. The gang of
aboriginal witnesses against the prisoner are kept
in the custody of the police till they are wanted,
therefore the police have every opportunity to
coach them, or to terrorise them, into giving the
evidence wanted. As the policeman’s promotion
largely depends on his success in “sheeting
home crime and bringing the perpetrators to
justice,” and as it is bad for him if it turns
out that he has been drawing Government money
for the keep of a lot of useless nigger witnesses, he
probably does usually coach them. Even if the
witnesses don’t give the right kind of evidence,
however, it doesn’t matter so much as one might
suppose, for the interpreter is almost always a
black “police boy,” and he has every reason to
interpret the evidence to suit the police case. The
court and the jury and the Crown Prosecutor are,
as a rule, utterly ignorant of the native dialects,
and the whole show rests on that “ police’ boy.
The accused has no lawyer—no interpreter of his
own —no case—nothing at all. It isn’t even
certain whether the charge is explained to him or
whether he is the right nigger; all .depends on the
“police boy.” There are even cases when the
interpreter’s defective English has to be trans-
lated by the arresting constable before it is intelli-
gible to the bench. So the accused is duly scragged
after trial by a jury, which is the palladium of
British liberty, and all is well.

Another instance of the Vic. Crown Law De-
Pint’s tender regard for its own witnesses is
anorded by tbe_ treatment of two temporary excise
officers—alias informers—at Ballarat. As long
ago as last Sept. 20th the two were witnesses in an
t> a/Ptv sly-grog-selling case at Scarsdale, and
H.M. Dickson, in dismissing the case, madestrong
comments on the manifest perjury committed by
someone. The Crown was from the first very shy
of proceeding against its own minions, but being
strongly urged thereto by the local press, it finally
brought them before a beak. They were duly
committed, but the Crown apparently determined
the matter should stop there, for last week Judge
Holroyd, at the Crown’s request, postponed the
matter for the third time, without any stated or
manifest reason, the pair being released merely on
their own recognisances. It looks very much as
if the Crown authorities aforesaid are anxious
for the men to take leg-bail. Contrast this
kindly, considerate treatment with that meted out
to the defendant in the next case. She was
charged with the same offence—perjury over a
sly-grog-selling matter—but her little difficulty
was quite recent. And the Crown, instead of post-
poning her trial until she had time to get away to
parts unknown, arrested her in great haste, got
her promptly committed, demanded heavy bail
pending trial and put her in the dock at the very
first opportunity. But her alleged perjury was
committed in her own defence ; she wasn’t a
Crown witness.
Not only the worker, but the employer, now
begins to find that in assisting that gigantic trad-
ing organisation masquerading as a charity—the
Salvarmy—he is merely providing a rod for his
own back. In Melbourne the “ Army ” now runs
a big printing establishment and engraving busi-
ness at cutting rates. With fine business acumen
but characteristic lack of straightforwardness, it
touts for orders and carries on the concerns, not
as the Salvarmy Printing and Engraving Co., but
under other names in nowise suggesting “ Army”
ownership, and most of the customers are quite
unaware of the real state of the case. In the
printing business the “ Army ” has two linotype
machines and a complete up-to-date plant, and, if
it is true —as stated—that every workman is paid
full union rates, that is certainly a violent depar-
ture from the “ Army’s” usual custom. But the
point here is not whether it sweats its employes,
or whether it is able to “ cut ” rates by reason of
its superior machinery and huge capital, but
whether an organisation that poses as philan-
thropic, and exists by cadging, and for whose
benefit Factories Acts and other protective legis-
lation are set aside, should be allowed to compete,
under an alias, against the very firms from whom
it solicits subscriptions, and the very people who
subsidise it.

At last the Lyne Cabinet appears to have made
the three appointments to the Sydney Harbor
Trust. After four months’ delay the names
announced are the three expected from the
first. It is known that there have been “ ructions ”
in the Cabinet over these appointments, as well
as over those to the Wharves Resumption Board.
Exception is taken in the city to the nomination
of J. F. Waller to the trust. Mr. Waller,
who seems to be in everything going, has been
valuator to the Savings Bank and land valuator
in N.S.W. Public Works Department ; he
has been connected with the railways, and
was quite recently appointed to the tem-
porary Commissionership of the Wharves-
JEtesumption business. It is generally supposed that
it was his appointment which has caused all the
trouble. Another version is that Premier Lyne,
halting between the dangers of defeat in the
Murray Federal electorate and the troublous times
before his State Ministry, was not averse to taking
on a steady job at £2OOO a year as Chairman of the
trust. But the Cabinet would not stand that, and
compromised by giving Waller a permanent ap-
pointment. Whether this and other stories are
true or not the Lyne crowd has only itself to
thank for their circulation. The whole concern
has been dragged through the mud, to the infinite
discredit of the State Ministry,
“ Suffolk” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin, —In acquitting Queen Victoria of “ all
pretensions to the semi-divinity” wherewith idiots encircled
her, you surely err on the side of mercy. For was H.M.
ever reported as abating one jot of the absurd Royal
ceremonies inherited from the days of regal semi divinity ?
That she enforced silence at her table, and sometimes
compelled her ladies to stand, may be false, but has often
been printed. And now, at her own request, it
would appear, she has been finally borne to
the grave on a gun-carriage ! What a satire up-
on her alleged mighty and beneficent influence for Peace !
Yet even this virtual tremendous insult to the Gospel has
not moved a single minister thereof to a word of protest.
And what of 12 months mourning ? How long did her
Court wear black for Darwin, or Huxley, or Carlyle, or
for any of the great men of her reign whose reflected
glories have turned her natural night to day* But white
hairs and sex and 40 years of widowhood disturb the just-
ness of our estimate ; and in any case all contemporary
criticism is largely “ out.” The dead Queen’s true charac-
ter will be appreciable about 1950. The verdict will then
endorse our appreciation of her domestic virtues, but
will be a total reversal of our views of her intellectual
value and national importance.
The N.S.W. political Labor people are still at
sixes and sevens over the extent to which a Labor
member (of anything) ought to be bound by “ the
corkis.” Lately the members in Parliament have
gone as the majority has decided on all matters
included in the “ platform,” on all others having
personal option. Which is a defensible plan : the
argument is that a Labor candidate is elected, if
lie is elected, first on the “ platform ” and then on
whatever ideas of his own he may submit; and
therefore he owes it to his constituents to vote on
everything outside the “ platform ” as his judg-
ment dictates. But the other idea (somebody is
daring enough to call it “advanced”) is that
every member ought to vote with the majority
whenever the majority chooses to crack the whip.
He might have told his constituents before they
were his constituents, as he had a perfect right
to do, that he believed in one thing, and it might
be that belief of his which won him enough votes to
give him the seat; yet if the. majority believes in
just the opposite thing, the idea is that he ought
to vote the latter way—and hang his constituents !
In practice, too, worse things happen. This is
the plan adopted by the Broken Hill Laboritesv

“ It is Never Too Late to Mend,” was re-
vived at the theatre of many revivals, Sydney
Criterion, the other night. This play belongs to
the rapidly increasing drama where the hero or
sub-hero alternates between the pale green light
of a prison cell and the bright red glow of a stage
Australia. On the boards Australia is always
represented as if recovering from a bush fire, and
if a crimson glare filters out beneath the curtain
before it rises, the audience knows that the land
which was lately told to “ Arise ” by several
hundred poets is about to be produced, “It is
Never,” etc., is supposed to be a blighting indict-
ment of the Englisn convict system, but as the
convicts go in and out of their cells at will, and
the cells seem to open right into the air, there is
very little in the indictment at present. Also,
when the parson thinks there is something in it
and writes to the authorities on the matter, these
personages answer within half-an-hour, having
evidently sifted facts, judged the case and
condemned the Governor all in this brief
space of time. The average Government
in real life would take longer to decide as
to the expenditure of 3d. When Charles
Reade’s drama is not blighting the management
of English prisons it is mostly a disorderly pro-
ceeding on an early Australian goldfield.
Alfred Dampier is up to his usual good
form as the convict who really is guilty of the
crime imputed to him ; and Miss Lily enacts the
simple village maid who is described as an Angel
oi Light, chiefly because she weeps over the
abbreviated hair of the convicts and tells them it
will grow again. The girl who thinks a convict’s
chief worry is the state of his coiffure is too inno-
cent even for the stage.

Further consideration of “Djin Djin ” at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) only confirms the good
opinion already expressed about that show —all of
it barring the Commonwealth Procession and the
Swearing-In. After all, the cinematographists
who present faked battles that took place in
circuses, and gory encounters in hired paddocks
between Britons at 2s. 6d. and Boers at 3s. (6d.
extra for whiskers) are wise in their generation.
The thing that might have happened often looks
good enough in a picture, but the thing that hap-
pened seldom does. It generally straggles too
slowly over too much space. Sydney’s Common-
wealth Day shivoo spread for about ten hours
over 12 miles of streets, a huge park, and many
public-houses, and it is only possible to show a
scrap of it here and there. To all intents and pur-
poses one might as well show one of Frederick the
Great’s boots as a sample of the Seven Years’
War. There are a few troops going along a few
little bits of road, and the Swearing-In consists
mostly of a man going into a building, and then a
man coming out of a building. These things are
not history ; they are only remnants. The fine
transformation-scene is a good enough place for
the casual spectator to end his evening’s “ Djin
Djin” at. It represents the “Birth of Australia,”
and Australia is a female with white arms and
cataracts of golden hair, born picturesquely amid
a dazzle of flowers and sunshine. Then you see
the real Birth of Australia, and it is mostly a
back view of a thin man going through a door to
sign a document. This sort of thing is anti-
climax. Also, it helps to blot out the recollection
of the great picture of the eruption of Fuji San.
Nobody wants dull, cold realism on top of the
blaze of Fuji San.

The “ Scarlet Feather” is understood to
have commenced its career at Sydney Royal
last night, but as this issue had already gone to
press before the curtain rose, if it did rise, details
must be held over till next week. The Bulletin
intends to say something especially nice about
Nellie Stewart in the ensuing issue, even if a
moderate straining of the truth is necessary in the
process. It is alleged that the truth is mighty
and will prevail, and this scribe would rather
strain a strong healthy thing like that in a good
cause than put needless pressure on some weak
substance that might be quite unable to stand it.

The great bush-fire of kilts that rushes
uphill at Sydney Lyceum and takes a place that
isn’t worth having is now in its third week, and
William Anderson’s soldiers will make a public
exhibition of their legs at the above address till
further notice. As a view of mountain scenery,
withcragsand peaks and precipices, the “Ladderof
Life ” is a matter that deserves to be held in grate-
ful remembrance, and if it is almost every possible
kind of a large glaring ass in a literary sense that
isn’t the scene-painter’s fault. Times are bad, and
when he got a commission to paint a series of
views to accompany four private lunatio asylums
with straw in their hair, it would have been folly
to refuse. So the artist did his best, which was
very good, and the author did his worst, which
was very bad ; and the combined result put on a
short red petticoat and rushed uphill with a gun
shouting at the top of its voice in Scotch, except
the part of it that blackened its face and put on a
longer skirt, and rushed downhill with a gun
shouting in Central Asia, or thereabouts. The
cause of it all doesn’t appear, but the men who
ran uphill yelling overcame the man who ran
yelling downhill, and much bare road-metal was
thereby added to the Empire of Edward the VII.

“Constance Clyde” gives “The Message”
a send-off:—
Went to “The Message from Mars,” the other night,
and was struck by the fact that Horace Parker, in his
unregenerate days, was an ideal husband from the Aus-
tralian point of view, and more of him as he was is
urgently required. What more does the average Aus-
tralienne desire than a husband who will sit at home and
read scientific magazines, when his intentions are to read
these magazines, and not go out on his own after his wife
or his flanc6 has departed? Other plays, mainly from
America, have circled round the gigantic Row the hero
got into by going out at night, and now he is getting
into exactly the same trouble by refusing to go. The
average woman would much prefer her husband in the
state of harmless egotism portrayed by Horace Parker
in Act I. than as he is later on, when he is liable to bring
home housefuls of stray outcasts at odd hours of the
morning, to the detriment of the furniture and
the family peace of mind, not to mention the
disappearance of drawing-room ornaments. This
view of the matter is aided by the fact that
the altruistic gentleman from Mars has a bilious com-
plexion, and looks as if the joys of otherdom were not all
they are cracked up to be. The women who took their
husbands to the Palace and dug their elbows into them
when man’s selfishness was dwelt upon, brought them to
the wrong moral. What we want in man is to see him
more selfish ; not less so. If every man would meanly
and egotistically look after his own morals and his own
tendency to selfishness, this world would be worth a hun-
dred small red planets up aloft. But everyone is so
unselfishly trying to cure the other fellow of his selfish-
ness that, through pure altruism, this world can be fairly
described by the bad word that the kind brown gentle-
man wouldn’t mention.
To which The Bulletin’s regular dramatic
authority says ditto, and adds, in an irrelevant
sort of way, that a new comedy, “ Tom, Dick and
Harry,” is to be produced at the Sydney Palace
on Saturday. “A Highland Legacy,” already
favorably reported upon, will accompany it.

George Rignold, with Miss Lilian Wheeler,
and the rest of his company, is going to play a six-
weeks’ season at the Theatre Royal, Perth, about
Easter, and then make for the goldfields’ towns.
This will be Rignold’s first visit to the West, and
the West ought to be glad to see him. In Sydney
and Melbourne long years of familiarity with the
big man has dimmed his lustre, and yet, if Sydney
and Melbourne were now seeing him for the first
time, they would probably decide that he was a
star of no ordinary magnitude. He is to be heard
at his best in a comparatively small theatre, where
his casual remarks to the limelight-man carry all
over the house. Rignold’s rehearsed parts are
generally good, but nis impassioned and unre-
hearsed addresses to the limelight-man, when the
glare is turned on the wrong individual, are
always filled with a rich humor that is irresistible.

The foreign campaign planned by the wily
Geach for Donald Macdonald has a touch of in-
spiration in it. They leave Melbourne on 12th
March, and make a pause at Colombo (where the
Boer prisoners go to) in order to test the possibility
of an Eastern tour anon. Then on to London
arriving towards the end of April, and beginning
at St. James’ Hall on about the night after the
opening of the Australian Federal Parliament.
On the night when there will be more “ Australia ”
talk in London than ever was talked before, the
“ Famous Australian ” will make his first appear-
ance. Mac. proposes to give two War lectures
ere reaching out for the dollars with his new
anecdotal, descriptive discourse on Australia, the
Land the Bushman comes from.

When J. C. Williamson left the other
week for America, London, and elsewhere, he
had some idea of engaging one or two big people
and three or four supporters for a Shakespearian
season, the bulk of the co. to be raised in Aus-
tralia. A round of “ legitimate ” plays should be
a success, if well acted and staged in all particu-
lars ; but the scheme, as stated, doesn’t look veiy
promising. A tadpole co.—big head and insig-
nificant tail —shows all its weakness when taking
on the works of the Divine William. Nor is even
one expensive star a necessary attraction. George
Miln, of yore, proved that an unknown leading
man, with moderately capable people around
him, can command attention up to a certain
point. There are —or there were until quite
recently—some well-seasoned Shakespeare com-
panies earning their yearly bread by their
general efficiency, not to say excellence, as British
provincial tourists. Every actor and actress in
such companies can take parts with ease and con-
fidence. For the manager who will import a well-
organised crowd of specialists in poetic drama a
large Australian public is always ready to provide
a boom.

“Puss in BooU,” which broke out at
Melb. Bijou on Saturday night, has the recom-
mendation of being an utterly different thing from
Musgrove’s gorgeous Xmas spectacle, late of the
Princess’s. The Rickards show got several words
of approval from this thoughtful publication when
it first took the floor in Sydney, and a few weeks
of hard work have improved the performance.
Given the Haytors, Harry Shine, long
Bob Bell, and other capable comedians in
any sort of pantomime, the result was found
to be fairly satisfactory. “ Puss in Boots ”
has ideas of its own. The earnest, intelligent
Devil (Harry Diver) who crops up at intervals
is the “Faust” devil adapted to new surround-
ings. When the glaring fiend stalks into a har-
vest festival, or something, with a view to annex-
ing a nice girl, the local King and his loyal villagers
throw little bits of comic song at the intruder,
until he wishes he was back. at . the place where
damned souls expiate their sins in an everlasting
stewpan. The earnest Devil is a humorous item,
also the Cat has a moment of comic pathos, and
the ordinarily frivolous accessories don t
stop too long at any one point.
Some of the songs by Billie Barlow,
Jennie Opie (who has succeeded that mas-
sive fairy, Miss Winston-Weir) and Edie Hay tor
are about the dullest matters in the show, and
these should be either cut out or cut shorter. Miss
Noni Rickards is a princess with at . least one
pretty song, which she warbles sweetly in a small,
clear voice. The De Wynne Bros., remarkably
clever acrobats, are new to Melbourne, likewise
the young man who rides a bike, but if the latter
artist were to catch the influenza and retire from
the bill he would not be missed. The cleverest of
trick-cyclists is a wearisome person nowadays.
** * *
Miss Bertha Bird who, for some time
past, has been going to take Europe by storm,
fave a farewell concert at the Masonic Hall, Mel-
ourne, one night last week. It was a rather sadden-
ing affair. Apart from the wan star of the pro-
gramme, the chief artist was Mrs. Frank Stephen,
who had really no pretensions to stand up and
warble before an audience which paid for admission
and didn’t come prepared to suffer on behalf of a
hospital or a Bible society. A strawberry-blonde
fentleman, in a white waistcoat, revealed an
r.T.B. tenor voice of the foggo-robusto
class, a growing lad played upon a fiddle,
and there were two or three other “turns”
including an amateur reciter, who earned a joyful
encore because he had something humorous to
speak and could say it in a plain, straightforward,
unassuming manner. Miss Bird’s snare of the
brief concert consisted mainly of top-note utter-
ances. She gave them the “Carnivalof Venice”
with, alas ! variations, and a footnote to the effect
that she was achieving Ain altissimo. She per-
formed the well-known trills of Lucia; likewise
the air from “Magic Flute” that is now-a-days
never sung in the original key—except by Miss
Bird. Also she made sounds purporting to
be an imitation of the violin part in a
string-quarteting of Mascagni’s Intermezzo. Two
large ladies and one fragile young man went
through a gruesome pretence of a humming accom-
paniment, whilst the prima-donna ejaculated her
foolish suggestions of violin effects. Altogether
they were too funny, standing in a row.
Miss Bird, if the truth must be told, has
little melody, feeling or colour in her
soprano to recommend it to the ear of the great
paying public. Had she a ten-mile range of notes
her voice would be no more attractive than at
present.
** # *
“The Great Ruby,” price £50,000, is
snavelled every night at about the same minute
from Brown’ssnopin Melb. Royal, where Detective-
Sergeant Bland Holt always arrives two minutes
after the gang have flown. Bland is recovering
his youthful vigor of late, but he continues to
wear a large, inhuman nose and a lofty brow, prob-
ably in the hope of taking the gang by surprise.
His nose, however, proves to be valueless as a means
of deception. It is likewise very unsuitable
for blowing purposes. The Gang are aware of his
identity the instant he strolls into their midst.
The recognition is mutual. Bland looks at the
men he has unexpectedly hunted down, and he
says, in the meditative tone of a bookmaker talk-
ing to himself, “4 to 1! ” Whereupon Albert
Norman comes along and haunts him with an
awful grin, and the detective-sergeant confesses
that the Gang have got him at their mercy.
But although Bland Holt is a disgrace to
the force, like most other detectives, he
seems a hero to Miss Harrie Ireland. Beau-
teous Harrie is a romantic damsel who keeps
the books at a fashionable pub.—the only artless
hotel-girl on record —and she gives herself to the
Gang-hunter for the sake of his air of mystery,
without raising the slightest protest against his
nose. Miss Frances Ross and Walter Baker and
the balloon are doing good work as before. Mrs.
Bland Holt has improved somewhat, but Brown
seems unable to assume the character of a jewel-
ler. The average jeweller is a cross between a
chemist and an ironmonger, whereas Brown is
Brown, and considerably Charley at that.
