“Florodora,” at Her Majesty’s, is rejoicing
in new additions of Grace Palotta’s frocks, and a
change of turns by the Prapses, otherwise Lauri
and Carrie Moore. Their most riotous turn, which
involves the startling apparition of a third Praps,
is still proceeding, but the harmonium catastrophe
and the trumpet that divulged a yellow sausage
have been replaced by selections from grand
opera.

The Princess’s has made a sudden an-
nouncement of its intention to resume business on
Saturday (23rd) instead of waiting for the opera
co.’s return. “Barabbas” is to be produced by
the Woods-Williamson forces, and the manage-
ment threatens the story of Barabbas (who was a
robber the last time The Bulletin heard of him)
will be even more splendid than “ Sign of the
Cross.” If it is as beautifully blue as the sermon
aforesaid the Woods-Williamson drama ought to
get along all right.

The deep-sea voice of Snazelle is pitching
yarns and trolling ballads twice a day, as usual,
a t the Athenaeum Hall. “Our Navy’ has been
on the sheet for several weeks, and Melbourne
speaks favorably of that interesting ’graph. Also
the picturesque appearance of the lecturer when
he does the Block in white ducks and nautical
headgear, is a matter for admiring speculation.
Nobody seems to know for certain whether the
late Francis Drake has made an admiral or a
commodore of Snazelle, but in either case he looks
the right man for the billet.

" Here it is again ! From Cassell’s, on Melba :
“ There are colonials who ignore their own colony. But
Madame Melba, wise woman, is proud of hers. It is her
wish, as soon as circumstances permit, to pay a visit to
the country, and to sing, first of all, at Melbourne, from
which town, by the way, she took her name of Melba.
The very finest artists available she will engage to accom-
pany her on her tour. ‘ Australia must have only the
best,’ she declares.” This concert-party, it is understood,
has booked its passage for Ist April, 1987.
“Bessie Wentworth, England’s ‘coon’ star” (writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent) “ died a few days
after Geo. Beauchamp, whom you recently heard. You
knew G. 8., so there is nothing that need be said, but
B.W. was a fine, big, curly-headed tomboy worthy of the
best hearts of her profession. She practically introduced
the 1 coon ’ song—for which Heaven forgive her—to Eng-
lish popularity, and was a serious, hard-working young
woman. She was to have principal-* boyed ’in panto,
this Xmas, but pneumonia ordered it otherwise. By
heart and by physique she was a genuine generous
woman. Alma Obrey took B.’s place on Boxing night at
the ‘ Broadway’ New Cross.”
A Sydney paper declares that “Harry Roberts is mak-
ing great progress in America, and is now fulfilling a
first-class engagement.” These vague allegations suggest
that the young man will soon return to Australia.
Roberts is a bright actor who ought to make headway
anywhere, but if he has done anything important in
America, why not furnish some details ?
Miss Emmie Owen, the jolly, tuneful little woman
whom Musgrove imported for “The Scarlet Feather”
and “ Cinderella,” has been acting under difficulties all
the time. Two serious outbreaks of hemorrhage have
failed to frighten her seemingly ; Emmie grows more fes-
tive as her natty figure diminishes, but she can’t dance
away from the fact that she ought to consider herself
unable to appear for the next two months.
There’s one song in “ Cinderella’’—the ballroom song—
which “ gives away ” the cruel truth re Nellie Stewart’s
voice. If she could sit in front of the house for once and
hear herself sing it Nellie would either abandon the song
altogether or have the music altered. All the ravages of
time are recorded in Nellie’s treatment of that monoton-
ous ditty.
“ Haresfoot ” : A new idea bursts upon me. George
Darrell is Gov. Madden’s model.
American actress Nance O’Neil is said to be a sister to
Frank Lawton, the clever ’Murkan whistler who was here
with the Chinatown crowd. But the yarn doesn’t look
over-probable. Lawton was mostly skin and bone.
Chas. Arnold takes “ Jones ” to Perth at the end of
May. He plays four weeks there and four weeks on the
goldfields.
“ S.”: What a doleful thing is the Commonwealth proces-
sion as a cinemat. exhibition ! Two or three disjointed slabs
out of Sydney’s wonderful drunk and walk-round and
breakdown have been squirted on canvas in Melbourne,
with nothing to relieve the glare of spasmodic swaddies
but the vague fluttering of Gov.-Gen. Hopetoun across a
casual empty space, wearing the bright smile of a man
who is going out to his own hanging. If these are the
official records that are to be handed down to posterity,
I am quite sure they will make posterity sorry for us.
Somebody suggests that the story of Queen Victoria
would make a good subject for a drama. Well, the story
of any monarch is good for spectacular treatment, but
something more than spectacle is needed. The success
of the late Queen’s reign lay in the fact that she was
never a heroine, or an important figure in any situations
of particular dramatic interest. And she is too modern
a subject to be effectively treated by the romancer. The
public will not accept a historical play which doesn’t pre-
sent a surface appearance of truth.
Amidst all the hubbub of the Queen’s death one half-
inch cable sufficed to tell that the immortal Verdi had
passed away.

much.”
Snazelle’s red-covered book of reminiscences, yarns and
reprints is a very readable thing. The title, “Snazelle-
parilla,” smacks of the penny comic paper, but the easy,
rambling style of the self-advertiser disarms criticism.
It starts by telling how a stranger tapped Snazzy on the
shoulder and invited him to go halves in a big bottle of
fizz—“‘Ha! ha! Devilish funny! Haw!’ He was of
the heavy order of swell and he laughed that way. He
had listened while I related a curious experience to two
or three friends .... ‘No offence, old chappie,’he went
on. ‘Of course you don’t know we, but I must stand you
a drink, if you will allow me, for you are the first Johnnie
I have met in the Strand in 30 years who has told a new
story.’” Snazelle’s remarks on Johannesburg (page
165) are a warning to would-be contingenters—
“We here struck our first illness on our tour, all
the five of our party being down with fever.
I managed to get up after four days in bed
and, to cheer myself, walked to the cemetery to see the
graves of six actor friends who had died in the town. I
met many of our good Australian mechanic friends who
had sailed with us to Africa, each one of them longing to
get back. I invited them into our entertainment on the
first night when it was possible to give it, and showed
them some of the perpetual green forest scenery of their
native land, taking care to remind them that they had
often grumbled at its monotony, and how much better it
was than the monotony of the barren veldt—a country in
which you travel for hundreds of miles without seeing a
single tree. Before I had done with them I saw some of
these Australians wiping away the furtive tear.”

Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in re; odelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “Luhrig” Vanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, Burning, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work. *

“Zip”: Replying to “Gamma” re Mt. Mor-
gan G.M. Co.’s water-supply. He is quite right regarding
the co.’s effort to secure water, but then what about the
management (which has sailed close to the wind re-
peatedly in re water-storage) ? It does not look any
further on than a couple of seasons ago, while being con-
stantly threatened with famine. The co. expended some
£20,000 on the erection of a 222-million-gallon dam
about 16 months ago, besides £lB,OOO on the series of
smaller dams which were built in early days. Add to this
£5300 frittered away in the trial surveys for the pro-
pounded schemes at Piebald Creek (nine miles away) and
Crocodile (13 miles away), and top the lot with the £l4OO
it was forced to expend in Dec. in emptying all the
creeks and waterholes within three miles, and you
have the respectable total of £44,700, which sum
would, it is admitted by the co., have paid for a pipe-
line connection with the river-supply at Rockhampton,
only 24 miles away. The present dam system is merely
a series of steps, and the great dam must be filled before
the second receives any, and there are seven dams.
Three and a half inches rain have fallen during the pre-
sent “wet season” so far, and the only supply derived
from this was caught and is conserved by the centre dam,
which drains a small watershed of its own. Only a
few months’ supply can be reckoned on at the outside if
no more rain falls, then—another close down. This is
the middle of Feb., too. And, re “Gamriia’s” remarks
about 873 out of 1600 being discharged, it might be
pointed out that the class who were discharged were the
workers, who would feel the pinch a great deal more than
the crowd of overseers and bosses who are retained. It
might be suggested also that the “ strenuous efforts ”
made to keep things going were put forth quite as much
on behalf of the absentee shareholders as the workers of
the Mount. The stoppage of the regular monthly divi-
dend of 7d. might lead to investigation.

The Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
(Melbourne), shows a return for the half-year ended 31st
December, 1900, which is rather better than might be
reasonably expected. Its progress in recent times has
been as follows :
Loss. Total deficit.
December, 1894 „ £5,632 .. £6,921
June, 1895.. .. 7,481 .. 14,402
December, 1895 „ 2,531 ... 16,933
Profit.
June, 1896.. .. 144 .. 16,789
(Deficit provided for out of forfeited capital.)
Total reserves.
December, 1896 .. 978 .. £978
June, 1897.. ... 912 ... 1,890
December, 1897 .. 1,051 .. 2,941
June, 1898.. ... 1,207 „ 4,148
December. 1898 ... 1,400 „ 5,548
June, 1899.. ... 1,257 ... 6,805
December, 1899 ... 1,575 .. 8,380
June, 1900.. ... 1,521 .. 9,901
December, 1900 .. 1,640 .. 11,541
Profits are equal to 7s. 2d. per cent, per annum on the
capital (£902,479) and reserves, and the profits are the best
which the Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank has
realised for a long time. The improvement isn’t large,
but it is accomplished under especially difficult circum-
stances. According to the terms of the reconstruction,
70 per cent, of the deposits were renewed till 1910 at 31
per cent., the other 30 per cent, bearing no interest and
being repayable by three instalments on Ist January,
1896, 1899, and 1901. The result was that till the end of
1895 the bank got the use of its reconstructed deposits at
an all-round rate of £2 9s. per cent. From Ist January,
1896, to Ist January, 1899, they cost all round £2 14s. 6d.
per cent. Since then they cost £3 Is. 3d. per cent., and
hereafter they will cost £3 10s. per cent, and remain at
that. But during the past half-year the bank has been
keeping the money ready to pay the third instalment,
and through loss of interest on the amount it has prac-
tically been paying £3 10s. per cent. The fact that the
profits have steadily increased, notwithstanding the in-
crease in the rate of interest payable on deposits, looks
as if the A.D. and M. Bank was strong enough to pull
through, despite the exceedingly humble profits.
The board stated in 1895 that out of £1,560,000
(paper value) of assets £730,000 (paper value) were yield-
ing no return whatever. Presumably a lot of this is
vacant land, and presumably, also, a great deal of it is
still vacant and returning no interest. The management
now talks of making an effort to realise a lot of these pro-
perties. Probably this means that £700,000 or so (alleged
value) of assets that yield nothing may materialise into
perhaps £200,000 or £300,000 of actual cash, which will
yield decent interest. If so, there will necessarily be a
heavy writing-down of capital, but at the same time there
should be a great increase of earnings, and dividends on
a reduced capital should again become possible. It ought
also to be possible to make good a part of the loss by re-
purchasing the old deposits at a discount. The A.D. and
M. Bank has £914,020 of shareholders’ funds, while the
whole liability to depositors is now only about £433,000.
With such a very wide margin for depreciation a good
deal of the capital should survive the wreck, however bad
it may be.

Balmain New Ferry Co. (Sydney):
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
Dec., 1898 ..£1,371 10 per cent. = £648 £2,201
June, 1899 .. 1,355 10 „ = 648 2,908
Dec., 1899 .. 1,687 10 „ = 648 3,947
June, 1900 .. 773 10 „ = 648 4,072
Dec., 1900 .. 975 10 „ = 648 4,399
The reserves consist of dividend reserve, £3OO ; insurance
reserve, £4OO ; and undivided profit, £3699. Why the co.
does not create a strong general reserve fund, instead of
keeping this large sum lying loose at credit of profit and
loss, it is hard to understand. It would make the
accounts look better at all events. Also, it seems to keep
its two little reserve funds separately, the £3OO in N.S.W.
Government stock and the £4OO in the Post Office Savings
Bank, which latter, considering the very low rate of in-
terest allowed, appears a most unprofitable locality. All
the same, the Balmain New Ferry' Co. is a solid, profitable,
and well-managed concern, and its balance-sheets are
perfectly clear and coherent now-a-days. In earlier times
some of them were about as intelligible as an insur-
rection of Chinese gridirons.

The Equitable Life Assurance Society of the
U.S. had an increase during 1900 of £9,500,000 in the
amount of life insurance in force. The policies in force
at 31st Dec., 1900, represented the stupendous total of
£230,000,000. Also, at same date, the assets were over
£62,500,000 (an increase of £4,125,000 during 1900); and
the surplus of assets over liabilities was £13,125,000 (an
increase of over £400,000 during 1900).

The half-yearly meeting of the Commercial
Bank of Australia again produced no infuriated rush of
information about the Special Assets Trust—that part of
the bank where over £3,300,000 of assets and liabilities are
put away without any information whatever being afforded
as to how they are getting on. So The Bulletin again
asks these few questions :
(1) What are the receipts and expenditure of the Trust?
(2) Does the revenue from the Trust’s properties cover
working expenses and interests on the Trust’s deposits ?
(3) If it doesn’t, does the £60,000 transferred yearly
from the earnings of the bank cover the deficit, or more
than cover it ?
(4) If it isn’t sufficient to cover the deficit (supposing
there to be a deficit), is the balance covered by that por-
tion of the earnings of the Commercial Bank which re-
whole concern (Bank . g pled e §
to°pa| on%Xence d shares, or is that dividend being
pa /M y o?ii d t°he b Ass a e S ts e 'Trust paying its way, and is the
£6O 000 a year transferred from the bank being simply
£7 500 000 of assets yielded a certain profit as against
their corresponding liabilities, and no information is
given as to Whether the other £3,385,000 of assets or
alleged assets yielded a profit or made a loss as compaied
with the liabilities set against them i . , . .
And (7) do the directors of the Commercial Bank of
Australia regard this way of doing business as desirable,
considering the possibilities it opens up / For instance .
Last half-year the A.J.S. Bank made £2547 profit after
setting the losses on its bad assets against the profits on
its good ones. Supposing it put £4,000,000 of its worst
assets and a corresponding amount of its deposß liabilities
into an Assets Trust, and said nothing at all about how
the Trust met, or didn’t meet, the interest on these de-
posits, doesn’t it seem just possible that the A.J.S. Bank
could show on the other assets a half-yearly profit of
£30,000 or so, and yet be no better than it is now l That
seems to be how the Assets Trust system might pan out
in the A.J.S. case. And the shareholders of the Commer-
cial Bank of Australia have every right to know how the
system is panning-out in their case.

The new deficit is mounting up almost as gaily as the old
one did, for though the writing-off of £250,000 capital
squared the accounts on paper, it neither diminished the
outgoings nor increased the income, and it improved the
prospects not one whit. M'Cracken’s Brewery owes
£500,000 on debentures at 4J per cent,, and two years’
vigorous negotiation has failed to secure any reduction in
interest. It also owes £51,698 bank overdraft and £112,891
on mortgages, and the interest on these huge liabilities
weighs it down. Paid-up capital was originally £500,000
—written down in 1896 to £250,000. Then the £500,000
unpaid capital was called up, but only £139,385 has
materialised. A further £38,070 is cancelled, and faint
hopes are entertained of the remaining£322,s4s. If enough
capital could be got in to pay off bank overdraft, mort-
gages and some of the debentures, the saving in interest
would enable profits to be made, and out of them the in-
stitution might in time write off all losses, and become a
good dividend-paying affair again; but there appears
little hope of that. Or there might be some hope if the
debenture-holders would accept 3 per cent., or if some
philanthropist would lend enough money at 3 or 3£ per
cent, to pay off debentures, mortgages and overdrafts.
Failing these things, nothing but a considerable increase
in drunkenness appears likely to save M'Cracken’s City
Brewery. It is a painful case of a splendid business
ruined by trading on little capital and a great deal of
money borrowed at high interest.

Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power Co.
finished its 12th year on 31st December, 1900. In pre-
vious references to this co. The Bulletin has drawn
attention, in an unfriendly spirit, to a paper or goodwill
item of £15,000, described' as “ Parliamentary Rights and
Concessions,” which the co. appears to make no attempt
whatever to write off. When this item is set against the
apparent reserves, as not being a visible or concrete
asset, the Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power Co.’s
reserves are nil ; in fact, it has, and always had, a con-
siderable deficit. The last six years’ results are as under :

By retaining among its assets the goodwill item of
£15,000 the Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power Co.
is able to show reserves to the nominal amount of
£13,807, but even of this sum £7750 is set aside to
replace the plant when it wears out, and £2OOO for depre-
ciation of leaseholds. Assuming that these sums are all
needed to cover deterioration up to date, then the only
real reserve which the co. even professes to have is the
£4057 undivided profit, and if the paper item of goodwill
is left out of the assets the deficit in real assets as com-
pared with liabilities to shareholders and others is
£10,943. Under these circumstances the 10 per cent, divi-
dend looks a good deal less justifiable than it might be.

The Deniliquin and Moama Railway Co. shows
a profit of £4603 for the half-year to 31st Dec., 1900, and
a dividend at the rate of 4 per cent, per annum absorbs
£2OOO. The other £2603, added to previous balance of
£20,744, leaves £23,347 undivided profit to be carried
forward. In addition, there is a reserve described as
“ Reserve and Renewal account ” of £15,917, making total
reserves 39,264, which is reasonably solid in proportion to
the paid-up capital of only £lOO,OOO. A curious circum-
stance is that the “ Reserve and Renewal account ” ap-
pears to have increased during the half-year by £1583
without the least visible indication in the balance-sheet
as to where the £1583 came from. Said reserve is rather
a bewildering thing under these circumstances.

A N.S.W. Parliamentary return, showing 20
years’ imports or exports of wheat for the whole Com-
monwealth reveals the curious fact that the biggest
wheat export year was as far back as 1884. Since 1880
there have been four years (1886, 1889,1896, and 1897)
when the Commonwealth didn’t grow enough wheat for
its own consumption, the biggest shortage being in 1896.
The other 15 years it had a surplus for export ranging
from 17,130,843 bushels in 1884, the best exporting year,
down to 1,341,596 bushels in 1898, the worst exporting
year. Only in seven years of the 20 has the export ex-
ceeded ten million bushels

The Commonwealth has been of much less importance as
a wheat-exporting country in the last ten years than in
the previous ten. From 1880 to 1889 it had a surplus of
70,989,378 bushels. From 1890 to 1899 the surplus was
only 66,064,426 bushels.

Mr. J. T. Walker, of the B. N.S.W., publishes
in United Australia a suggestion, which looks original
and is certainly valuable, for the prevention of banking
crises. His idea may be summarised thus : The banks of
the Commonwealth have about £17,500,000 of paid-up
capital and £15,000,000 uncalled capital. If by bad
management or unforseeable disaster the paid-up capital
is to any great extent lost, the unpaid capital of
£15,000,000 is relied upon to fill the gap, and secure the
banks’ creditors from loss. But the only time when the un-
paid capital is really wanted on a large scale is when
there is a general collapse and panic, and at such a time
even wealthy shareholders can’t pay. Their cash is
locked up in suspended banks, their securities and pro-
perties are unsaleable; and the time when un-
called capital is very much required indeed is the time
when, by the very nature of things, it can’t be collected.
Mr. Walker’s proposition is that the uncalled capital
should be paid in good times, by setting aside a small
percentage of the profits every year to accumulate at com-
pound interest in the hands of trustees (presumably the
State would be the trustee). When it had all accumu-
lated the situation would be this: The shareholders in a
bank with a reserve liability of £1,000,000, instead of
being responsible for £1,000,000, would not be responsible
for anything. The bank, instead of calling on its share-
holders in a time of disaster for £1,000,000, and getting
perhaps £500,000, and that by slow instalments, could
call on its trustees and get the whole sum. And if
it never needed to call for the said £1,000,000 it would
have £30,000 to £40,000 a year extra revenue, for as soon
as the million had fully accumulated the annual income
from it would be paid over to the bank. The idea, in
short, is to provide for the future in good times instead of
in bad ones, and it seems about as practical a way of pre-
venting bank crises as could be devised,

The smelting works of that big N.S.W. steel
rails concern are to be located on Parramatta river—
much to the delight of a certain bank, which is over-
joyed at the sale in London of the site. Also, there is a
provision, which has escaped the public eye, in the Har-
bour Resumptions Act enabling the Harbour Trust Com-
missioners to find a deep-water frontage for the rails co.,
which provision will no doubt be brought into play in-
stantly, if not sooner. With these facts, and a little other
information, a variety of things become more or less
plain and clear. Apropos, the denizens of Ryde,
Hunter’s Hill, and thereabouts are already beVailing the
sad fate of the beautiful gardens and orchards in that
now delightful residential area, so soon to be “ knocked
endways’’ by the grime of a sordid smelting works.
Broken Hill has got over its water-famine
scare ; on the day after the B.H. Water Co.’s half-yearly
meeting 2in. fell in a few hours. There really was
danger of a water-famine, tho’ not quite so big a danger
as was represented. One of the local municipal Labor
party’s chief planks is a public-owned water supply, and
the worse of the scare was the result of electioneering
talk. All the same, the water got so near the bottom
that it was unfit to drink, and it would probably not
have lasted the mines for their purposes more than 10
weeks, and it isn’t safe to get so close to the end in
Oentralia, where often eight or 10 months pass without
a decent shower. The worst thing actually disclosed
during the scare was the silting-up of the reservoir.
Thus tho’ the gauge showed about 12ft., no greater depth
of water than sft. 9in. could be found, and that
was over only a very small area. Close up to the weir
as little as 3ft. 9in. was indicated. This means that
there has been 7ft. or Bft. of silting in places, and all this
inside eight or nine years. The reservoir consists of a
dam thrown across the sandy bed of a creek (flowing only
after the very occasional heavy rains of those parts)
just below its confluence with two or three small creeks.
The water is dammed back into these creek-beds, and
after filling them spreads out over the comparatively flat
country on either side. The silting-up of the creek beds
is thus a very serious matter, because it forces the water
out on to the shallows, exposing a great surface to eva-
poration ; and out there the evaporation averages be-
tween Bin. and Ift. per month. As a source of supply,
then, the reservoir is losing value ; while the enormously-
increased demand for water at B. Hill for ore-dressing,
and the complete dependence of the industry and the
place on that ore-dressing nowadays, make it necessary
to have not a poorer but a better supply than has hitherto
been available.
• * * *
One proposal, now before N.S.W. Public Works
Committee, is to spend £200,000 on new works at Umber-
umberka Creek ; but as the State gets the present co’s
works (which have cost over £200,000) in 17 years with-
out paying for them, the proposal needs thinking over.
It would be all right if the Umberumberka were a big
scheme ; but it isn’t —isn’t so big as the present co’s.
Another proposal is to spend about £25,000 in damming
another creek (the Yanco) and digging a channel and
tunnel to the present reservoir. This would save a second
pumping station (Broken Hill is higher than any possible
source of supply), a second line of mains, and a second
retaining embankment—a saving' of, say, £150,000. The
question is—On what terms will the co. do this work ?
The drawback to a second comparatively small
reservoir, apart from cost, is that two reservoirs
mean two surfaces exposed to evaporation ; and the co.’s
experience is that about eight times more water goes
through evaporation than through the pipes. The
Directors, according to a hint dropped from the chair
at the half-yearly meeting, would be willing to extend
the watershed. Presumably it would then want its term
(originally 28 years) extended ; and then (if the people
will put up with exclusive private ownership at all) there
would have to be a substantial reduction in price and an
undertaking to keep the water pure and the reservoir
clear of silt.

Australia is finding good customers in the East
for frozen concentrated milk. A little while ago small
quantities were shipped as experiments, and were so suc-
cessful that at present orders cannot be met. One vessel
recently left Melbourne with about 10,000 one-gallon tins,
equal to about 35,000 gallons of ordinary milk.

An unusually big smash has been added to
the list of N.S.W. railway wrecks. On Friday
evening at 5.55 p.m. a through train left Sydney
for Hurstville with about 500 passengers on board.
A Bulletin man was in the last car with his wife,
and just after passing Sydenham the lady re-
marked that they seemed to be travelling at a
higher speed than usual. Then there was a sudden
jolting—a rocking to and fro, a grinding sound of
brakes hard on, and a suppressed general shriek
as the passengers were thrown to one end of the
car—then a dead stop. Scrambling to their feet
the startled and shaken passengers looked out of
the window. Down below them was an engine
that seemed to be a mass of flame shrouded in
clouds of steam—and a heap of wreckage that had
a grotesque resemblance to a railway carriage.
The people began streaming from everywhere,
and, with the exception of the first-class pas-
sengers, who mostly appeared to be a slow lot,
and' afraid to soil "their clothes, did their best
to succour the wounded and extricate the dead.
With a few notable exceptions the first-class gen-
tlemen confined their efforts to passing wet hand-
kerchiefs and similar light assistance. It was the
second-class passengers and the people of the
neighborhood who gave first help. There
was a most discreditable delay in the arrival
of proper surgical aid, which did not
come till nearly three-quarters of an hour.
At a station like Redfern a hospital
train with a staff thoroughly expert in “First
Aid ” should be available in three minutes, and
the seene of the disaster could have been reached
in 12 minutes. Out at the wreck people ran to and
fro hysterically, and got axes and saws, and began
to cut through beams to get at the people pinned
down beneath. In one case half-a-dozen people
were sawing and chopping as gently as possible at
a framework to get to the injured when someone
si o jted, “ There’s enough here to lift three times
the weight—up with it! ” A hundred hands re-
sponded as soon as a leader wms found, and the
frame was lifted right over. One man, a fireman,
showed heroic promptitude by stepping on to the
engine and drawing the fire, thereby preventing a
disastrous explosion.
* * * *
The whole scene bristled with harrowing
incidents. A girl standing stupefied saw a
stretcher going by ; the hand hung down at the
side, and she recognised the sleeve. “It’s my
brother—let me speak to him—let me see his
face ! ” she cried, springing forward. She won-
dered why men held her back with brutal force,
a id dragged her away, and shouted to the driver
o the cart into which the covered object had been
lifted, to drive away for God’s sake. They knew
it was her brother, and that the form beneath the
s .ok was headless.
Interviewed by the daily Press, the N.S.W.
Railway Commissioners declare the great Syden-
ham disaster to be inexplicable. Possibly some-
vagary of the continuous brake was responsible
for the thing. According to the Railway Secre-
tary, there were eight ears on the train, each
weighing 19 tons—a total carriage-weight of 152
tons with about 400 passengers, who at lOst. each
would give another 25 tons, or a total of 177 tons —
be.imd an engine weighing 39 tons 3 cwt. with
coal and water. In the old days, when brakes
were applied on the engine and the guard’s-van
only, railway-people had always to consider the
relative weights of engine and load. An engine
might be strong enough to draw a load, but not
strong enough to stop it. But the invention of
the continuous-brake did away with that. With
the continuous-brake the man on the engine
applied the same brake-power to every vehicle
on the train simultaneously—if the brake
worked properly. The train in question was
travelling at a speed which appears to have
struck regular travellers on the line as unusual
and excessive, and was nearing its first stop-
ping place. When the continuous brake was
applied it may have failed to act on the
carriages simultaneously with the engine, and the
result would be that the engine’s speed was
checked while that of the 177 tons behind was not,
in which ease the load behind simply knocked the
engine off the line. The end of the engine next
to the carriages would be kicked up, and when it
fell the flanges failed to fall between the rails.
The fact that the brakes acted for all they were
worth immediately afterwards does not affect the
case. The failure to do so for a fraction of a
second was enough to start the catastrophe.

Victoria’s fifth S’African contingent of
1000, who left last Friday, were evidently out
after glory and coin. Most of them knew nothing
of soldiering and not too much of shooting, and
they took very unkindly to drill and camp life.
Also they were so keen on the farewell and pre-
sentation business, as to raise the ire of Col. Otter,
who told them they were “ like a lot of Sunday-
school children, appealing in shoals to go some-
where or other to social evenings in their honor.
It is not soldiering at all. It’s mere nonsense.”
And a day or two before they sailed, and after
they had signed, they found the pay cheque wasn’t
likely to be nearly as large as they reckoned. The
Imperial Govt, only paysfromdate of embarkation,
while the Victorian ditto allowed them to believe
their wages would start from date of engagement ;
so, unless that overburdened State foots the bill,
they will be working off a dead horse for the next
three or four weeks.

As for the “Dolly Vardens,” as the 250 Aus-
tralian Scots for Tullibardine’s regiment were
neatly nicknamed, they were a really choice
assortment of the casual, irresponsible Australian
swashbuckler. They were nominally under con-
trol of Col. Creagh, who was more or less respon-
sible to Col. Otter and to a certain extent under
the orders of the Orient’s skipper, but they signed
no agreement or contract; they just went over as
ordinary civilians on the chance that the
Marquis of Dolly Varden, or the M‘Ginty, or
somebody, wanted them, and would pay them
decently and put them in the way of getting
plenty of glory and loot.
* » » *
And there was a very abbreviated farewell-
march for Victoria’s latest lot of S’African farm-
burners. They merely stepped into the city and
out again, then on to Port Melbourne. The
authorities had fixed up barricades (with those
white post-and-rail affairs that bard Fitzgibbon
uses to prevent the casual drunk falling down his
sewer-holes;, but they weren’t needed. Those
who are usually about town in the morning lazily
stepped across to take stock of the contingent,
but there was no huge crowd ; also, there were
few cheers, no groaning verandahs, and few flags
visible, and the only motto that writer saw was an
ungrammatical insult, outside a Bourke-street
pul).: “Cod leads Australia’s sons to victory”
(sic). Also, the girls didn’t rush to embrace their
departing “ boys,” though certain gentlemen with
warrants (vide police-court reports) were very
anxious to discover some dozen or so and take
them up to old Dr. Dloyd, at N. Melb., there to
give security for the payment of 7s. 6d. a-week a-
piece.
* * * *
In Maoriland the Imperial troopers were
dragged round with a vengeance. The swaddies
only had about eight days to spend in the place,
and they were hauled from the Bluff to Auckland
in that time. Besides putting in a day at each of
the four big towns, they had to spend an hour ortwo
at every little accessible township and be stared at.
At Christchurch, when it was said that the men
would probably be wearing kharki, several of the
committee stated that nothing would be sub-
scribed for their entertainment unless it was
guaranteed that they would be seen in full dress.
Evidently the public wanted uniforms, not men.
The Government’s idea was to billet the men
among the public. At Wellington, when they
advertised for billeting offers for 900 men they got
84 replies, each coupled with the proviso that the
offer did not apply to privates. Yah !

more brains to make a good baker than a good
member of Parliament.” The baker class has had
a bitter grudge against M’s.P. since the latter
first began to pass Adulteration Acts.
Gundagai, N.S.W. has
just seen the end of a curi-
ous story. Somefew months
ago a family was discovered
in Wollongong quite desti-
tute. It consisted of a
father, delicate mother and
five children, one an in-
fant. The father informed
the local police that they
had all come from Coota-
mundra on foot. This
story was not taken serious-
ly, but arrangements were
made to send the children
to a Sydney charity. Be
fore their tickets of admis-
sion arrived the whole lot
had disappeared. Later
on, advices were received
from near Bathurst that
these people, having walked
from Hurstville, were found
in a distressed condition.
• The father was “ looking for
work,” so he said, but his children
were starving. But again they dis-
appeared ere assistance could reach
them, and made. off West. They
were traced through Molong,
Orange, Forbes, Parkes, Eugowra,
Canowindra, &c., right down to
Crookwell near Goulburn. Here
tlie father, whose story was that
the family had trudged practi-
cally the whole distance from
Wollongong, obtained em-
ployment and announced
his intention of settling
down. But they were
soon off again, and
walked to Gundagai,
where thepolice arrested
them as vagrants. The
children have been hand-
ed over to the State Re-
lief Department, and the
wants of the mother at-
tended to. Nothing previ-
ous to their original de-
covery at Cootamundra can
be obtained from the father,
who must have, during the
last 12 months, journeyed with-
out money, begging his way, with a sick
wife and five small children, nearly 1000 miles.

One curious by-product of the S’African
troubles has been the drawing of a supposed dis-
tinction between typhoid and enteric fevers.
They are, of course, absolutely identical, but the
public loves its mystery, and must have the more
dreadful sounding “ enteric ” as an appendage to
the pomp of war, scorning the humble typhoid
which is always with us. “Enteric” sounds
better and has a more lethal ring about it, but it
is only the latest popular label for the selfsame
disease which in early Australian days carried
off many a gold-seeker under the name of
“gastric fever,” “colonial fever,” “Bendigo
fever,” “scrub fever,” and a dozen similar aliases.
Typhoid happens to have been the best accepted
of its names up to the present, and no disease is
popularly credited with the possession of more
than one. The distinction made, even by edu-
cated people, between a “ fractured ” and a
“broken” bone is an illustration of the same
peculiarity, a “break” being looked upon as
more serious than a “fracture,” although they are
synonyms. Incidentally, the returned invalids
will probably be responsible for a rise in the
typhoid—or enteric—returns, since the bacilli
continue to be discharged from the intestinal
tract in active form for a considerable period
after convalescence is well established. Since
typhoid is a “place” disease it is more than
probable that a goodly number of new foci
will be established throughout Australia
wherever returned invalids recovering from
enteric—or typhoid—are sent home to recuperate,
and this will form another toll claimed for the
glory of England. With plague at the Cape,
typhoid fever arriving in regular consignments for
distribution, and the curse of militarism stopping
the progress of her agricultural and pastoral inte-
rests, Australia can well afford to look upon her-
self as having “done something for the Empire.”
* * * *
The dust-storm that struck Melbourne the
other Thursday was one of the thickest in the
history of Yarropolis, and the wind that pushed
it along was responsible for some queer happen-
ings. One woman was quite lost in the dust in
Port Melbourne and blown about half-a-mile off
her course at a great bat, but not nearly as fast as
her perambulator containing a baby and a load of
vegetables. When the dust passed and the mother
discovered herself, perambulator and baby were
nowhere in sight, and a distracted female, heaped
up with dust, went wailing through the suburb,
crying in anguish for her last-born. The peram-
bulator had been rushed along City Road in a bee
line, and overturned in a far-off gutter. Similarly
the storm bolted with a frantic Dago in his ice-
cream barrow at Albert Park. The most horrify-
ing comment the papers were able to make was:
“ What if the Duke had been here ? ” That would
indeed have been a catastrophe ; but let us give
Providence credit for knowing better.
• * * *
Is there any reason why a tar-mixture
should not (as in Melbourne) be spread over the
Sydney street blocks to prevent horses slipping,
and to deaden the sound of traffic ?

The fruit crop is very good this year, and
the prudent housekeeper is busy looking after the
year’s supply of preserves and filling up shortages
in Jam Jars. There is but little satisfaction in
using a common, badly-fitting, badly-made jar,
•which, moreover, is liable to break when the fruit
is poured in. Some new patterns have been intro-
duced this year by M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd.,
Melb. and Sydney. Notably the Gilchrist— this is
a large-mouthed jar, with porcelain immerser;
and the Canton jar, a clear, white glass, with easy-
fitting spring top showing the contents to the best
advantage.*

This interesting circular was issued last
month to all Maoriland railway employes :
Circular, GOl/10. To Staff.—On the day of the funeral
of Queen no railway business of any kind whatever is to
be transacted in any part of the colony between the
hours of 11.30 a.m. and 12 noon, and every member of
the Railway Staff, whether permanent or casual, is to
cease work for the half-hour specified, and to stand
reverently by during that period. No train is to be
started between the times mentioned, and every train
running on any portion of the railway lines of the colony
must be brought to a standstill at 11.30 a.m. and remain
motionless until 12 noon, at which hour running and
ordinary business of the Department is to be resumed.
Trains which have been stopped being, of course, cor-
respondingly later throughout their journeys. Please see
that these instructions are strictly complied with.
But why wasn’t it carried to its logical conclusion?
Why didn’t all trams, omnibuses, bullock-drays,
carts, wheelbarrows, and foot-passengers also stop
short at 11.30 and stand exactly where they were
till 12? 'Why didn’t the whole province stop
short, the policeman paralysed at his corner, and
the drinker looking at his beer, and the eater
looking at his victuals, and the live person sud-
denly becoming dead, so that there might be
silence in all Maoriland for 30min. just as there
once was in heaven ? A thing like that should be
done well or not at all.

In a M.L. township, some of the inhabi-
tants were engaged in a tennis tournament when
the news of the Queen’s death arrived, and his
Washup the Mayor came out and ordered them to
desist on the instant. They refused, whereupon
he ordered the hose to be turned on them and
routed the damp and disloyal citizens from the
field.

* * * »
Victorian P.M.G-. Outtrim was called
upon t’other day to determine whether a letter-
carrier, charged with being unfit for duty, was
really drunk within the meaning of the Postal
Act. The infallible writing test was applied, and
the man sat down and wrote in copper-plate style
a dictated passage. After he had been honorably
acquitted and had lurched off triumphant, one of
the P.M.G.’s colleagues declared with a grin,
“You couldn’t have given him an easier test.
That man can never write properly until he has
had a few whiskies.”
* * * «
The superiority of the news-service of the
American papers, which English and Australian-
English people affect to despise, is well proved by
a recent event. As far back as Dec. 28’Frisco
Chronicle had an article on its front page an-
nouncing the serious illness of the Queen
guardedly hinting that the worst might be ex-
pected, and ascribing sufficient importance to the
fact to accompany it with a large block. Yet no
London paper touched upon the matter until
almost a month later, a few days prior to the
actual death of the monarch. As a well-known
Australian pressman said about the great London
dailies : “ They want an affidavit about an item
before they’ll chance publishing it.”

At the swearing-in of N.S.W. Judges on
commencement of term, there was some curiosity
as to whether the Jewish member of the Bench
(Mr. Justice Cohen) would divest himself of the
full-bottomed wig and don the hat in orthodox
Hebrew fashion. But it turned out that his
Honor had sworn himself in a week before.

Maoriland’s lawyers, who are needy and
numerous, have greedy eyes fixed on S’Africa.
Where the carcase is there will the eagles gather.
However, as the M.L.-made bird of carrion is not
recognised in Africa, King Seddon has been
petitioned by the Devil’s brigade to obtain status
for it by the time martial law is superseded by
civil law in the happy hunting-ground.

Now that they have erected a “ Mafeking
tablet” in Blayney (N.S.W.) Town Hall, it is
appropriate to point out that the “ siege ” was a
farce, since “we could have got away at any
time,” said Barnum Powell; that the “hard-
ships” were a humbug since Rev. James Green,
Bushmen’s Contingent chaplain, reported the gar-
rison fat and jolly, with 1000 sheep to spare ; that
the tablet’s inscription of “small British garri-
son,” contrasted with “several thousand Boers,”
is a lie, since the Boers hardly outnumbered the
garrison for the greater part of the siege ; and
that the “ heroism ” is a myth, since the garrison,
after seven months’ idleness, marched out practi-
cally as strong as it marched in, without any losses
to speak of. All this in the teeth of Roberts, who
has to delude the public like the rest.

Writes Subscriber :
Re your recent Jaypee par let me say that in the small
town where I reside, not 100 miles from Sydney there are
over 40 “ Jaypees,” among them being butchers, grocers,
hay and corn and potato men, police-court attorneys, a
money-lender’s agent, and “ sich like.” Is it not time
there was a revision of the list ?
* * * *
The wife of the Governor of an Australasian
State has “ taken-up ” a person whom “ Society ”
has more or less tabooed. The lady in question is
the much-married wife of a functionary whose
position it was impossible to gainsay, but there
was much ado when she appeared at Guv’ment
House. However, nothing was said till it was
found that Lady took Mrs. out driving,
and made a chum of her. Some of the front-
rankers then received the latter at their houses
out of. compliment to her vice-regal patroness, but
one blue-blooded dame wrote informing Lady
that the ladies of the city objected to her protegee
and giving a picturesque account of the latter’s
alleged career. Result—prompt removal of the
writer’s name from Govt. House list, followed by
a still greater display of friendship for the
maligned woman, concerning whose character
Her Ex. is no doubt perfectly satisfied.

An Adelaide detective has long enjoyed
an enviable reputation for his eloquent reports,
not to mention frequent eulogistic paragraphs in
the press. The cat came out of the bag last
week when the detective was sued by an ex-law-
clerk for £23 19s. for “services rendered.”
Plaintiff claimed that all the able reports were of
his composing. Eloquent extract from his claim :
1898. October. Re gang of burglars. Attending you
several times with reference to preparing a report regard-
ing the shadowing and final arrest of certain men at
Petersburg, £2 2s. Re Holt—July. Attending you on
different occasions and receiving instructions for all
particulars and report to the Attorney-General, re Holt,
for assault and robbery ; drafting same at length and
afterwards reading it over to you, when you desired
certain alterations and additions, which were made, £1 Is.
Re Spielers—Various attendances on you, obtaining
details for a report on certain spielers who had been
arrested for various offen ces, drawing report, tabulating
convictions, and going through same with you, and
making alterations, £1 is.
Although the detective alleged that all this was
“a dream” to him, Russell, S.M. thought
differently, and awarded plaintiff £lB 14s.

The query arises in Vic. Education De-
partment which implement is best for castigating
wayward small boys—strap or cane? “What’s
best administered is best.”
As the late Queen’s eldest daughter, the
Empress Frederick, is dying—she is afflicted not
only with kidney disease, which has now caused
dropsy, but also with cancer—there is yet another
source of gloom to interfere with the Duke of
York celebrations in Australia.
♦ * * *
The Lyne Government’s agreement with
the innumerable boarding-housekeepers of Sydney
for the housing of that Federal guest who never
came was so much per day for “board and lodg-
ing” in the usual way. Now there is a belated
tendency to economy, and consequently there are
unseemly rows with anxious, bony females in the
palatial halls of the Treasury as to whether pay-
ment should be for the board ordered or said to
be ordered, or only for the board actually eaten.
The Commonwealth celebrations are already a
thing of the far gone-by, but still the worn and
yellowed hash-lady streams up and down the
Treasury stairs, with her greasy bill or memoran-
dum in one hand and her mortified umbrella in
the other, and wants to know. And Lyne is so
busy being two things at once, and not much good
at either of them, that he hardly has time to know
himself as yet.
• • * *
“Insulting behaviour” is a useful and
comprehensive charge. Whatever it doesn’t cover
can be usually included in the other expansive
charge, “conduct calculated to lead to a breach of
the peace.” A Victorian aboriginal lately lodged a
boomerang on a roof while giving performances
with the implement, and in recovering it he fell
through a skylight into a bakery, where his sable
form bounced considerably on tAe pastry. He
was charged with “ insulting behaviour” for drop-
ping on the tarts, but the case was dismissed.
For once even good old “ insulting behaviour ”
missed fire.
* • * *
One of Victoria’s new Old Age Pensioners
got so excited at the prospect of leaving that
Abode of Lost Hopes—the Benevolent Asylum—
that he forgot all about his heart-disease and
rushed back to pack up his few belongings, thus
over-exerting himself so seriously that he died at
the gates.
Apropos of something which happened in
Sydney recently. The Bulletin expects shortly
to see-the following advt. in the daily papers :
A fascinating widow, having great social influence,
undertakes to get refund and pay vouchers through
Govt, departments in the shortest time on record.
Guarantees to get vouchers issued and payments made by
the Treasury within 2i hours.

It was at a Sydney seaside resort, and the
two men who ran the musical merry-go-round
held an earnest consultation as
to whether they should cease
going round merrily to show
their respect for the late Sove-
reign Lady. Being too tender-
hearted to deprive all the wait-
ing children of amusement,
they decided to drape appro-
priately and continue running.
A messenger was despatched to
obtain the necessary funereal
trappings, and the show went
on, and the pennies tinkled in,
and the sky and the sea were
blue, and everything was charm-
ing. When the draping arrived
the machine stopped for about 35
minutes while it went into mourn-
ing, and the youngsters, attracted
by the black hangings, made
business very brisk. Things
were booming, when a party of
heavy-eyed individuals ap-
proached and glared with dull,
fishy eyes at the proprietor. The
proprietor saw trouble, and en-
deavored to pacify the angry
visitors with two bottles of beer.
The gentlemen drank the beer,
and the man with the merry-go-
round felt easier, and his partner
opined it was “all right.” But
just then a spirit of cussedness
seemed to enter the minds of
those evil men, and they broke
into language, and talked about
their shocked feelings, and called
the proprietors Boers. Also, they
raked the fire out, knocked off the
safety valve, and carefully car-
ried off the till and poured the
contents down the bar-sink of the
nearest pub. When King Ed-
ward dies that merry-go-round
man is going to stop business immediately word
comes that the sovereign’s condition is serious.
* • * *
Melbourne raised a most remarkable sui-
cide a few days ago. The chief actor, a widow of
40, seems to have taken poison in the street while
out walking with her little daughter aged seven.
Then she rushed with a rope in her hand into the
house of a casual acquaintance, told the child to
sit down for a while, and stepped out into the
back premises and hanged himself. The small
girl (aged seven) went to look for her after a
while, and finding her dangling by the neck care-
fully took all her money out of her pocket for fear
of accidents. Then the thrifty infant gave the
alarm, and was sent for the police. Meanwhile
the owner of the house went to look at the body,
but apparently didn’t cut it down, that being left
for the constable to do. Everybody connected
with the affair appears to have been cool and col-
lected—the lady who hanged herself in another
lady’s house; the other lady who apparently
didn’t suspect anything unusual even when a
comparative stranger rushed through her premises
with a rope in her hand; and especially the small
girl of seven who cleaned-out her suspended
parent first of all and mentioned lier unusual atti-
tude afterwards.
* * * *
The outside doctors have at last found
that they cannot get on without the Adelaide
Hospital practice, and so they have surrendered
at discretion. In future deaths under chloroform
and other little “accidents,” which have hitherto
always been made as public as possible by the dis-
affected parties, may occur at the institution
without being quite so loudly mentioned. Also, the
University students will be let loose on the
patients and the nurses who have for years had
none but qualified doctors to deal with, as the
University took the side, of the “striking” me-
dicoes. It is not difficult to see who is getting
the'best of the peace now patched up.

An ingenuous Caledonian hight Jamie
Macgregor was recently observed by a Melbourne
policeman moving stealthily among the wild
thistles on a vacant Latrobe-street block towards
a pair o’ troosers that flaunted defiantly from a
neighbouring clothes line. Jamie having stalked
his prize, put them on, and when arrested he ex-
plained that whilst he slept among the thistles
aforesaid, someone stole his own pants and left
him in his coat and shirt; so be had to steal
“ a pair o’ breeks or be pit in gaol for indecency
ye-ken ? ” But, as be was wearing his own under-
neath the others, he got 3 months.
Sydney chess-players are notorious for
squabbling over trifles. Chess champion J. Ij.
Jacobsen lately left the Sydney Chess Club amid
mutual recriminations, and now he has come in
violent conflict with the School of Arts Club, per-
haps the best-known and oldest club in Australia.
Aided by the timely intervention of what is known
as the “democracy of chess,” the champion
triumphed over his detractors at.a recent special
meeting of the members of the club. At the con-
clusion of this meeting the president, vice-presi-
dents and several prominent members of com-
mittee resigned their membership, and the old
club now seems split in two.

THE
WOMAN
SPEAKS
So you think because I’m a woman
1 was made but for pleasure and tears, j.
You, who smile and sneer at tbe sex I claim //
With the savoir faire of your forty years /t
Ah, yes, I’m a woman, and human, too, vv,
I can laugh and weep, and—pity me— love. I
That's the part of me made for the play of maniS
Man ! No thanks, I can manage my glove '/I
We shall meet to-night at the dance perhaps
You’ll see me flirting behind my fan, )/
With arms agleam and shoulders bared, £
To the critical vision of men, oh man
And you’ll come to me claiming a waltz
I’ll grant your wish with a grateful smile ;
And your arm will clasp me a moment or two.
And we shall be lovers, a little while.
But ob the thoughts that my smiles suppress
(For I am strong, quite strong, oh man 1),
The measuring, searching, judging thoughts
That I hide as only a woman can 1
l I’m only a woman, whose passionate heart
Is made for laughter, and tears, and love,
\ And that is for men ; but soul and brain
I keep for myself and the gods above.

A WOMANS LETTER.
„ Sydney, February 19. 1901.
Mt Dkab Myee.—
Another dull week. Sydney continues to emu-
late The Man Who was Away and the only excite-
ment that goes on is located at Bowral or
Katoomba. The Carrington has been breaking
out into dances and card-parties lately by way of
a quiet time for Society women after the Federal
racket. I hear that at a euchre party there last
week Society frocked itself as if no Queen had ever
died, one smart matron flaunting pink silk, and
several airing white without any black whatever.
Mrs Harvey Lovely, who won the prize, was
gowned in black, and Mrs. Brenton’s white voile
was kept mournful with a jaunty black velvet
bolero.
By the way, such an exodus of society women
is in preparation that everyone wonders what will
become of pur next “season,” to say nothing of
the lugubrious festivities when the Yorks arrive.
Among those who are leaving are Mrs. Willie
Mitchell, Mrs. Ted Simpson, and Miss Clara
Manning, Sydney’s smart trio.
Last week it was whispered that several fair
travellers had arranged to make a prolonged stay
at Capetown, where all the men are now, but the
bubonic outbreak lias changed their plans, and
they will now make for London, which is always
better manned than Sydney, anyway.
Tell it not in Gath, but between ourselves the
general feeling in Sydney last week, when the
Yorks decided to go to Melbourne first, was one
of intense relief. It was all very well for Sydney
to achieve that “ Pre-eminent in Woe ” on paper,
but to put it into practice, and receive Royalties
with a combination of effusion and mourning, and
damped smiles and craped bouquets, was neither
an easy nor tempting thing to look for-
ward to, and we handed their Highnesses over to
Melbourne with “feelings of joy not unmixed
with gladness,” as the—the—Woman of N.S.W.
once put it in another historic address to a Some-
body. The King’s request that all festivities be
of a restrained nature turns Sydney’s thoughts
back mournfully to its Champagne Commonwealth
week, and the old hope of having that all over
again is being sorrowfully abandoned.
Art students hostessed an “ At Horne” at the
Society of Artists’ rooms last week, when the
men were all boys, and the difficulty of getting
up a party in February was demonstrated to the
satisfaction of lots of pretty young girls in aesthetic
hair. By the way, the Government, has refused,
definitely, to subsidise either Art Society again
unless the rivals amalgamate. The matter is
agitating Art circles fearfully, and everyone is
holding his breath. That amalgamation is needed,
and is the only way of keeping Art on a commer-
cial level with, say, chironomy, is the opinion of
the man in the street; but artists hold out little
hope of either Society “yielding,” as they put it.
Hawtrey’s co. practically ran the Rand wick
races last Saturday. Hawtrey himself was out
there in a striped blue-and-white suit and sailor
hat, escorting Miss Ruby Ray, who is one of the
gracefully-thm kind that look better in a lot of
clothes than in the decollete she wears in the
“Message from Mars.” Miss Ray, who is only
19, by the way, wore a long tan coat over a black
skirt, and was sailor-hatted, with her hair dressed
down on her neck. She looked very pretty and
chic in that style of dress. Herbert Ross was
with Mrs. Herbert Ross, a bright-faced woman in
flounced black voile with a lace yoke. Mrs. Her-
bert doesn t act herself. She merely travels round
with her husband. Society has taken up the
Rosses, and the lady is as popular as Mr. Ross him-
self. The Messenger from Mars—Mr. Stephenson
—was also out on the punt. He is the image of the
manager of the famour Press Bazaar, Mr. Druce,
and the resemblance increases as he speaks. Dainty
Irene Franklin hugged the fence all the afternoon,
merely turning a back-view of herself in a short
tt>lue-grey frock with her red curly hair tied with
a white ribbon. The prettiest thing about Irene
is her feet, always exquisitely shod, which accounts
for her short frocks, that are ever so much younger
than her figure.
Black was the lawn frocking, as it always
is now. People who got black frocks a fortnight
ago must wear them, but I note that a decided
leaning towards touching-up the black with colour
has already set in. The Land Minister’s wife
had a turquoise vest on her Cimmerian gown.
Mrs. Lassetter wore a well-cut black cloth coat and
skirt, with a jaunty little black toque and white
wing. Miss Irene Marks wore a black tucked
silk blouse with silver buttons down the front, a
voile skirt, and big black hat. Mrs. Harry
Levien was the best-dressed woman present in a
much-tucked black voile with a train, and a
black-feathered crinoline hat. Mrs. Alfred Marks
wore a black frock, and a sailor-hat on her golden
hair.
By the way, one horse was named Rabato, and
a woman, told me a lovely story of how she went
to a clairvoyant the day before the races, and
asked, among other questions, “ Can you tell me
anything about Rabato?” “Oh, yes,” said clair-
voyant ob igingly, looking into space. “ Rabato !
Rabato ! I see him with a noble brow and long
white hands. He is dark, and he loves you, and
I think he is a sculptor. Is that right ? ” “ No,”
said the woman. So the clairvoyant returned to
her trance again, and presently discovered that
Rabato was a musician. “ I was a little confused
at first,” she confessed ; “but I saw all along he
was doing something with his fingers.”
That reminds me that if anybody wants to learn
that sort of thing, I have just received a prospec-
tus from a Teacher of Occultism, who has classes
to instruct in Chiromancy, Mesmerism, Astrology
and five things ending in “ ology,” Chironomy,
and four things ending in “onomy,” and Divina-
tion by Cards and Teacups for 10s. 6d. a quarter.
Also Crystal Gazing, which I’d like to learn my-
self if it has anything to do with a Stony Stare.
This teacher of occultism is raising a flutter in
the dove-cotes where they give you a dark
husband for a shilling. They don’t like their
secrets given away to the public, for occultism is
one of the best-paying professions going. All you
need is a palm in the window, two chairs and a
gabble. Then the money rolls in.
Not in the same breath, but after a long, long
pause I went to the Metaphysicians’ At Home last
Tuesday in Vickery’s Buildings. The Mets. gave
the soiree to Miss Catherine St. Hill, a well-known
laly Met. The guest of the evening was so
beautifully frocked in an aesthetic high-necked
black silk gown that I surmised Metaphysics is
not the impoverishing profession it might have
been in the dark ages of Sydney when people lived
by bread alone instead of faith. The evening was
pleasantly filled with music and recitation.
By the. way, Miss St. Hill was once the success-
ful palmist, Alexia St. Hill. She used to work
for the Illustrated Sydney News, and had an
immense private connection as well. At the
zenith of her success, coining money as she was,
and considered a cut above the ordinary palmist,
she gave it all up for metaphysics, and has devoted
herself to faith-healing ever since. Under her
yellow-shaded lamps, on Tuesday, I saw Mr. and
Mrs. Robert M'Millan, Mrs. Kingsmill Shaw,
Miss Edith Lamb, Mr. and Mrs. Nall, Mrs.
Curnow, Mrs. Le Gay Brereton, and heard
Mercy Murray do the Teazle Quarrel with grace
and brains.
Last week there were paragraphs in all the
papers announcing the serious illness of John
Tebbutt, the astronomer. Mr. Tebbutt was a
Windsor boy, and lives at Windsor still, in a
lonely old house called The Observatory, a melan-
choly poetical home surrounded with pine-trees,
on a peninsula between the Hawkesbury and a
creek. In this house for many years there went
on a long succession of sad occurrences that
reminds one of the ill-fated Bronte sisters.
There were eight daughters at The Observatory
at one time, all charming, gentle girls, looked
on as heiresses in the district. One by one—
with the exception of two —as these girls grew to
womanhood they faded and passed away, despite
all that could be done. Mrs. Tebbutt was a
familiar figure in Windsor, driving with her gen-
tle daughters, hut now she is rarely seen. And
her husband, the astronomer, leads the life of a
recluse up there among his telescopes. He was
offered N.S.W. Govt.-Astronomership in 1862,
but refused it. He is now 70, and is well-known
in European scientific circles for his observations
of the Donati comet and other wandering bodies.
As lively as anything else that happened last
week was a church meeting to welcome a new rec-
tor. A few extracts from the addresses—the rec-
tor, of course, present : “Our new rector is a
good-lookin’man.” “The Rev. Mr. Blank is a
young man. Ha! that touches the ladies’hearts.”
I see the ladies have heard that our new Rector
is young, for see how they all have their new
dresses on !” “I ’ear that Mr. Curate, who is
leavin’ us, was spoken of by our young men as a
jolly little fellow, but I’m not goin’ to tell you
what I ’eard the ladies sye about ’im.” And so on.
There was a titled citizen in the chair who could not
keep up with this flow of church witticism. He was
only an ex-mayor,_ and the aldermanic stock jest
about water-carts is a long way removed from the
church-meeting one of “the ladies” and “the
young ladies.” S’Matthew has never been given
that way, and looked painfully conscious of his
inability to skip round and be funny on the giddy
lines of the church. The Rector being welcomed
was the Rev. Mr. Charlton, who has taken over the
charge of St. Barnabas.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Queen is dead ! Although full mourning
is supposed to be worn till March 16, and half
until April 17th, already one sees little black
beyond a few ties on the men, and hat-bands on
the women’s ‘gems.’ And the fund for Mel-
bourne’s memorial statue drags dreadfully, the
Age raking in about £4 a day, and the Mayor little
more.
“A leading Melb. family which is fretting
bravely for the good Queen has turned out its
coachman in dark livery done up with crape arm-
bands, and has attended even to the mournful
detail of sooty rosettes on the whips—while the
distinguished head of the family wears the aspect
of a professional pall-bearer. Grief could go no
further!

graph re the Blanks and Dashes of Sydney with
the remark that “Mrs. Blank Dash has six
children, who, when children, promised to be as
lovely as their mother.”

congregation.’ A photograph of that ‘painful
thrill would probably have revealed something
very like a smile. A fashionable Anglican church
crowd doesn c take the Litany in a serious spirit
The prayer for the Royal Family is regarded as a
humorous item, and Canon Tucker’s perfunctory
mention of the old monarch instead of the new
not likely to make many of his flock
shudder.

Lord Rosebery’s eldest daughter is of marriage-

“Fifty of the Naicest ‘employers’ turned up
inßuxton’sGallery(Melb.)onThursday, at the bid-
ding of a smart young woman who purposed setting
up a new-fashioned registry office, ‘ to consider the
servant question.’ But, unfortunately, the daily
papers are taken by registry-office keepersand Bid-

The oratory of Herald Reav, of the Anti-

ass: the provoking meeting, called to protect em-
ployers, would only bray blessings on the default-
ing maids.
“Bland Holt’s attraction, ‘The Great Ruby,’ is
both exciting and convincing. Such a comfort
not to see the plot hampered by an impossible
funny man, who ‘talks back’ to desperadoes and
trips up ruffians, but fails to check the course of
villainy or serve any other useful purpose by his
irrelevancies. Frances Ross, as a bad, beautiful
Russian countess, sets off her impressive points
aud paces by various striking gowns, notably an
evening toilette of blended orange and lemon
color with black lace and a note of opal-green.
Vice suits the decorative Frances, who is ever so
much more interesting as a sinner than in her
usual stage groove of pale, pure, proud saint,
‘icily regular, splendidly null,’a smile frozen on
her lips, aud her eyes glued to the great heart of
the gallery. Baker’s imposing Indian prince
army man and champion cricketer is not so much a
lithe passionate Oriental youth, infatuated with an
adventuress older than himself, as a sable, serious
Personage, inseparable from fat, white gloves and
broad spotless cuffs. Somehow he suggests Mrs.
Stowe’s hero ready for camp-meetin’ in broadcloth
and boiled shirt, and in the end, when he’s carried
in moribund on a stretcher, one’s heart sinks as if
the dying darkie represented, not ‘ Kassim ’ but
that much older friend ‘Uncle Tom’ freshly wal-
loped by Legree.
“Re the wicked Countess’s knack of egging-on
her admirers, white and colored, to steal gems,
writers seem all alike in depicting women as ready
to go to any lengths for the sake of sparklers.
Now, here’s a true-as-death story. An insignificant
Australian patriarch saved up and took his wife
and dumpy domestic daughter for a year’s jaunt,
the treat of a lifetime. On the way they got to
know a rich educated Eastern. I can’t swear he was
a ‘ prince,’ but his income seemed princely. None
of the Australian trio angled for his notice or tried
to coax diamonds or emeralds out of him, but
somehow he fell in love with the daughter, wanted
to marry her, and offered her heaps of presents of
the sort that woman gets credit for valuing beyond
everything—rin>fs, &c. Neither pa, ma, nor
daughter took any stock in ‘colored persons,’ so
they unanimously repelled all overtures, came
home at the end of their holiday, and have lived
a dull life in Australia ever since, though for some
time the traveller acquaintance kept on writing
to the old man and forwarding to the daughter
gewgaws which were always returned.
“Miss Lillah M‘Carthy, the handsome other

coming along right up to Feb., with Lillah’s New ■

hail from the fair one, to give old friends the
opportunity of applauding her latest successes.
“The other night, a strange thing happened at
the Pantomime. A prominent blossom of the
ballet, who sheds, as a rule, smiles on all the
stall Johnnies, smiled and smiled at one
particular mirthless individual till half the d.c.
and the one occupant of the box had fixed the for-
tunate with a curious eye. Between the acts,
men wondered who the lucky stranger could be.
It was only her hubby, after all !
‘After all,. that poor 71-year-old amateur
cyclist started in that big recent wheel-race, other
competitors being of the Martin and Beauchamp
calibre ! When the infatuated Ancient saw him-
self ‘lapped’ he withdrew, owing, as he explained
later, to ‘want of breath.’ For ages, practical
jokers have been egging-on this misguided
patriarch, inducing him to drink bullocks’ blood
daily at the abbatoirs, ‘ pack ’ in oiled blankets
and otherwise torment himself for the amusement
of scoffers.
“No matter how old a woman may be, she is
always open to conviction that Age has not
withered her charms. And most old men need
little flattery to convince them that in bodily
prowess they’re a match for the youngsters
Remember in. ‘ What Happened to Jones,’ how
ready the flabby old bookworm Prof, is to listen
to the wheedling ‘drummer,’ who, for his own
ends, hails the senile scholar as ‘a wonder,’
because of his rapid crawl when surprised by the
police at a glove-fight.
“ Mayoress Gillott has apparently a twin-sister
whose resemblance to Mrs. Sam. is bewildering
even to her friends. This lady, who was not
much in evidence with her sister till the
Mayoral reign began, comes in for a lot of civic
gush. People are always making mistakes—and
apologising to the real Mayoress for saluting and
flattering her double. Both seem to take the
matter of mixing ’em as a joke and hold it no
offence. But what mistakes will arrive in the
May jumble! It would be better for the twin,
instead of explaining, to accept the honors and
live up to the misunderstanding.
“Mrs. W. Williams (nee M'Caugham) fought
tooth-and-nail for her husband during the recent
St. Kilda electioneering campaign, quite in the
fashion of the Conservative dames in England,
and watched the polling on the eventful day with
breathless interest. It is generally conceded that
young Williams won the seat mainly through the
popularity aud influence of his chic bride. A good
beginning for the pair.
“ Kharki-worship turned the good-looking
officers of the Orient green with envy last
Thursday. Formerly the boys in blue broke the
record at mashing, but that was in times of peace
and quarter-deck philandering. On Thursday a
stream of black-bordered belles made their way
down to the liner at Port Melbourne with secret
souvenirs for the departing boys. ‘ Keep this for
Lieut. Blank till (sob) you get into the bay.’ Or
the request ran : ‘Do you mind looking after this
little parcel for Lieut. Dash till you leave to-
morrow?’ till in the end the purser had about 60
parcels piled roof-high in his den, and every cor-
responding crib in the place was packed with pre-
cious presents for the dear pets. ‘Not a glance,
not a tear, not a word for us, ’ groaned the nice
nautical boys; ‘ nothing does a girl now but the
wretched kharki or kilts.’ And then they went
off and prayed for big seas, to have revenge.
“The most presentable of the officers that left
with the last batch for Africa is a magnificent
youth who used to give Collins-street promenaders
an occasional glimpse of his fine figure and fancy
cravats. He had an aggravating habit of throw-
ing out his chest and pointing his nose upwards,
but all the same he was what the gushers call ‘a
lovely-looking fellow,’ and he had a flock of them
tumbling over each other to say good-bye on the
pier last Friday. He insisted —in his wisdom-
on saying good-bye, not au revoir, alleging that
either a bullet or a billet would anchor him in the
Transvaal.
“The eldest son of Rev. Dr. Strong (who has
denounced the Boer war from the pulpit as wicked
and unjustifiable) has gone with the last Vic.
contingent as veterinary lieutenant. That comes
of not listening to his pa’s sermons.
“The son of a distinguished official wore tan
boots at a fashionable dance the other night.
This is the young man who tells the Melb. girls
he is going to be Sir Blanky Secundus. Only
fancy! If the father had but half his son’s
assurance !
“ The cupola on top of the dome of Melb.
Exhibition building has been brilliantly gilded.
Whether this is because somebody committed
suicide there lately, or because the Yorks are
coming, is not sufficiently explained. Probably
the latter affair has something to do with it.
“Queen Alexandra’s private secretary, Major-
General Stanley Clarke, is at present in Melbourne
with his wife. It is ostensibly a purely holiday
trip that he is in—just a tour, don’t you know.
Nevertheless, the letters sent to England by
Stanley Clarke should be very interesting reading.
He is a handsome, distinguished-looking man,
with a regular official smile on him—the smile
that never was on sea or land, except on a private
secretary’s face.
“Premier Peacock only laughed once at the
huge meeting Barton addressed in Melbourne.
The explosion took place over a story told by
Drake illustrating how he came to be included in
the Federal Ministry. ‘ Two people were being
married,’ related Drake —‘a man and a woman.’
Then Peacock, having recently ‘been there,’ let
go so tremendously that Drake was visibly
startled. The story told how when the parson
asked the man ‘Wilt thou have this woman for
thy wedded wife?’ the man said ‘Yes, but I’d
rather have her sister.’ So, said Drake, Barton
took him for Minister but would rather have had
Dickson. Apropos, a certain politician’s father-
in-law said lately :—‘ If I had a vote in my son-
in-law’s district, I don’t know that I’d give it to
him. Of my two daughters he didn’t choose the
best, and that shows that he is not as sound in
judgment as he’s cracked up to be.’
“The principle reason for sculptor Bertram
Mackennal’s coming to Australia just now is to
superintend the erection in Brighton cemetery
(Vic.) —where poet Gordon is buried—of a splen-
did mausoleum to the memory of the late Mrs.
Springthorpe. Dr. Springthorpe has spent about
£7OOO on the monument, for which he sent the
commission to Mackennal about two years ago.
Lately, however, the clever physician has resolved
to solace his loneliness by a new wife. Apart
from this monument and that of Gordon, the
finest piece of sculpture in the Brighton burial-
ground is that over the grave of Surgeon O’Hara’s
first wife. It rather encourages a second lady to
say ‘ I will ’ when a husband is so lavish with his
chiselled marble over his first mate.
. “ The loss of one’s store teeth and trifles of that
kind in the baths is a mere bagatelle compared
with disappearance of the wedding-ring and
keeper which a relative of a celebrated
Australian contralto parted with while
having a dip at Mornington t’other day.
When the distraught matron discovered her loss
she gave the alarm to the man in charge, and
hundreds of willing hands went through the sands
under the water for the priceless badges. But all
the search came to nothing, so the tearful wife
went home to wire her husband (who was in town)
of the loss. Alas ! men don’t always replace lost
wedding-rings quite so enthusiastically as they
purchased the original token of surrender.
“The joys of mixed bathing at a fashionable
resort reached a climax, a week or two ago, when
a fancy-dress carnival was held in the water, by
moonlight. Over 20 well - known Melbourne
revellers took part in this unique wet, and all the
local residents assembled on the beach, with
breathless eagerness, to watch the proceedings.
The Naiads, with scarcely an exception, seemed
to have run short of garments, and what little they
wore was too brief and skimped to be associated
with any bona-fide fancy costume on record. The
men were clothed with more liberality as regards
‘ covering ’; one sported a retrenched Highland
uniform, and another a cycling-racer’s dress,
which fitted quite fancifully enough.
“ The girl who set her heart on outshining all
the water-nymphs of the Bay is one of the leaders
of the anti-Suffrage movement. Her gown and
bathing-cloak (the latter dropped before immer-
sion) are a picture of daintiness, and a pink bonnet
revealing a few patent-preserving crimped tresses
adds to the piquancy of the f ire. Ribbons
flutter, flounces vacillate, and be.'„s jingle as she
trips along the passage to the bath-steps, and as
she finally poises, bird-like, on the board
preparatory to taking a dive, all the womankind
roundabout stop short and admire reluctantly and
spitefully. The woman who dresses for woman
gets only a hard grunt of approval anyhow. Now

The cabled decision that the Yorks are to
land first at Melbourne has caused exceeding
great joy in the haunts of the Naicest. The
Toorakiennes will be first to meet their Duke face
to face, and be first to discover that an indefinable
something in the figure of the dear Duchess pro-
claims her a member of the P. D. sisterhood. For
that matter, if the keen-eyed angels look very hard
at the Duke, they will probably notice that his
waist owes a little to the P.D. corset.
