One of the dangers of Sydney during the holi-
day season will be the burglar and sneak-thief. The
Federation festivities will attract all the rascaldom of
Australasia, and plate and purses will be constantly
menaced. Those who are leaving Sydney, and visitors
to Sydney, therefore should deposit their plate, cash
and valuables in the Sydney Safe Deposit for security
Access can be had to them at any time during business
hours. The vaults are situated behind Paling’s, off
George-street, and are always open for inspection. They
are in themselves a sight worth seeing.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
What the oldest Melb. inhabitant cannot
remember—When Wax. Madden wasn’t bald ;
seems to have been born so.
The late Henry Ricketson, of Barratta,
used to boast that he had fought 84 Supreme
Court actions, 63 of which he won.
G. H. Reid will be 56 years old on Mon-
day next (25th Feb.). The Bulletin wishes him
many happy returns, and hopes that his Dog will
remain Dry.
One recently-arrived Maoriland infant
(whose father is contingenteering in S. Africa) has
been saddled with the names of “Zealandia
Afrikander.”
Earl Beauchamp, who is still nominally
Governor of N.S.W., despite Lyne’s sentence of
banishment, completed his 29th year on Wednes-
day, Feb. 20.
The story goes that wife-poisoner George
Dean was “forgiven” and released from prison
some time ago, and granted a sum of money to
leave the country.
Piet de Wet, safe in the British camp,
urges Christian de Wet to accept the British
terms of peace. Ever does timorous Peter seem
destined to deny and betray.
•Kingston was the first Federal Cabinet
Minister to visit Queensland, for Dickson did not
live to return with his new honors, and Drake was
not appointed till Kingston’s tour was complete.
A certain Q. politician took a parcel of
stale sandwiches from his pocket on a railway
platform t’other day and tendered the provender
to a perspiring porter who had been wrestling
with his luggage. Refused with scorn.
Queen Victoria’s last thought was for a
pet Pomeranian dog. The hand that had held a
sceptre for sixty-three years, and had been kissed
by h undreds of sycophants, was finally stretched
out to receive what she anyhow thought was the
genuine caress of a dog.

daily remarks • that John Forrest “was un-
doubtedly lucky to get the G.C.M.G., especially
as Mr. Kingston has been overlooked.” It would
f reatly surprise a vast number of people if, despite
is “Right Hon.,” Kingston was found to be
ambitious of a G.C.M.G.
A Melbourne paper declares that Cardinal
Moran is a probable Senatorial candidate in
N.S.W., which is probably all moonshine : once
bitten twice shy. In Melbourne, Presbyterian
parson Ronald has announced himself as a starter,
and Pastor Bevan is also spoken of as a hopeless
candidate in the Shoddy interest.
Died lately at Rangiora (M.L.), Angus
Macdonald, shepherd, allegedly aged 105. He man-
aged a run for Cracroft Wilson in days agone,
assisted in mustering until he was 90, and did odd
jobs until 99. The old man never needed a doctor
until he was 96, was a confirmed smoker, and
shaved himself until the day of his death.
Once when Lord Hopetoun was Governor
of Victoria someone told him of a certain poli-
tician who, instead of saying “ Aides-de-Kong,”
said “Aides-de-Camp,” pronouncing the word
like camp, in the ordinary English way. “Aides
“ONE PEOPLE, ONE DESTINY.”
decamp,” said Hopetoun, “ well so they do when
they can get anyone to decamp with them.”
That good Radical, Judge A. I. Clark, of
Tasmania, will to-morrow (Sunday) enter on his
54th year. He is a native of Hobart. His Honor
is one of the very few Australian lawyers who
have been sent, to England to appear before the
Privy Council although, as a compromise (over
the Main Line Railway dispute) was arrived at
during his voyage, he did not face the Court.
Ex-Governor Brassey has promised to
build a public reading-room and institute at Cats-
field, Sussex (Eng.), “ as a thanks-offering for his
safe return from Australia.” He fell off so many
things, and so frequently, that the wonder is he
ever did return. Victoria has
thanks-offering for having got rid of the pompou
old gentleman.

A little knot of Single-taxers tried to make
things hot for Barton at the Adelaide meeting,
but they suffered each time, and at last became
quiet. Once Barton hesitated over a word when
referring to Reid, and the small coterie laughed
loudly. “ I stick at a good many things, good-
humoredly remarked the Premier 5
some people who stick at nothing. The audience
caught the point.
The Duke of Cumberland, Queen Vic-
toria’s uncle, was Grand Master of the English
Orange Lodges. He used to sit there m state,
honored as monarch, during the last year oi
William IV.’s life. William disliked Victoria, or
rather her mother, but he hated Cumberland and
did all he could to thwart him. Cumberland s
“pull” with the Orangemen was through their
belief that Victoria was a Catholic.
Dear Bulletin, —You speak of Donald
Macdonald “reconciling his Presbyterian con-
science ” at Coolgardie by lecturing after church-
hours on Sundays. Now, Mac. happens to belong
to a far northern Highland R.C. family. Also,
as a matter of fact his Scotchness is chiefly m his
name. His father was a Canadian, and his mother
an Irishwoman, and Mac. himself was born at
Keilor, Vic., so what does that leave ?— Dudley
W.
Mr. Lachlan Beaton, just announced as a
commissioner to the Harbor Trust, is well known as
former manager of the business of Messrs. Wm.
Howard Smith and Sons, Ltd., the inter-state
steamship owners. He is the only commissioner
who has been brought into direct contact with the
shipping interest, and his appointment seems an
unusually sensible one. Mr. Beaton married a
daughter of the late W. B. Dailey, Q.C.
Melb. Herald, commemorating the anni-
versary of the Rensburg disaster where fell Major
Eddy and five other Victorians, says, “the day
should be memorable in her history, as that day
the great test of quality was first applied to
Victorian citizen soldiers.” Wrong ! It was at
Eureka the fighting qualities of Victorians were
first tested. The paper that maligns the dead
diggers of Ballarat by saying, even by implica-
tion, that they didn’t show fighting qualities is
only another proof that no good man knows what
strange animals will walk over his grave.
“Jack” Quick, the new Knight, might
fill a chapter in a new edition of Smiles’s “Self
Help.” He was battery-boy at one of the Bendigo
mines when he taught himself shorthand ; he
studied his Latin grammar while driving a horse
in a whim; and later on, when reporting for a
Bendigo paper, he was often found studying law-
books in the camp reserve after working in the
office till daybreak. In short, Quick is the in-
tellectual first cousin of the renowned grocer or
soap-boiler who starts life with twopence in his
pocket. If all mankind were Quicks how could
industry and perseverance earn a fair reward ?

WHEN A HUSBAND IS REALLY OLD.
HIM : “ There is not much difference between a
man of forty-jive and a woman of four-and-
twenty.”
HER : “No, but it is a big difference when a
woman is only forty and her husband is get-
ting on for seventy .”
John Forrest has retired from the Wes-
tralian Premiership, and George Throssell, an
affluent and very deaf flour-miller with an ear-
trumpet, takes his place. Throssell is a bitter
anti-Federalist, and a solid gentleman of great
age. He is alleged to be the best man in the old
Forrest Ministry, which may mean a good deal or
very little. To be a better man than Lefroy, for
instance, sounds like being as dead as Queen
Anne, but not quite so dead as Hengist and
Horsa.
Premier Holder’s luckless first opinion
that there was no real necessity for a lot of grown
men to dress themselves up in red skirts and false
hair and cat-skins, and formally tell the Adelaide
public what it knew already, that Edward VII
had become King, roused S’langdon Bonython’s
paper to language that would have been barely
justifiable had Holder slain his aged mother with
a gin-bottle. When a man gets a title he generally
feels called on to get excited over circus-flummery
of this sort. Heaven only knows why it should
be so, but it is.
That clever orator. John Langdon Parsons,
the Tory nominee who beat Labor - candidate
Robinson for S.A. Leg. Council, has been in S.A.
politics off-and-on for nearly 25 years. He
originally came from Maoriland as a Baptist
parson, then became an Adelaide merchant, and
subsequently reigned in Palmerston as Govt.
Resident. Although an out-and-out Tory, he is
related to that soi-disant Liberal organ, the
Advertiser, and Liberals sniggered when S’lang-
don Bonython’s sheet half-crowed over his vic-
tory.
Paragraph puffing of H. H. Champion as
a suitable editor for the Age must look ridiculous
to all thinking journalists. The clever Champion
has none of the wisdom essential to successful
editing of a big daily, or a small weekly, for that
matter. A deal of literary ability, and a lot of
enthusiasm for fads—but no tact, foresight, or
capacity for viewing things which ought to be
regarded only from the standpoint of solid fact.
With all the gospel of Liberalism on the tip of his
tongue, and with his adroit pen, and a cool, cheeky
faith in Champion, he has failed to persuade the
mob that they want him. The force of common
sense is lacking in H.H.
One Phillips, manager in Maoriland for
the Imperial Insurance Co., has been gaoled
for getting at the co.’s money by a rather
unusual process. He insured a non-existent pro-
perty for £4OO in the name of a non-existent
man ; then burned it down in his imagination, and
collected the money on the alleged other man’s
behalf. It was an incapable fraud in one respect,
for the Insurance and Banking Record pub-
lishes every month a list of the past month’s fires,
and the absence from the list of the building
which only blazed up in Mr. Phillips’s head was
calculated to attract attention. Further, when
the chief manager comes round on his tour of in-
spection, he often drifts about to look at the
burnt premises that the co. has had to pay for,
from tne same morbid instinct which makes a
certain kind of young man go on wet nights to
his best girl’s tomb —until he gets another best
girl.

N.S.W. Works Minister O’Sullivan has
put his office correspondence into heavy-
mourning—all his papers are issued with deep
black margins. The statement that he has pro-
hibited the use of red ink is, however, a libel.
Chief Secretary See has restrained himself to the
issuing of black-edged official note-paper, the
ordinary Departmental foolscap being unchanged.
The other N.S.W. Ministers have not yet made
any alterations in their correspondence.
“M.A.P.” has told at least one story of
the late Queen that looked true. On the retire-
ment of any servant who had been in her service
during her husband’s time she presented the old
identity with a piece of silver plate, and with the
gift was a black-edged card inscribed “ With best
wishes from the Queen and Prince Consort.” It
is "quite certain that H.M. didn't die “a good
Catholic ” as declared by some imaginative RC.
priests. But probably she died a good spiritualist.
Every mourning widow likes to remain on speak-
ing terms with the lost one.
“Billy” Williams, the elect of St. Kilda
in succession to George Turner, is the festive bar-
rister who once sang a “ comic ” song —two songs,
in fact—at Melb. Princess’s. The occasion was a
theatrical benefit matinee. The bill concluded
with a club scene—Purves, Q.C., took the chairl
and Williams figured along with sundry pros as a
singing member of the club. He warbled ‘ 1 My
Birthday ” in the style of the average Billy, and
was easily induced to respond to an encore. At
that time, January ’99, nobody thought of the
young man as a coming legislator, though a little
reflection might have shown them that the bar-
rister who could stand up and roar “My Birth-
day ” under such circumstances was quite likely to
get into the Parliament where Alick Peacock rose
to distinction on a “ bloomin’ larf.”
“ The sensation of the week,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent by last mail,
“has been Pall Mall Gazette Astor’s £5OOO
contribution to the Soldiers’ and Sailors’ Families
Fund, the pet ewe lamb of the Princess of Wales,
and thereby hangs a tale. For those who don’t
know : Capt. Sir Berkeley Milne, United Service
Club, a Royal Yacht guest, and one of His and
Her Royal Highnesses’ patrones, was enamored of
Miss Pauline Astor. Papa Astor wanted a
coronet, so Sir B. got cold shoulder. Big dinner,
Astor’s house—late Duke of Westminster’s. Sir
8., no invite. Miss P.A. : ‘Do come, I'll send
you one.’ Forgets to send, or anyhow doesn’t
send ; the Cap. nevertheless arrives and encounters
the irate multi-millionaire who courteously
reminds him that he was not invited. The Cap.,
chivalrously silent, bowed out. Then this ex-
American-Englishman hies himself to the Pall
Mall Gazette, which he owns, and insists on
par. being inserted : ‘ Cap. Sir B. M., of the U.
Service Club, attended dinner last night at Mr.
Astor’s house without invitation.’ Editor Douglas
Straight nearly wept, but in it had to go—and out
of Society fell William Waldorf Astor in one act.
Now he would buy his way back, but Albert Ed-
ward is staunch to his friends and never forgives.
W.W.A. had better get back to the U.S.. and be
de-naturalized quick. Positive Ass, comparative
Astor.”

Aboriginalities.
“Z." : It knows how to be hot here (Mt.
Drysdale, N.S.W.) at times. During last week’s
hot weather, an ordinary beer-glass, centaining
cool water, placed on the counter of a local pub.,
was heard to crack; and on inspection a ring of
glass, about half-an-inch wide, was found snapped
clean off the top, level with surface of the liquid.
The thermometer registered somewhere over 110
deg. at the time. Local scientists pronounce it a
case of unequal expansion of the two sections of
the glass, while beer-soakers are firm upon the
theory that the break-up was caused by the shock
of unusual contents. Please decide.

Queensland papers recently teem with
such items as “ Death in the bush. Verdict, heat
apoplexy.” “ Found dead 10 miles from Jundah
a swagman. Supposed to have died of thirst.”
“Another case of exhaustion. Too long absti-
nence from food and water,” etc., etc. No won-
der they rush off to S’Africa. Why, the Trans-
vaal business (with “ five'bob a day and found )
is a mere picnic to them !
* * * *
Re drought phenomena. Mobs of turtles
have been noticed of late in Moreton district (Q.)
leaving the creeks and making their way to the
Bremer. In some of the N.W. towns the horse
appears to be usurping the functions of the goat,
sundry old stagers tackling the straw and other
debris of back yards. The spectacle of sheep
chewing wool is by no means uncommon.
* * * *
A selector (one Birret), in the Beech
Forest (Vic.), while cutting firewood noticed a
tree falling towards him. In his eagerness to
escape he got his right foot caught in a prong of a
short stump. He made a desperate lurch to get
away, but his foot remained firmly fixed, and he
fell. The falling tree cleared him by several feet,
but when picked up he was found to be terribly
injured. Through the unfortunate man’s foot
being caught in the crevice it was almost torn off
by the weight of his body ; several ribs were
broken, and his lungs were injured. As he was
too ill to be carried 26 miles to the nearest town,
a surgeon went out and amputated the foot.
* * * *
The tragedy of Paddy Madden, of N.
Queensland. Madden went out, a few days ago,
with a black boy, to look for horses. The search
was for a while unsuccessful, and the boy was sent
back. Nearly a week after, poor Paddy’s body
was found under a tree by a search-party. A
tourniquet was round the thigh ; the leg had been
lacerated to cause a flow of blood. Evidently a
snake-bite. A remarkable feature of the case was
that Madden, when under the influence of the
poison, had commenced to walk round and round
a tree, and had kept on until a regular circular
track had been formed. Is there any record of a
similar happening 1
THEY BOTH LIVED ON IT.
MAGISTRATE : “Sorry to see you again.
What brings you here?”
PRISONER: “Dhrink, yer Anner. Same thing
that brings you here.”
MAGIDTRATE : “ You insulting vagabond ! I’ll —”
PRISONER: “ Hold on, yer Anner. I didn’t mean
it was the dhrink that was in you that brought
jou here. I meant that it’s the dhrink that’s
in me that brings you here.”
When Missionary Baker was killed by a
hill tribe in ’6B, the body was eaten in accordance
with custom. Some of the cannibals told a white
settler a couple of years later that they thought
the unfortunate gentleman’s top-boots were part
of his legs; also that they had to cook the legs
five different ways before they could masticate the
outer skin or leather.

News artist, illustrating a story by Walter
Besant which forms the summer number of that
paper, has a group of men in the N.S.W. back
blocks, boiling tea in an iron cauldron, shaped like
a gold-fish jar. Forestier’s colleague on the
1.L.N., S. Begg—an old Bulletin man, by the
way, who once had a share in this paper—would
have used a billy.
Williams, the eminent missionary, was
eaten by the Erromarigans. His bones were
recovered and buried in Samoa. Bishop
Patteson’s body was not interfered with. It
was found carefully sewn in sacking and
placed in a canoe. A frond had been
stuck • in his vest, knotted four times.
This showed that his death was in
revenge for four natives being carried
off by a “ thief-vessel.”

In the early Clarence River (N.S.W.)days
a bridal party of four took boat to the nearest
town—30 miles away. En route the prin-
cipals disagreed so severely that ere they
reached their destination the bridegroom
had withdrawn in favor of the “best man,”
and not long afterwards married the intend-
ing bridesmaid. Perfect amity subsequently
existed between the two families, who were
established within coo-ee of each other.

his pit-wallaby-traps every Saturday night lest
the marsupials might desecrate the Sabbath by
falling in on that day.
“ Dan ” : What “ Burragorang ” says
26/l/’l) about the Germans of South Q. working
their women is true enough, but I can’t see that
it calls for special mention when in that same
quarter British wives and Australian girls —com-
pelled by circumstances, of course —are any day
to be found ploughing, wood-cutting, breaking-in,

* * * *
The grandfather of a prominent Austra-
lian solicitor was, years ago, literally “ potted ” by
the blacks on the West Coast of S.A., where lie
and his sons had a sheep-run. The sons were
away once for a few days, and on their return
could find no trace of their father, until after a
long search they discovered his body in a huge
boiling-down vat. A couple of gins gave infor-
mation, and when the sons reported the matter
they got permission to punish the blacks. They
rounded some 60 of them to tine cliffs on the sea-
shore (there 60ft. to 100 ft. high), shot some, and
drove others over the edge, to be dashed to death
on the rocks below.

“P.L.”: Re “ Burragoraug’s ” remarks
on the German colonists of S. Queensland :
The charge that Hans Schneider works his women is
.perfectly true. Writer has seen German girls hoeing
corn, felling timber, and riding (cross-legged on a bare-
backed steed) after cattle in several districts of Southern
Queensland. But the statement that he makes his chil-
dren speak German only when shopping is decidedly in-
correct. At home the use of the Teutonic tongue is de-
cidedly (and often forcibly) encouraged ; but when the
youngsters are abroad in the town they invariably speak
English, and the parents are rather proud of their chil-
dren’s bi-lingual facility. The fact that merchants in
Ipswich and Toowoomba employ German-speaking em-
ployes is explained by the necessity of having men fami-
liar with the tongue of the Fatherland, for the purpose of so-
liciting orders in the country-—inasmuch as great numbers
of the S.Q. Teutonic farmers hardly ever come near a
town. . . As to “ Burragorang’s ” statement that the
Frauenfelders and Schneiders don’t consume their own
wine but “ sell it to the Smiths and Murphys,” the asser-
tion is a piece of pure romance. The Germans of Too-
woomba district (Middle Ridge in particular) are great
wine-drinkers, as are many of those settled in the Rose-
wood. But the wine they drink is not the same sort of
stuff they sell to the bibulous Smiths and the genial
Murphys. The Celt and the Anglo-Saxon won’t buy
Schneider’s wine unless it be first heavily fortified and
Hans is not a teetotal organisation. “ Politically,” we
are told, “ the German is all-powerful in S.Q.” Pure non-
sense ! There are not more than half-a-dozen electorates
in S.Q. where the German vote is really of any weight,
and these are chiefly in the neighbourhood of Ipswich
and Toowoomba.

A study in etymology. “ No, Peter Swan
is not my real name. I come from Sweden, and my
name is Sven Petersen. But beoples can’t say
‘ Sven ’ ; dey say ‘ Swan ’ and call me ‘ Swan
Peterson.’ Den dey turn him round and say
‘ Peter Swan.’ So afterwards I call myself Peter
Swan; I write my name Peter Swan when I
marry; and now —no matter, is it ? lam Peter
Swan.” And, by the way, his name amongst his
mates is “Peter the Goose.”
“ Alick” discourses on (let us hope a very
exceptional) bush-dance: —
Went to a bush-dance t’other night. Mob arrived in
carts, buggies, or on horseback —racing, hooting, blowing
horns and challenging each other to mortal combat.
Danced in large barn. Cask of beer in out-house at-
tracted most of the “ gents ” and a few women-folk after
each dance. Buggies with edibles in them had savage
cattle-dogs chained to the wheels for the benefit of would-
be purloiners. String of damsels pass through the door-
way ; yokel youths on either side put out their feet to
trip them. Music —concertina and alleged violin. Con-
certina-man seated on chaff-bag in corner, clay pipe in
mouth, pants pulled-up to knees, exposing unwashed
legs. Nondancers perched on rafters overhead,
spitting impartially down. Loud voice of irate
female—“ Good Gord !—y’ clumsy hound, y’uv tore me
blinded skirt t’ rags ! ” M.C. could only dance the waltz
and took no interest in any other dance. Persistently
called upon the “ gints ” to “ Shooze yer pardners fer a
worlse,” till at last a disgusted citizen —prompted by the
red-haired girl upon his knee—loudly requested M.C. to
“ ’Av a bit of ‘ common,’ blarst yer ! ” M.C. promptly
stoushed protestor, who welted him on the jaw in turn.
Then they went at it, while the women screeched and the
men appiauded, until, after much ruby had flowed, a
trooper came along and parted the warriors. The floor
was splashed with water and swept; but one clot of
gore, having escaped notice, overthrew a huge, half-
drunken barbarian who was stalking down the room with a
plate of “ samages” for his grinning “ clinah.” Had to
be assisted from the room with a dislocated shoulder.
Then the concertina blared, the violin screeched, and the
revel was resumed.
* * * *
“ Dyamba ” re Sunday-work at Victorian
butter-factories :
There is a growing agitation in Victoria for the aboli-
tion of Sunday-work at butter-factories. At present nine-
tenths of the factories work seven days a week, and, put-
ting aside the question of wages, it has been found that
this constant grind wears down the employes more than
would much harder work for six days a week. The cry
of the dairyman is that Sunday work is absolutely neces-
sary, but the fact that some of the biggest factories in the
State do without it is a conclusive reply to this. The
Koroit factory, near Warrnambool, has had no Sunday
work since its inception, and is one of the most prosper-
ous factories in the district. It takes the Saturday even-
ing’s milk in the same night, and then shuts up all Sun-
day. All that is necessary is for the farmer to attend to
his Sunday morning’s milk carefully, and even this only
for the two hot months of the year. At present in the
neighborhood of a factory there is the same roar and tear
of factory carts on a Sunday as on a week day. By the
way, what has become of that famous Act of Charles 11.,
under which a teamster was, some time ago, heavily fined
for Sunday travelling ?

“Y.” : He “P.H.C.’s” snake-yam
(8., 19/1/’01). In Borneo once I saw a tea cup
perambulating my bedroom floor. Investigation
showed that a snake had swallowed an egg and
then crawled through the handle of a tea-cup,
after which the reptile had more eggs —with con-
sequent inability to either advance or retreat.
The whole show was duly preserved in spirits,
and, for all I know, may still be in Sandakan
Museum.

A Printer’s Misfortune.
The particulars of a serious misfortune which
overtook Mr. W. Filmer, a printer residing at
Abbott-street, West Maitland (N.S.W.), have
been gathered by a reporter.
' Mr Filmer’s long and honorable connection
with the Maitland MekcUKY has caused him to be
well known and respected throughout Maitland,
where great sympathy has been expressed tor him.
It appears that some time ago he fell away to a
mere skeleton, the result of diarrhoea, which
became chronic. He was very often seized wi
fainting fits, due to weakness, which the diarrhoea
caused, his food gave him little strength, and
despite the best of treatment he did not recover.
A friend who had been cured by Dr. Williams
pink pills, recommended them. One box benefited
Mr. Filmer greatly, three more boxes cured him.
He has increased thirteen pounds in weight in
nine months, and he feels better, brighter and
stronger than he has for thirty years.
Mr. Filmer will be pleased to verify these
statements, which were made before Mr.,J.
Thompson, J.P., should anyone write to him.

“Alpar ” : Once from a
window watched an iguana
stalking a chicken. The
reptile approached the
chick from the cover
of a geranium bush.
Something startled
the poultry, and
the “ ’go.” backed
under tne bush, but
in a couple of min-
utes came stealing
out again. When
within about 4ft.
of the prey he
opened his jaws
and went forward
with a rush. The
chicken fluttered
and fell in. I
just got out in
time to see the
tail of the captor'
disappearing in
the spout of a big
eucalyptus.
Dear Bulletin. —
Re “P.L.L.’s” cow-
skeletons in trees (B.
19/1/’01). Near Sigi-
lind River, in British
Borneo, in ’9O, I saw
perched in the fork of a tree,
a mile from the nearest navig-
able water, an 80ft. steam-
launch, which had been de-
posited there —40ft. from
nigh water mark —during
the flood of a previous
year.— Yang Antut.

“ Alpar ” : Re luck of drunks. One
morning, on coming out of our humpy in the
cutting, I spotted a drunk asleep near the brink,
50ft. up. I called my mate and we were just
starting up to remove him when he gave a lurch
and rolled down to the door. We ran to raise
him when he looked up and asked : “ Say, mate,
did yer see anything of my dorg ? ” After he had
some tea he danced a hornpipe for us and shuffled
off to his camp three miles away—but next night
he died of bladder-injury.
* * * *
•‘‘0.0.”: Your snake-climbing competi-
tion recalls an incident illustrative of Young
Australia’s keen sense of humor. A ringbarker
at work upon a sapling paused to enquire why his
eight-year-old son shrieked with laughter every
time he drove his axe into the tree. “It’s that
black snake up in the fork,” replied the youngster
grinning. “Every time you shake the tree lie
reaches down and makes a lick at your ear.”
“ Well, you’ve got a shocking fine idea of a joke,
retorted the indignant parent, and shortening the
snake by a head he used the remainder as a strap
upon that portion of his heir’s anatomy where it
would do most good.
ETIQUETTE.
TRAMP (to cookie leaning over selection fence and
just about to strike a match to light his
pipe): “After you with the match, mate.”
COOKIE: “I should think so — my blanky match,
too! ”
A new-chum on the Wosbralian goldfields
was told that the condensed water was bad for
strangers, so he slaked his thirst with eight or 10
pints of “shypoo,” which, after Melbourne beer,
tasted like a mixture of cowheel, hops, and soap-
suds. Being still thirsty when he got to his
room he drank half a glass of water with much
misgiving. In the morning he awoke with a taste
in his mouth as if he had dined on horse-blanket
fried in axle-grease, and as he rushed to
the pub. for a pint he said to himself with the
solemn conviction of a man who has made a great
discovery, “ By cripes, no more blanky water for
me ! ”

A gruesome bush episode. Recently at
Edendale (M.L.) three rabbiters were poisoned by
strychnine. While they were writhing in agony
the shadow of a casual friend fell across the door
of the hut, and the casual friend looked in. ±lis
first idea was that the three contorted wretches
were rehearsing for an amateur nigger -minstrel
entertainment, and he shrieked with laughter and
said it was a very good show. Barely do the
horrible and the humorous meet like this.
“ Widgeon ” : “ Wat Tell’s ” horse (B.
20/10/’00) wasn’t in it with one I sa,w on a
Queensland station—which used regularly to
“scoff” any soap the cook happened to leave
outside. It was a long time before he was
caught in the act and suspicion thus removed
from a black-boy who was supposed to take the
soap to annoy the cook.
•»» * *
A record in the way of farm slavery comes
from Taranaki (M.L.). Last season a Plains
resident and wife milked 79 cows twice daily
besides getting through ordinary farm work.
They didn’t spend a cent in
wages during twelve months.
An old loafer entered a
bar and asked the girl for
draught whisky, but when the
bottle was put down he sniffed
at it disapprovingly and de-
manded brandy. This, how-
ever, he also rejected, saying
that they kept a very poor class
of spirits, and would she give
him a long beer. This he got,
drank it, and was marching out
when the girl hailed him :
“Hey, you haven’t paid me
for that beer?” “No,” was
the reply, “but I gave you
the whisky back for it.” ‘ 1
know, but the whisky belonged
tome.” “Well, didn’t 1 give
you the brandy for it ? ” But
you never paid for the brandy. ’
“ I know I didn’t, ’cos I didn’t
drink any.” And while the
girl was trying to drag the
knots off this problem, he
calmly “ gat.”

Recently said the eminent Irish as
tronomer Ball: — . .
The sun was getting smaller every day. It was shrink-
ing daily at the rate of nine inches, and this shrinking
had been going on from time immemonal. In twenty
years the sun would shrink a mile. At the beginning ot
the centurv it was five miles wider than it was to-day,
and at the beginning of the Christian era it was 100 miles
wider Perhaps some of his young hearers mg it >e
alarmed at tins shrinking process, but he could assure
them that the sun was big en °ug h to hear a k.t of shrink-
ing The diameter of the sun was 860,000 miles , w,uuu
years hence it would have lost 2000 miles-but it would
not be a bad sun even then.

A TARIFF CATECHISM.
*Q. What rathe last argument urged why the
farmers of'Victoria should support a High Tariff,
levied upon their tools of trade, their clothing,
and all they buy at the store ?
A. It is an assertion that under Federal oondi«
cions the duty on wheat may become operative
again.
Q. —Has that doty been pperative in Victoria
before ?
A.—Once, in 20 years.
Q. When was that ?
A.—When, in consequence of a deplorable
failure of the crops, most of the farmers had little
or no wheat to sell.
Q.—ls that event likely to occur again either in
Victoria or in the Federation ?
A.—As far as human foresight can perceive oer»
tainly oot. Abous.

II I 11 MELBOURNE HAPPY AGAIN.
Melbourne, Thursday.—This morning the Premier received
ihe following cable from the Agent-General : —“ I have the
i authority of the Secretary of State for the Colonies for the state-
ment that Melbourne will be the first Australian port of call of
His Royal Highness.”

want any more ! ”

Melbourne Age, in a recent leader on the
literary tendencies of our time, O Lord, gives
what looks very like a proof of mental incom-
petence that would appear indecent in the
columns of the Neddwaddy Bugle. Lamenting
the weakness of the literary output the writer
wails: “Tennyson, so far as we can see, left no
successor ; and Carlyle, Hallam and Froude have
been followed by nobody on whom the historian’s
mantle can rest. Dickens and Thackeray are not
replaced bv the Hardys, the Wards, the
Merediths and the. Corellis.” The critic who
lumps Hardy and Meredith with Mrs. Ward and
Marie Corelli lias narrowly missed plumbing the
abyss by the exclusion of Hall Caine.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE,
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals,- Sapphires; Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find- it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
The latest attack on the British Army
system in S’Africa comes from no less person than
General Colvile. General Buller has condemned
the lack of intelligence and independent action on
the part of the junior officers and the rank and
file, but Colvile hits out with both hands and
all his feet at the sacred Headquarters-staff itself.
In May last, Colvile was instructed to reach
Heilbron via Lindley with 4000 Highlanders and
1000 Scouts on the 29th of the month. Particular
stress was laid on the necessity to be at his desti-
nation by the 29th, and the General immediately
came to the conclusion that his brigade was to
take part in a general advance to the Yaal river
on or about that date. The Imperial Yeomanry,
or rather a portion of them, were to join him en
route, but after starting he received word
that they were so late that they would be
unable to overtake him, and would join
him later on via Kroonstad.- The roads were
bad, the country thick with Boers, and Colvile’s
troops, worn out with constant fighting, arrived a
day late at Lindley, to find that town, much to
their surprise, strongly held by the Boers. They
had been lookiug forward to pleasant welcome, but
instead had to fight a pitched battle before they
could enter. Next morning they went on, and
when within one day’s march of Heilbron, the
force was overtaken by an orderly with a message
from the Colonel in command of 500 of the Yeo-
manry, that he was blocked outside Lindley,
and had been compelled to retreat three
miles on the Kroonstad-road, had only
one day’s provisions, and that he needed
assistance to get away without heavy loss. Col-
vile replied that he had imperative instructions
to be at Heilbron on 29th, and had only one day’s
provisions left himself, and that not only would
it make him late at Heilbron to come to the Yeo-
manry’s rescue, but make it possible for the Boers
to starve the 5000 men into surrender. Also, he
said that as the Yeomanry had retired three miles
they could no doubt make good their escape with
less loss and danger than if his brigade re-
turned to their assistance. The Yeomanry
were captured, and although Colvile reached
Heilbron on the date instructed, and drove
the Boers out of the town, and generally
did good work, a loud demand went up for his
blood for having abandoned the Yeomanry. The
General was sent home and requested to resign
his position in the army, but has refused to do so.
He says that the whole trouble is that the Yeo-
manry — De Wet’s Own were all “society”
soldiers, many of them millionaires, and, had they
been a portion of a common line-regiment, he
would probably have been court-martialled had he
gone to their assistance in defiance of his orders.
He did not believe in social distinctions on
the battle-field, and considered that the safety of
4000 Highlanders and 1000 Scouts was much more
important than that of 500 amateur soldiers. When
Colvile got to Heilbron, he saw some more of
the Headquarters-staff’s fine work. He had been
officially informed that he would find Heilbron in
occupation of Gen. Hamilton, and full of supplies.
Instead of which he found no supplies and only
120 sick and wounded troops living on the charity
of the inhabitants; but he received information
that a convoy of 60 waggons, guarded by 90 men,
would be dispatched shortly. He replied promptly
that the country was infested with Boers, that
90 men were not sufficient guard, and that a force
of cavalry with guns was absolutely necessary.
His advice was unheeded, and 4000 Boers
had a good time on the contents of those
60 waggons, while Colvile’s troops went short.
He wired for instructions, and received from
the Military Secretary orders that he should
remain where he was. Some days later he re
ceived another message from the Military Secre-
tary that he was to take no notice of the former
wire, but act on one sent by the Cliief-of-Staff
same day. The Chief-of-Staff’s wire never came
to hand till six months later, when it was handed
to him with a lot of undelivered telegrams, and
the brigade just fooled about Heilbron and ate its
head off, and was finally taken to Pretoria after
all the fighting was over. Now they want to sack
Colvile because of all these things. Colvile says,
“ Sack the man who was responsible for the
bungle, and not the victim of it.” And it looks as
though Colvile was right.

SYDNEY.


Chairman Hickson, of the new Sydney
Harbour 'trust, is one of the best-known N.S.W.
civil servants, being the Under-Secretary for
Public Works and Commissioner for Roads. He
is also chairman of the Public Service Tender
Board, and at one time was a favorite tip for a
seat on the Public Service Board But few officers
of the State enjoy a salary of £2,000 a year, and
fewer still reach to such a position through the
Civil Service. Promotion for Mr. Portus, general
secretary to the Commonwealth shivoo, and
already a high official in the Works Department,
is predicted as a consequence of Mr. Hickson’s
step up.
Verb. sap. Christchurch (M.L.) Press
London correspondent discourses on Jingo grati-
tude and Jingo patriotism :
London, which was able to give its Volunteers a cham-
pagne supper, and generally roared itself hoarse over
them, now raises it hands in deprecating impotence when
asked to find them work wherewith to earn an honest
living. The gilt is off the gingerbread, and the City’s
regiment is left, in many cases, to tramp the streets on
that most heart-sickening of all quests—the quest for
work. Since the end of October the sight of the C.l.V.’s
tramping the streets has been common. They have in
many cases been able to find nothing else to do. So great
did the distress become amongst them that the Daily
Express lately opened an employment bureau, for the
purpose of bringing the City Imperial Vagrants in touch
with employers. Some of the many C.l.V.’s who have
applied seem to have been at the very end of their re-
sources—thankful for the gift of half-a-crown to get a
meal.

POLITICAL
POINTS
It is commonly reported that the Com-
monwealth celebration bills presented at Sydney
Treasury had, two weeks ago, reached £150,000,
and they were still dropping in gaily.
President Zeal on reform of Yic. House of
Landlords declares that he “will take care there
*s no reductio ad absurdum That reduction was
made by the Council itself years ago.
Solomon, Opposition leader, is, at time of
writing, the only S.A. politician who has spoken
as a Federal candidate. All the others seem to
be lying low, particularly men like Symon, Baker
and Holder, who are pronounced Freetraders.
Parsons’ election to S.A. Leg. Council
means that the Tories will again have a majority
of one in that Chamber. It was only Kingston’s
vote last session which secured Early-closing— a
boon for which the workers had striven for five
yerrs.
Doctor Ahearne. of Townsville, Q.. is a
candidate for the Fectc.H Senate. The Bulletin
appreciates his capacity ; and enjoins every elector
to vote him down because he is a root-and-branch
black-labor advocate, and therefore an enemy of
white Australia.
S.A. Labor Party has curtailed its list of
Federal candidates, and now only intends to run
M Gregor and Kirkpatrick for the Senate and
Price and Batchelor (Minister of Education) for
. ' ® L°wmr House. All four politicians are now
in S.A. Legislature.
It has been definitely ascertained that the
Colonial authorities in Downing-street, London
wall not consent to local Governors. Her late
Majesty imposed a personal veto on the proposal
it is said. It was her last remaining prerogative
appointing Colonial Governors.
Brisbane Worker recently remarked re
Philp s utterance that an extension of the fran-
chise would soon have to be introduced : ‘ ‘ Federa-
tion is stirring things up a bit.” According to the
Worker s utterances some short time ago, Federa-
tion was to be abundant parent of every political
evil. Well —times change!
Kingston is doing his best to work up
enough strong Protectionist candidates for the S.A.
federal seats to render it at least certain that
Freetraders like Holder, Glynn, Baker and Solo-
mon (he, too, pretends to be a Reidite) shall
not have matters all their own way. The first
three are pretty sure to be returned ; but so they
would be if they professed Protectionist views.
EXPLAINED.
ANANIAS (joining the staff of a Sydney morning
paper): “Hullo, Noah! Fancy meeting
you! ”
NOAH: “ Yes — I do the ‘ Humor’ column.”
It is now quite plain that £100,000,000
(one hundred millions sterling) will not nearly
cover the cost to Britain of the Transvaal war.
“The price of subjugating my country will
stagger humanity,” said Kruger. The price to
date includes not only all those scores of millions,
but tens of thousands of the best lives under the
Union Jack, also possibly the life of the venerable
Queen.
It is reckoned that the N.S.W. railways
and tramways, for the second half of 1900, yielded
£Bl,OOO more revenue than for the second half of
1899. At that rate they are probably returning a
real profit at last, and this is a most convenient
time to enact that all railway profits, instead of
being swallowed up in the general revenue, shall
be used to pay off the railway debt. Will the
Cornstalk Government kindly lend an ear ?
An interesting Federal Parliament contest
will be that for Korangamite electorate (Vic.) be-
tween Jack Murray, the truly democratic member
for Warrnambool, and the Camperdown squatter,
of polo fame, J. C. Manifold. J.C.M., by the way,
has seceded from the local National Ass, whereat
there is much wailing in the squatter ranks. He
has developed Protectionist views, and follows
Barton with one exception—female suffrage.
S.M. Herald for the first time for many
years gives notices of John Norton’s meetings—
m his Senatorial campaign. This because Nor-
ton is a bitter anti-Bartonite. One argument of
Norton’s is that Barton means to raise an eight-
million customs tariff, of which N.S.W. .must
gay a large share. How Barton can run the
ommonwealth any other way and whom he
(Norton) would select to run it, John does not
explain.
Amazing admission from the Brisbane
Coubier :—■
We place the sugar industry in front and say that as
soon as it can stand alone ivith the help of white labor the
Kanaka must go.
But the Coubier and Co. won’t give the sugar in-
dustry a chance to “ stand alone with the help of
white labor.” They devote all their energies to
keeping white labor clean away from the industry,
and then exclaim : “Sugar with white labor —an
impossibility ? ”
When Gr. H. Reid visited Adelaide to talk
Freetrade, Premier Holder, who, though he pro-
fesses fervent Freetrade, was then looking for
office in the Barton Cabinet, carefully absented
himself from the meeting, although he was in the
city the night before. Holder, however, shed the
light of his countenance on Barton last week and
appeared thoughtful over the Protectionist en-
thusiasm manifested by the audience. Perhaps
conversion may come to him again. Holder is a
most susceptible man.
The Federal elections are now only a few
weeks off, and both parties seem wholly unpre-
pared. In N.S.W. the situation is apparently a
nght Sydney v. Country. The Foreign-trade
party has swooped down on the metropolitan area
and all its big guns are fighting for the Sydney
vote. Reid, M'Millan, Bruce Smith, Joe Cook,
Carruthers, Millen, Jawbone Neild, E. M. Clark,
and Dugald Thomson are all nominated for city
and suburban seats, leaving the country prac-
tically alone. Not one man of note has been put
forward by the Govt, side for one of these nine
seats. Barton seems to be relying on the country
districts ; but, if only for the look of the thing,
should make a bold show in at least two or three
of the city constituencies.
N.S.W. Freetrade Association has en-
dorsed the candidature of Labor member Thomas
Brown, as against B. R. Wise, for Canoblas.
Wise could not have expected to be consulted as to
who should be selected to oppose him, but
he is wrathful at the action of the Eree-
trade Association in chosing Brown, of
whom nobody, or hardly anybody, ever
heard before. This Brown is one of the
weakest of the “Solidarities” in N.S.W.
Assembly, and the Freetraders run him,
not because they want him elected, but
because they detest Wise. Brown, in the
event of his success, will be pledged to
vote with the labor caucus, and as he is
not the stamp of man to sway the caucus,
his election won’t do much good to the
Ereetrade party. But to defeat Wise !
Dear Bulletin. —Touching that
list of candidates for the Senate from
N.S.W. The long-promised additions
of the names of men in whom the State
could have a little confidence is very
slow in coming, and if these are not
soon announced the squalid, multy crowd,
already with a long start, will take much
catching. In the event of a scarcity of
candidates of respectable political weight,
how would it be for the big rival associa-
tions to each nominate three candidates
in a bunch of six and run them against
the field ? Dick O’Connor and E. D.
Millen for instance. Otherwise some push-
ing, well-advertised nonentity such as Eden
George, or some gilded Incapable like In-
dependent Terry, from Ryde, may sneak
past the electors. Heaven forbid that
N.S.W. should be misrepresented, in wbat
should really be the Ur per House, by a set
of riff-raff. —A.M.
The new appointments (Cribb and Leahy)
to Q. Cabinet make it more Tory than ever.
Both are ardent friends of the syndicator, coddlers
of every variety of alien, squattophiles, and bitter
foes to every form of Democratic progress.
Leahy is a dour, determined, caustic puller of
wires ; Cribb—halting of speech, muddled of in-
tellect, and Ipswichian to the backbone. The
present Queensland Cabinet cannot be called a
A CROOKED POLITICIAN WANTS TO
HEAR OF SOMETHING STRAIGHT.
“Who ever heard,” asked G. H. Reid, “of a
straight up-and-down Protectionist now?”
Cabinet of little men ; it is simply a conglomera-
tion of political shrimps.
Melbourne’s ex-Mayor M‘Eacharn has
now recognised from a platform that he has “a
losing battle to fight,” against Radical Dr.
Maloney for the Melbourne seat in the Federal
House of Representatives. Apart from compara-
tive personal unpopularity the knight is in, a tight
place as to policy. If he runs as a Bartonite the
Freetraders will put up a candidate and split his
vote, and if he runs as a Reidite the Age will
“stoush” him. So poor Smalkum sits on a
barbed-wire fence in his kilts.
Westralia has spent more money than it
likes to think about in making a harbour at Fre-
mantle, and is pledged to spend a further hair-
raising sum before the work is finished. And now
the Forrest Govt, purposes to deepen the Swan
River so that vessels can come right up to Perth,
whereby a good deal of the money spent on Fre-
mantle will be rendered wholly superfluous. Even
riotous Westralia feels something with cold feet
walking up and down its spine as it contemplates
the probable cost of this new inspiration.
Free newspaper postage is foreshadowed
as the policy of the Barton Cabinet. The Sydney
papers that benefit most by free postage at pre-
sent are the Sydney dailies, three of which are
fighting against Barton and his Ministers on the
fiscal issue, so it may be long ere so good a chance
of insisting on postage-upon-newspapers again
occurs. By advocating it Barton cannot make the
Sydney press more bitterly antagonistic to him,
and he would secure the enthusiastic support of
the whole of the country press through the Com-
monwealth.
A London paper tells indignantly how
Gov. Lamington, during his Carpentarian pere-
grinations, visited Burketown and found 25
Chinese in the local gaol on a charge of evading
poll-tax. If the London journal only knew it.
the Q. Govt., under the circumstances, is more to
be pitied than the Celestials. The latter get free
board and lodging for some weeks, and are then
either slowly transported whence they came (if
this be possible) or silently kicked out into the
stilly night when the authorities are sick of sup-
porting them. Whoever loses by the deal, John
doesn’t.
N.S.W. Defence Minister See is in a diffi-
culty over the pay of the returned contingenters.
These doughty warriors signed-on under the
belief that they were to receive 4s. fid. a day, plus
Is. 2d. Imperial pay. The local Defence office
maintains that the local pay was only to make up
the difference between Is. 2d. and 4s. 6d. But
the returned heroes, finding the 17th, or whatever
it is, contingent going out at ss. a day, don’t see
the force of receiving only 4s. fid. when they were
taking part in the more anxious part of the war—
and they are kicking hard.
The “ lion in the path ” of the Sydney
Wharves Resumption Board appointments is said
to be Varney Parkes, who was certain of being
offered the chairmanship when he resigned his
seat for Canterbury in N.S.W. Assembly, six
months ago. The Labor-party chose to infer that
he resigned in expectation of getting the appoint-
ment, and so it has been fighting him ever since.
Lyne now wants to appoint George M'Credie,
late boss bubonic rat-catcher, to the chairmanship.
Mr. M'Credie, who has a large practice as a con-
sulting-engineer, will not take a plain commis-
sionership. Mr. Parkes also aspires to the chair-
manship—and there you are.
Re Arthur Lawley, the new Governor of
Westralia, ah official opinion goes the rounds in
N.S.W. that lieutenant-governors of that State
will, for some years to come, be men of army rank
who distinguished themselves in the Boer war.
Knight Lyne can say something of a suggestion
in that line made to him. Lyne and others have
made the Imperial authorities believe that Aus-
tralia will fall on the neck of anybody who has
killed a job lot of Boers, and consequently as
occasion arises the valorous blood-letters will be
drafted to one or another official position in Aus-
tralia. This was the. bad practice of the authori-
ties in the early Botany Bay days. If S’African
butchers be sent to Australia as Governors, the
authorities will simply court danger.
Apropos of the demand that Australia
should give special tariff concessions to British
goods because Britain is the only country
which lets its goods in free, it may be
noted that wool represents nearly two-thirds of
the Commonwealth’s foreign exports, and France,
Germany, Italy and Belgium admit our wool just
as free of duty as Britain does. They do the same
with hides and bones, and France, Italy and
Belgium also admit tallow free. Further, in the
matter of Australian wine and brandy, some of
these States levy less duty than Britain does !
France, Italy, Germany and Belgium treat
Australia almost as well in practice—though not,
>erhaps, in theory—as Britain does, and there is
o special balance of gratitude due to anybody in
the matter.
Reid, the great promiser, at Corowa . “If
I were Prime Minister of the Commonwealth I
think I would be prepared within a day, or at any
rate within 24 hours, to give some expression of
opinion on a matter of such importance to the
people. This anent Barton’s alleged delay in
propounding a Federal scheme of Defence. Reid
was ever thus. He was always prepared to give
an expression of opinion on any subject under the
sun or in the ether beyond it, even as to how to
reduce his own front. He gave N.S.W. stacks of
opinions as to how to abolish the unemployed in
one month, as to how to create a system of local
government, how to provide for old-age pensions,
and how to run the finances on a strictly cash
basis. But he never got beyond the expression
of opinion in any of these things. What is wanted
is a man with less ability to give expressions of
opinion, but who can show a considerabl deal
more expressions of practice.
Progress, the stationary Brisbane paper
owned by Federal P.M.G. Drake, struggles hard
to whitewash its owner for leaving his party and
selling himself for a portfolio to Philp’s Tory
Ministry :
I s . apparently under the impression that
the Philp Administration is in no way different from
some of those that preceded it. Now the Ministerial
programme last session contained the following measures
—Early Closing Bill, One Man One' Vote, &c. . . . Was
it inconsistent for a democratic politician to join a
Government with such an advanced radical programme ?
If the Queensland Labor Party placed measures before
men they would give a whole-hearted support to Mr.
Philp.
The Bulletin at all events doesn’t share the
Worker’s gross mistake. The Philp Government
isn’t like some of its predecessors. There is a
blessed gift of hypocrisy and snuffle about it
which makes it very different. Only a very
different kind of Government would have
struck the idea of putting One-Man-One-Vote at
the front of a fine-looking democratic programme,
and then devoting almost the whole session to
shoving through a purely Tory scheme of disas-
trous syndicate railways which will further enrich
monopolists and rob the Commonwealth. There
is something rich and rare in the gall of Drake’s
paper, which claims that the Labour party should
have voted, contrary to all its principles, in sup-
port of Philp’s great railway swindlecat pro-
gramme, because the Philp Government had
promised One-Man-One-Vote and had broken its
promise.
The defeat of Labor-man Robinson by
Parsons, the Tory nominee, for Central District
of b.A. Leg. Council was due mainly to the un-
popularity of Early Closing with suburban shop-
keepers ; but had Kingston been available to
vitalise the campaign Robinson might have been
elected. Apropos, the Laborites are much dis-
gusted at the apathy of Holder and Co., none of
whom uttered a word in support of the democratic
candidate, and, as a result, the Leg. Council re-
ceives a member pledged to vote against almost
every item of the Liberal programme.

Every democrat should have a copy of The
Bulletin pamphlet, “A Policy for the Common-
wealth,” now receiving vicious notices from the
Calico press. On sale at Bulletin Office, 6d.; by
post to any Australasian address, Bd. in stamps of
any State.
The new Oceanic S.S. Co.’s steamer
Sonoma has put up a record by doing the Auck-
land-’Frisco trip in 16d. 3h. 58m. actual steaming
time.
Let the galled jade wince ! Melbourne
bank-managers and clerks wail loudly in the local
Age about the shameful sweating practised on
them by directors and general managers. Where-
fore it is well to recollect how, when the Vic.
Clerks’ Union (now over 500 strong) was started
last year, these same haughty bank johnnies rolled
up to the initiatory meetings at the instigation of
Murray Smith and sundry other pseudo-friendly
directors, and scoffed at the idea of a plebeian
“union,” and consequent affiliation with the
Trades Hall crowd. And now the latter—who
could help the bank-chollies to some purpose—
would be quite justified in letting them work out
their own salvation.
Visitors have fought particularly shy of
prohibited Clutha (M.L.) district since the
imposition of the embargo on the “ accursed
drink, ” and the Otago Professor who holiday’d
there lately is likely to make it his last visit. Of
course drink (of a sort) can be procured anywhere
in Clutha, but the Pro. thoughtfully brought up a
whole case of old Scotch, and when at midnight
the house was raided as a suspected sly-grog shop
the imported swanky gave the landlord away.
He kept his hidden, and the sight of a spectacled
Professor, in his night-gear, arguing fruitlessly
with a smiling bobby at tne front door was remem-
bersome. Said bobby arrested the plunder, but next
morning the Professor got it back as the result of
a wire to the police-inspector.
Gow’s picture of “The Jacobite Rebel-
lion” in N.S.W. National Gallery was bought on
the recommendation of the present King of Eng-
land. Parkes’ account of how the purchase was
made is worth recalling just now. The old man
was at the Royal Academy dinner in 1882. He
mentioned to the Prince of Wales (as he was then)
that he thought of buying the picture for Sydney.
“ The Prince,” Parkes relates, “took a chair, and
sitting down before it, carefully examined the
picture for some minutes, and rising he pro-
nounced his opinion in favor of the purchase. I
telegraphed the opinion of the Prince of Wales
to Sydney, and in reply I was requested to buy
the picture.” One is afraid to recall the story for
fear some absurd Jingo should demand that it be
painted on the frame.
The champion mean man hails from Mel-
bourne. On Xmas Day he fell in with an ac-
quaintance named Wickham. The latter, after
some drinks together, rather than see the other
man go dinnerless, took him towards Bourke-st.
with the object of buying him a meal. When
Wickham extracted a sov. from his pocket in mis-
take for a shilling his friend’s cupidity was imme-
diately excited. He violently struck Wickham,
snatched the coin and ran off. When convicted
five weeks later, he got an extra two months for
contempt of court in remarking to the prosecutor
“ I won’t forget you for this, my lad; I’ll kill you
stone dead.”
A few bitter observations from the Far
North :
Rockhampton (Q.) Municipal Council is rapidly gaining
a reputation. One of its members signs his correspon-
dence “ X—his mark.” There is some talk of running a
Chinaman hawker at next election in order to have
municipal affairs properly attended to. It is about time
something was done. The other day, after lengthy dis-
cussion, a n\o,tion to supply an additional water-cart to
lay the everlasting dust in the streets was negatived.
Reason—no funds. A few days later a motion was carried
with acclamation granting the Mayor—a jolly, good, come-
an’-have-a-drink sort of fellow—an addition of £lOO to his
already handsome remuneration. Rockhampton water-
supply is derived from dirty lagoons. At present you can
scent a glass of this water at 3yds. distance. The color
is sea-green, and the “ water ” is impregnated with the
essence of the droppings of the wild fowl that crowd the
surface of the lagoons. In fact, the water is in such bad
condition that all the fish in one of the lagoons died about
a week ago and, rising to the surface, floated about in
myriads. Twenty-seven cart-loads were removed by the
authorities. And ten thousand or more people have to
use this water daily.
“ Veteran ” to The Bulletin
« Banjo ” Paterson, writing in S. M. Herald (12/2/’l),
quotes an “ Australian cavalry officer to the effect that
“ the only place for cavalry weapons is at the bottom ol
the nearest well,” and refers to “no less an authority
than Conan Doyle" (who made the fat doctor an
authority ?) as saying that swords and lances should be
put in museums. Then “ Banjo ” goes on to show that
the cavalry horses were overloaded, overworked, and
underfed. “ The saddle and gear weighed no
less than 7st.” Well, an English cavalry saddle,
without the gear, weighs 361 b. Add to that
1201 b. for a day’s forage, and 12st. or so
for the man, and the cavalry puzzle vanishes. With 20st.
on a horse's back, ride him 20 or 30 miles a day, part of
the time at a gallop, picket him in the open at night—all
this with half-feed—and then put the blame of inefficiency
upon “cavalry-weapons” forsooth! “Banjo” also
wr it;es:—“ While generals are summarily ‘Stellenbosched
for losing men, it was not likely that our leader (French)
would risk his whole career by sending cavalry on to meet
possibly a destructive rifle-fire. He had his reputation
to consider, and if he had attacked the fleeing
Boers, and had not got the guns, but had lost
half the men, he would have been sent home in
disgrace.” “ Banjo ” doesn't know it but . these
remarks embody the severest possible condemnation of
French. War is full of risks, and the difference between a
good and a bad leader is that the former can, and the latter
cannot, calculate them. If Kellermann had been another
French, he would never, with half-a-dozen French squad-
rons, have charged into the midst of the victorious
Austrian army at Marengo, and changed the history of
Europe!

Newfoundland has risen in revolt against
the “introduction of capital for the development
of the resources of the colony ” and such-like
ruinous foolishness. That bleak and fishy island
stood out of the Canadian Federation and kept
aloof from the Protectionist system which has
made Canada prosperous, and when it went prac-
tically broke in ’95 its rulers decided that what
was wanted was “the introduction of capital to
develop the resources of the country.” So they
arranged to sell or give away the railway rights,
telegraphs, docks, mail service, the chief coal de-
posits and 1,625,000 acres of land to one huge
millionaire, who was to put the country on its
legs again by the plentiful introduction of foreign
capital. And after five years of these blessings a
new Parliament has been elected in which 32 out
of 36 members are pledged to get rid of this awful
foreign capitalist at any price. _ The trouble is
that he will probably want a million or two as
compensation, and Newfoundland is, if possible,
more “broke” since foreign capital came in than
it was before.
“Kiwi” on the noble old pioneer :
“Go up one” for the “Blatant Pioneer” article in B.
5/li/l. Of the many illusions under which Maoriland
labours one of the worst is that the present generation is
horribly ungrateful to its noble self-sacrificing pioneer.
The bald truth is that the pioneer is generally a purse-
proud egotist, who brags a good deal about what he has
done for the country, though he never dilates on what the
country has done for him. The pioneer had splendid
chances to enrich himself, and it is that portion of him
which made the most of those chances that is loudest m
indecent self-advertisement. The other part who didn’t
do so is generally overlooked. Let any one read the his-
tory of our early land transactions which led up to the
Maori war and our railroad swindles, and he will have
no difficulty in locating the people who now form our
vulgar landocracy and snobocracy, and who would have
us believe that they made Maoriland. Apropos of all
this there is a good story. A thrifty Irishman forged
his way to the exalted position of Mayor of one of our
cities, and one day he invited his old towney Doolan,
the policeman, to dine with him. “ Now, Doolan,” said
his Worship, “you’d be long in Oireland before ye’d
dine wid the Mayor.” “ Thrue for ye,” retorted Doolan,
“ an’ ye’d be long there ye’self before ye’d be Mayor.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The February meeting of the V.A.T.O.
promises to have an interesting third chapter on the 23rd
and already the course of events at Caulfield has ensured
something of a boom for the V.R.C. Autumn fixture,
which starts a week later. Last Saturday’s form opened
up a question as to whether the hitherto underrated
Clean Sweep is not the actually best colt of his vear when
the colors are up. In a small field for St. George’s Stakes
ine Melbourne Cup winner was allowed to start at 10 to 1,
a clear proof that trainer Scobie thought him incapable of
beating Paul Pry over nine furlongs, at a difference of
0n nj ° B ' ® Rupert Clarke’s old horse carried the stable
confidence and went out a hot favorite (6 to 4 against)
but when it came to racing down the straight Clean
Sweep came from the ruck and with one long rush landed
himself a length ahead of poor old Pry. A brilliant
performance.
Evidently Clean Sweep doesn’t show them his
true form in private. The colt’s wins have been more or
less of surprises on three occasions. After the Melbourne
Cup it was supposed by Scobie and Co. that Derby winner
Malster, the runner-up, could have easily beaten Clean
Sweep on level terms, though the lighter-weighted one
seemed to win with pounds in hand. To-day the Ballarat
stable must be feeling very doubtful about Malster’s
supenority. The “ crack ” was backed down to 3to 1 for
eld Futurity Stakes, wherein nothing had a chance
W!th the 6.13 Aurous. Malster (9.8) lost no reputation by
finishing behind the half-sister to Aurum and Auraria,
yet the fact of Finland (9.1) splitting the pair looks bad
tor the Derby winner’s chance of winning Newmarket
Handicap, anyhow. He is probably not a great colt —
merely a good one. Stouter built than Clean Sweep, the
handsome Malster ought to be a better weight-carrier,
but with not more than, say, 8.5 on their respective backs
this scribe would take the Cup winner to beat the Derby
hero over any distance from 6 furlongs to 3 miles. So
there now !
Caulfield Futurity Stakes and St. George’s
Stakes are allowed to rank as w.f.a. events, whereby the
winner escapes a penalty. As a matter of fact) the
penalty conditions are so numerous and unreasonable
that such races amount to handicaps with some of the
candidates “chucked in.” Take five of the 3 year-old
starters—Aurous, Finland, Malster, Fides, and Debenture
—in the so-called weight-for-age Futurity Stakes. Their
respective weights were 643, 94, 9.8, 8.9, and 8 stone. In
the other weight-for-oye struggle young Clean Sweep had
to give 41bs. to a 5-year-old mare—Mane Corelli.
Lancaster, who appeared to have solid backing
down to 5 to 2, ran nowhere behind Clean Sweep and
Paul Pry in the 9 fur. race at Caulfield last Saturday. On
this form the vaunted 4-.vear-old doesn’t look likely to do
great things at the V.R.C. meeting. Present scribe has
no fancy for Lancaster in the Australian Cup. The horse
that, for no sufficient reason, gets selected by the
“ books ” as a nominal favourite for future events is never
worth considering. Thus far Lancaster has not beaten a.
good field of triers over a li mile course.
• * * •
Subsequent happenings make it look as if
whilom manager(C. L. M’Donald) of St. Albans knew some-
thing when at the dispersal of that stud he secured Wake-
ful and Aurous. Within a few days Wakeful won Oak-
leigh Plate, and Aurous (Sat. last) the valuable Futurity
Stakes. Prize-money won by each has more than paid
their cost, and atop of it all there’s the pile raked in
through bets. Wakeful has incurred a penalty, but not
so t’other. She has 6.12 in Newmarket, 643 in Don-
caster, and 6.9 in Sydney Cup. Her performance in
Futurity States, though accomplished easily, is nothing
to rave about. She only humped 641 to Malster’s (third)
9.8. Last-named nag is of the same year as the winning
filly. Aurous (Wallace—Aura) hadn’t previously won a.
• » * •
A par last week spoke of the speedy Wakeful
as a “Sunrise mare,” whereas shj is a daughter of
Trenton and Insomnia. The Wakeful (by Sunrise)
recorded in recent “ Racing Chronicles ” would probably
want a long start from Leslie M’Donald’s filly on a six-
furlongs race.
By the way, intending backers of the New-
market favorite had better be wakeful likewise. Doesn’t
follow that because the “ good thing” got home in the
Oakleigh Plate it will be asked to win Newmarket.
Handicap at a short price. The stable, up to time of
writing, has little or no money on Wakeful for the big
sprint race, so unless the good thing is regarded as almost
a cert,’ M’Donald will possibly prefer to keep it for a
less risky spec, and a better market.
* * * .
Three of the four Futurity Stakes run have gon&
to St. Albans nags. Resolute won first, Bobadil second
and Aurous latest. Other winner—Palmer—was prepared
at Queenscliff.
* * » *
Fulminate’s win of S.T.C. Challenge Stakes,
last week suggests the query, What weight will stop her ?'
Though eighteen faced the barrier, there was only
one in it from first to last. It was much the best
performance in the big list of Challenge Stake winners
for Fulminate (with 9.6) humped a far bigger burden than)
any winners before her, and equalled Kirry’s Australian
record (1.13) put up in same event two years ago. Fact
that there was a gale of wind blowing detracts somewhat
from the merit of the feat as far as speed goes, still there
was much to admire in it. She had 2st. (less 21b.) more
than Kirry in the saddle. Field was the smallest (with
two exceptions) during past twelve years. Fulminate
first saw light at Kirkham, N.S.W.
• * * *
Rand wick-trainer Kelso made a big impression
on the final day (Saturday) of S.T.C. meeting. Moravian
(Hurdles) Bira (Corinthian H’cp), Dandy (Anniversary
H op), and Miss Melos (Highweight H’cp) had their num-
bers hoisted first. Moravian’s success was no good to
backers as stewards disqualified him for interference with
Noble, who got the money. Though Miss Melos won
easily, betting didn t indicate that anyone fancied her
much. Rarely does Kelso make such a slip, but the best
of them do it sometimes, which is why Mo thrives.

An incident at S.T.C. meeting (Randwick) on
Saturday makes it appear that P.M.G. Crick can’t get
through with his self-imposed mission of clipping the'
C'-n'r?' 8 it 00 soon—for ’tis common property o that
b. 1.0. and A.J.O. are, to an extent, identical. At finish
of Anniversary Handicap many competent judges and
most pressmen well-placed to see felt certain that corn-
par itive outsider Bombshell had won, but Dandy’s;
number went up. Owner, smarting under his heavy
loss, (for success meant a big cut of the sweep the
prize money, and much in long shot bets) protested
Ascertaining later that the laws of racing placed the-
judge’s ruling beyond question, owner wished to with-
draw, but the stewards weren’t on and fined him £5 for
entering a “frivolous protest.” Rubbing it in with a.
vengeance !
* * * •
Rush on top-notch M.L. nag Advance for New-
market Handicap continues. He’ll need to be everything
he is said to be to have a chance of winning, as, under exist-
ing conditions, he can hardly be seen at his best. By the
time Advance reaches Melbourne he will have been seven
days out of training, and the big sprint is only two weeks;
off. It seems far too much to expect, but still the money
comes and in big parcels.

* » * *
At Hokitika (M.L.) races a man drew Derry in
a three-horse sweep, and backed the other two, Apre-
mete and Nayborn, on the tote. Apremete won, but was
disqualified, and the race was awarded to Derry. As the
sweep-money went to horse first past the post, however,
the man didn’t get anything at all. It’s certainly a
curious situation to have 'every horse in the race going
for you and yet not win a single shilling.
•-* » •
M.L. champion sprinter Blazes lately carried
9.12 over 6fur. in 1.15 at Wellington, and next day put up
nearly a stone more (10.11) and galloped his field off their
legs in 1.17. Record: 45 starts, 26 wins: 10 times un-
placed.

* • • *
Reported that famous brood mare Idalia, foaled
over 20 years since, died at Hawkes Bay (M.L.) t’other
day. She was the dam of Sir Modred, Idalium, Cheviot,
July, Liverpool, Fair Nell, Enid, Ravenswing, Eros, Sir
Lancelot, Ideal and many more.
♦*. * *
A jockey named Bonner was killed in M.L.
recently during a race. The day before, he had purchased
a song called “ Break the News to Mother,” and sent it
home, where it arrived next day just about the time the
race was run.
