Sydney “ jock,” on asking the owner for his riding fee was
coolly referred to those who had given him his ruLing in-
structions for the race in question.
* • m m
Apropos the Sloan slump. Seems from late
Yankee mail news that Sloan can ride on some race-tracks
there. Though stewards of the California J.C. shutdown
upon him he got licenses from other clubs, such is the
way they have of doing things in the U.S. Interviewed
in America Sloan said that his case would come before
the English turf people again ; he’d be there, and when
’twas definitely deterniined one way or t’other the public
might expect something interesting from him.
* * * *
The base insinuations of the T. and C. Journal
that “doping” coursing dogs in N.S.W. is a common
thing has caused Sydney Rrfer.ee and Melb. Sportsman
coursing scribes to hold up their hands in holy
vr°?i r< A r al }d w ax indignant. Former is president of
N.O.A. or N.S.W. The doggy man is laughing in his
sleeve at them. Writer knows of his own knowledge that
twas done scores of times almost under the noses of the
mother-state Coursing Ass., which has a heap to learn
about the game before it can get even nearly up to
date. Says the Sportsman authority: “I witnessed
about 2500 courses, and the only time I saw the hypo-
dermic syringe in use was for the purpose of injecting
cocaine when a dog was wounded. Twice only during
the season did I witness cases of apparent stopping dogs.”
Of course the “ dopers ” advertise that they’re going to
dope, and do it right in the centre of the enclosure *
• • * *
Captain’s Flat (N.S.W.) miner Dan O’Connell,
Armidale (N.S.W.) M'Coll (both heavyweights,
though their poundage was announced 11.4 each) disap-
pointed a big house at Gaiety A.C. Monday night. O’Con-
nell’s appearance indicated great lack of preparation, and
his actions were somewhat dreamy. Work of both sides
was badly directed and ineffective’from the outset. After
round two Dan did little but flounder and tumble, while
the crowd hooted and groaned lustily. Ultimately
referee Weeks called the farce off and declared M’Coil
winner. ’Tis to be hoped O’Connell will forswear the
game now, as Sydney public won’t stand him again.
M‘Coll is a big raw ’un who’ll probably take a lot of
thumping and give some too in his career, but he can
never be a champion ; he’s too stiff and slow.
* * * .
Whilom glutton-for-punish ment Dan. O’Connell
quitted palpably on Monday night, as all of his kind
invariably do after a time. Joe Goddard did it, ditto
Sharkey, and numbers of dimmer lights. Plucky and
as insensible to blows as men can be, they get all the heart
pummelled and punched out of them, till by-and-bye
they ask : “Is the game worth the candle ?” and, feeling
convinced that a big dose of stoush is invariably their
lot, shape up determined to take as little of it as possible
for the money. The same thing is often seen in the
fighting bull-dog who has been worsted a few times.
* * * *
Sydney waterman-pug. Johns, who has been a
winner right through his career, goes to ’Murka in the
Sonoma, on Tuesday, accompanied by Cribb’s mascotte-
trainer Stoneham, whom the M.L. bruiser sent for. Yankee
fight-promoters have cabled for Tim Hegarty to meet the
latest U.S. wonder, “terrible” Teddy M’Govern, but
Hegarty is in Perth (W.A.) now, en route ’Oine, and meets
-Jack Cullen for the Australian lightweight supremacy on
Wednesday.
* • » *
Match between M.L. pug Cribb and Yankee
bruiser Thurston at ’Frisco (Jan. 18) is referred to as the
toughest ever seen in that State. “ For fierce onslaughts,
terrific batterings, unquestioned and physically heroic
gameness, Thurston has no equal in the business,” says
the local writer. In the 16th round the fight looked'a
sure thing for Cribb, but Thurston crossed on the point,
-and went within an ace of outing the Australian. Cribb,
though badly dazed, weathered the term through taking
an awful pounding, and at the close of the 20th round
was fighting stronger than the Yankee. The referee’s
verdict was a draw. From start to finish the battle was
a fistic whirlwind, a veritable pugilistic cyclone. The
opposing forces lashed and clashed again in a
manner that shook both from neck to heel,
while loud heartfelt “ Oohs 1” came from the dense mass
of man in the building. How long they would have con-
tinued is conjectural, but the stipulated limit had been
reached. The knock-down in 16th round was all that
deprived Cribb of the decision. He was on the floor five
times in that particular 3min. and gave an object-lesson
to the crowd of how a fighter in dire extremities may
save himself by ducking and clinching. There wasn’t
much science in the struggle, but it fairly bristled with
exciting situations. For four days subsequently medical
men were not sure that Thurston would get over the
effects. Cribb spent the rest of that night and next day
in the Hammam (Turkish) baths.
• * * *
Otto Cribb’s first act on reaching ’Frisco was to
seek out his elder brother, who was keeping a saloon in an
•obscure corner, and set him up in a good house in a
fashionable street.
* * * *
Delightfully refreshing to read that at Syracuse
'(N.Y.), on Dec. 3, Fitzsimmons banqueted the judge
and jury who, six years before, acquitted the freckle-
faced freak of a charge of manslaughter laid in connec-
tion with the death of Fitz’s then sparring partner—Con
Riordan—while the two were engaged in a bout. Fitz.
feasted the same crowd immediately after the verdict and
promised to repeat the compliment when next in Syra-
cuse, hence the late treat.
* * • •
Among M.L. old-age pensioners, ’tis said, is
Bob Fitzsimmons’ mother ; she is 83 years of age and hale
and hearty as can be. Son Jack, who runs a smithy at
Timaru, took her to a dance on Xmas eve, when she had
as jolly a time as most others there. Fitzsimmons p'ere
died about 12 months ago.
* * * *
Word from Yankeedom is that Australian
grizzly Joe Goddard recently started as a fight-promoter,
and experienced a hot time the first night. House wasn’t
a paying one, and Joe refused to put the full progamme
on, whereupon hundreds kicked hard and rushed the
ticket-box. Goddard locked himself in while a squad of
police kept the kickers in check. Joe wouldn’t disgorge
though the crowd stood round and howled all night.
* » * *
Big struggle ’tween Dick Cavill and Bob Craig
at S. Melb., 9/2/’l, for 220yds. Australasian Champion-
ship, which former only won by a couple of feet, made it
look as if same pair would supply a hot tussle in the
Mother-State Premiership over same distance last week ;
but ’twas not so, as, after 150yds., Craig kept dropping
behind, until at least 10yds. separated them when Oavill
touched the finish. Time (1.41 1-5) is 3sec. slower than
Fred Lane’s Australasian best, and 7 l-ssec. outside
world’s record opposite Lane and Englishman Derbyshire’s
names, accomplished on the occasion of their dead-heat
“ down under ” last year. Difference in Craig’s swimming
is explainable. He is older and stronger than Cavill, so
the very rough S. Melb. water suited him better than the
smooth, fresh article at Redfern which Cavill fairly
revelled in.
* * * *
At Balmain Swimming Gala on Saturday
Bob Craig (second only to Dick Cavill in recent
Australasian championships) won the 100yds. District
Supremacy by a foot from M‘Gregor after the hottest pos-
sible struggle. Time, lm. 4sec. M'Gregor is a compara-
tively new man, whose latest swim was his first im-
portant engagement. He will make the very best move
briskly.
* * * •
Australasian champion schoolboy swimmer,
Claude Corbett, gave a fine exhibition of stamina in the
100yds. Inter-Ciub Handicap at Redfern (Sydney) baths
t other night. He put up 1.6 3-5, 1.6, and i. 5 3-5 respec-
tively in the preliminary, semi-final, and final heats.
Fact that water was fresh much enhances the perform-
ance.
•'* * •
Looks as if water-polo may boom again in
Sydney. The hitherto invincible Balmain team, whose con-
tinued success year after year choked all interest in it,
went under to Mort’s Dock reps, last Saturday. Now
that the weak spot has been found, other affiliated clubs
will probably take to the game, and perhaps make it
interesting. In the days of old Wentworths, the standard
of polo-play was high in Sydney.
* * * *
Determination of Hunter River (N.S.W.)
Tressider to retire is regrettable. His grievance is that
handieappers don’t treat him right. Tressider’s admirers
have long looked forward to matching their man against
Towns, and had the latter returned in Jan. last, as once
understood, a £2OO a-side bet could easily have been
raised. Such a match might have helped to boom scul-
ling again, but there’s no chance now, as Tressider really
means to give the game rest, he’s so disgusted.
• * •
Sydney Grade Cricket Competitions were con-
tinued after 4 weeks spell. Rain of previous night played
up with the wickets, and made it a bowlers’ day, but no
one did anything startling. Highest score in first grade
was Pite’s (Sydney) 46. Humphreys’ (Paddington) 73 in
third grade topped the day’s doings. Sydney compiled
most runs (106), but Waverley, with five down for 103,
looks like putting this in the shade. Averages of
bowlers : Ball and Gray (4 for 18 and 4 for 17 respectively)
stood out. M. A. Noble had 7 for 35, Howard 6 for 27,
aboriginal Marsh 5 for 25, and M‘Death 5 for 33.
• * * *
Suggested in Adelaide that local inter-state
selectors don’t give the youngsters long enough trial-
one or two failures and out they go. It is called to mind
that Giffen came to the front and kept there after a
succession of failures ; and it is remarked that in those
days selectors were not guided so much by figures as by
form.
* • * «.
George Giffen must bite bis nails when he thinks
of what he missed, long ago, by declining to stop in Eng-
land and be enlisted among the pros. There is big* money
in the game for the best professionals, if they choose to
take care of themselves. The programme of county
matches for the 1901 season includes a benefit for Giffen’s
veteran contemporary, William Gunn, to take place at
Lord’s on Whit Monday, also a bank-holiday ben. for J.
T. Brown, who is still on the sunny side of 3 j, and similar
plums for Lockwood, little Board, and Lillev. Such bene-
fits always mean a net profit of from £lOOO to £3OOO.
* * » *
Where are the rising Australian wicketkeepers ?
There is a splendid chance for juniors just now. Kelly,
Ross, and Jarvis are all unequal to a trip to England, and
all of them are veterans. The best of them (Jarvis) is 41,
and uncertain. Altogether, this season’s record seems
to show that Australian cricket is suffering from a re-
action.
* * • a
“ Yar ” : It should now be easy to pick the very
best Australian XI. Take the nine men (omitting Poi-
devin) on top in batting averages in inter-State matches,
add Trumble and Kelly, and you have it. Seems that
Saunders—who is no bat—should be among ’em, but I
don’t know whom to leave out.
* * * •
Advent of J. Horan in Tas.-Vic. match recently
recalls his father “Tommy,” a one-time top-notch Vic.
bat, and now “ Felix ” of the Australasian. “ Tommy’s ”
last stand against N.S.W. was close on 10 years since.
He is the only player who has made three centuries in
these contests and his total (1295) exceeds that hit up by
any other Victorian in opposition to N.S.W. Bruce
stopped at 1286, but might yet be heard from again.
* * * .
The sparse attendance at Sydney Cycle meet
on Saturday should make those pulling the strings give
the sport a rest. Despite persistent press booming (often
extending over columns of space), the game doesn’t catch
on as of old. The public is cloyed. “ Dull and threaten-
ing weather and Randwick Races materially affected
the attendance,” says Sydney D.T. Writer' saw little
wrong meteorologically, and wouldn’t mind wagering
that S.T.C. meeting didn’t make a differenc of more than
a couple of hundred at the very outside. Too much
wheelman—that’s the only explanation.

First distance (3m.) of Inter-Nation event—
Green and Megson (Great Britain) v. Bilmartin and Ivor
Layvson (U.S.) was the best item of Sydney cycling 1 at
Saturday’s carnival. Race was hot from pistol-fire
Lawson won by inches, with Martin and Megson together
second. Time (6m. 4. 4-55.) indicates high pressure.

Suggested by. Melb. police that all cyclists be
compelled to register and carry a number on their
machines as a corrective to their persistent habit of
giving false names when stopped for reckless riding.
This habit has become so general that any offender
giving the name of Jones, Smith, Taylor, or such is now
promptly “ shot in.”

Sydney Bowling Club premiership tourney was,
on Saturday, fined down to two—Victoria Park and
Waverley. Each has survived every match played, but
conditions demand that one must beat t’other twice ere
absolutely winning. Waverley and City Clubs were-not
drawn together this year. In each of the previous ,t.wo
seasons City won right out with Waverley runners-up.
Victoria Park has never before been so close to getting
the pennant, and, as it has shown the best form of all
right through, its prospects look bright.
* * * *
N.W. Province Bowling Association (Kyneton,
Vic.) claims the oldest Australian bowler in the person of
Mr. John Watson who played with the Maypole Club
(Scotland) 70 years ago. He is 95 now and until three
years ago was one of the Kyneton club team. St.
Leonards’ Club (Sydney) owns a member —“ Daddy "
Musgrave—(still in the game) who’d run the Victorian
ancient pretty close.
* • » •
A Warragul (Vic.) resident named Kemp
Penney has so fine a sense of touch that be can tell a
horse’s age by merely feeling its closed mouth. Several
traps have been laid for him by horsey men, but so far he
has invariably been successful. At a recent bazaar he
performed the equally difficult task of telling every variety
of poultry blindfolded.

Doctors are catching-on to the motor-cycle in
N.S.W. ; Elliott, of Austral Cycle Agency, having made
several sales lately. The doctors were the first to utilise
the motor-cycle in England. Then business men
engineered Royalty into taking a on a motor, and
setting the fashion.

Brunker is not keen on opposing Barton
for Hunter, and Quinn has withdrawn from the
East Sydney contest, so it looks as if the Federal
Premier and the Federal Opposition leader will
both be elected unopposed.
Siniler Hales was running the Bluff Hotel
at Coolgardie up to- the time of his departure for
S’Africa. By the way, he had his first bit of bad
luck when he arranged to start war lecturing at
St. James’s Hall, London, on what turned out to
be the night of the Queen’s death.
At Barton’s meeting in Adelaide the
“ arrival ” of Kingston seemed almost to have
been stage-managed. The mailboat from the
West only anchored at Largs Bay at 8 p.m., and
Kingston—per special launch and train —ascended
the platform just as Barton was concluding. A
packed audience cheered again when Kingston
explained that, “like my advent to South Aus-
tralia, I came as soon as I could.”
Mr. T. B. Curran (son of that popular
Sydney identity Mr. Tom Curran), who succeeded
to Parnell’s seat in the Commons, in which
assembly he sat for some years as the youngest
member, has been admitted to N.S.W. Bar, on
the motion of Mr. Barton, K.C, Mr. Curran is a
member of the English Bar.
Colin M'Kay Smith, infhrance-man and
journalist, died at Sydney last Sunday, aged 68
years. He was a.fine old Tory, who wrote good
English of the TiMES-leader order, and had much
to say on paper against the Commonwealth Bill;
also, he strongly upheld the capitalistic cause in
all labour and political disputes. But withal he
was an honorable and compassionate gentleman
with whom the world had dealt none too kindly,
and his death will be sincerely regretted by all to
whom he was known.
At Newcastle (N.S.W.) police-court on
9th Feb. 19 wheelers and drivers were charged
with laying Stockton Pit idle without giving 14
days notice as per contract. The wheelers had
no grievance, but struck wit u the drivers because
they considered the latter underpaid. They were
undefended, and pleaded guilty, and were each
fined £2 with 4s. 10s. court costs, and ss. wit-
nesses’ expenses or 7 days. One asked for time to
pay, and was granted 14 days; same terms were
offered to the others, but they declined and de-
cided to “take it out. ” A strong agitation ensued,
and the Minister for Mines wired on Tuesday the
12th to the miners’ general secretary that defend-
ants would be released at once. Work was re-
sumed on the following day. Which affair affords
strong plea for the Court of Arbitration.

Mt. Garnet, Queensland • Smelters shut down ;
some difficulty in treating the ore. The official yarn ‘ ‘ owing
to the extreme variation in the composition of the ore in
the bins and consequent irregularity in smelting.
When the metallurgist has sufficient ore bedded smelting
will be resumed.” Shares fell, but not to such an extent
as to indicate all-round failure, and the market
as a rule is a great truth-teller. A Herberton, and
therefore probably a biassed, correspondent writes that the
best thing in copper in Q. is undoubtedly Mt. Garnet,
that the cost of charcoal is the trouble, that there is
enough ore in sight to keep the smelters going for two
years, and that the first 50 tons of ore produced matte con-
taining 40 per cent, copper and 120 oz. silver per ton. But
no getting away from the fact that some serious mistakes
have been made at the smelting start. Not a word said
about zinc, and in silver and copper-mining silence is sel-
dom golden, but often
Cobar Chesney, N.S.W.: Shareholders’ meet-
ing in Melbourne last week. Shares increased from £1 to
225. 6d., givinga calling-power on 180,000 shares of £22,500.
Already the co. has a debenture debt of upwards of
£20,000. Favourable report read from Green way, man-
ager of Block 14. Says mine can be brought to a pro-
ducing stage with about £lO,OOO, but to put
it in a position to do its own smelting will
need another £20,000. Copper values of a big lode from
3 to 5 per cent, average, probably in the immediate
vicinity of 4 per cent.; some serious mistakes made m
the design, construction, erection, and working of the
concentrating mill which will cost thousands to remedy.
Ball-mills will take the place of second rolls, the second
iigs will be done away with, and when complete the plant
will treat 200 tons of ore a day, and the concentrates and
picked ore will be sent elsewhere. Mine development
« * *
Mt Lyell: More explanations of recent de-
velopments and the condition of the workings, and several
other unsatisfactory features in connection with this
great mine which cannot be explained away by newspaper
“ vabber ” It is a poor lookout when columns of corres-
pondents’ gush are wired to different newspapers. The
fact remains that the co., having worked an undue propor-
tion of the footwall part of the lode, which contains
the best copper, have now to face the other
part and the average must continue to fall. Chances
are that it will come down to less than 2 per cent. More
than that, the lower developments of the mine are not
encouraging, giving good ground for the suspicion that
the Mt. Lyell ore-body is a huge basin, that it will not go
much below No. 7 level, and ’tis said there are in
that, as well as in the level above, undoubted
evidences of the two walls of the lode coming to-
gether. It must not be forgotten that there re-
mains an enormous quantity of low-grade ore above
this level, that while it is low in value it is a magnificent
smelting-base, and the mines for miles around it, almost
without exception, can’t do without it. Estimated that
the co. made about £70,000 last year by smelting ores
from other mines, and the profits in this direction are
likely to be considerably increased, although it is likely
that, as time goes on, the parent co., to make the
same amount of money, will have to treat a
much larger quantity of custom ore—in other
words, smelting charges will have to be materially
reduced as soon as it is generally krtcwn that the co. can-
not afford to be any. longer independent. Hitherto all
the mines have been dependent on the Mt. Lyell. It will
be quite a different story when Mt. Lyell is largely de-
pendent on the other mines, and that time will arrive
the moment it is established beyond doubt that the
Lyell cannot be worked alone at a profit. From all
appearances that time is not very far off.
N.S.W. Dredges: Araluen Proprietary, 30oz.
Good enough to pay probably, but doubtful if there is
much profit in it. Araluen Valley, 330 z. ; ditto this.
Shoalhaven River, 14oz. If the others don’t show any
profit, this must show a loss. Turon River No. 1,16 oz.
No. 2 could not work for want of water, and both seem
somewhat languishing for want of gold. Turons were
once up to £3 a share, when the Sabbath tipster advised
his readers to wade in, jand he has never wearied,
and is still at it, now that buyers are willing
to give 11s., and sellers to take £l. This margin is
representative of others, though not quite so wide. Mac-
quarie No. 1, which so far has maintained the best aver-
age of all the N.S.W. dredges, has some trouble with its
tailings, and delay is on account of the attempt to remedy
this Macquarie No. 2 was built on a rock which came
through the bottom of the pontoon, and it will take time
and money to put it right. The Associated Dredges
will be the biggest in N.S.W., if not in Aus-
tralia, costing £12,000 each, and when they get going in
a month or two they will probably deal with an enormous
quantity of stuff every week ; as they are situated on
that part o the Macquarie not far from the estuary of the
Turon they should make a name for themselves. There
are few spots on the Turon suitable for dredging ; the fall
in most places is so great in that river, and the banks so
precipitous, and the bottom so rough that the bulk of its
drift must have been carried into the Macquarie.

The irrepressible Foster, late Vic. Mines Mini-
ster, is just now barracking for an alleged iron-mine in
his electorate, and the Vic. Government is being asked to
lend the necessary rails to make a 21-mile line from Gipps-
land Lakes Entrance to the “ iron deposits.” The mana-
ger of these deposits appears to be a gentleman of the
same name as the manager of the Mount Deddick silver-
lead-gold-copper mine when Foster the Minister was agi-
tating for a Deddick railway.
* * • *
Jones’ patent gold-saving table, now in use on
the Grey River, New River, and Leviathan dredges
(M.L.), is, The Bulletin hears, proving invaluable on the
West Coast in the saving of fine gold from the black sand,
a matter which has baffled mining people for years.
Jones’ patent can, no doubt, be much improved upon in
time, in which event the hundreds of miles of black sand
in sight can be most advantageously worked ; in fact,
these “ tables ” will, apparently, do for beach deposits
what cyanide and chlorination have done for low-grade
ores.

Mr. L. C. Snowdon, Surgeon-Dentist, writing
from Post Office Hotel, Maryborough, Queens-
land, on 3rd Nov., 1900, says : “ I may state that
I was a sufferer from chronic kidney disease,
which made my life perfectly unbearable. I con-
sulted the best available doctors both in England
and in Australia, but with one result—absolute
failure to obtain relief. I tried different proprie-
tary medicines, but with no beneficial result.
During this time you can well understand the
pain and misery I endured, to say nothing of the
expense. I was staying with a professional
friend, and related my trouble. He suggested
Warner’s Safe Cure, but I ridiculed the idea, tell-
ing him I was heartily sick of both doctors and
medicine. The symptoms gradually became
worse, the pain more intense, and, in a fit of
desperation, I despatched my assistant for a
bottle of this remedy, although expecting no
better results from it than from other medicines 1
had used Thank God for that bottle ! It was
but the forerunner of my complete cure. I used
half-a-dozen bottles, and my cure was perfect and.
marvellous.”
Maxwell, the pious Jingo barrister with
the hatchet face, is standing for the Senate in
Victoria. When a born parson is educated for a
lawyer and turns politician, he invariably suffers
from too much snuffle and shuffle. Maxwell
might have been in Parliament long ago if he
could have professed the same set of principles tor
a month at a stretch. He has been rejected bv so
many constituencies that a vote of sympathy
might inflict him on the Commonwealth.
An English paper :
The unconventional manners of the Australian Bush-
men at the front are amusingly illustrated by the follow-
ing extracts from a trooper’s letter, written from Zeerust.
“ The Bushmen are going home, and we shall be sorry to
lose them, for we have always got on A 1 together, they
had sports at Zeerust last Saturday. Methuen was there,
and the funniest sight was a tall Australian, about Oft.
4in., telling Methuen in a dramatic way how they had
been done out of a tug-of-war by ‘some idiot telling
them to cease pulling when they only wanted to pull their
opponents about another six inches to win ! Methuen
was laughing heartily, and his staff were in hysterics
nearly. The Bushman didn’t care a hang, but spun his
yarn the same as he would to a Tommy. They are a
splendid body of men, and don’t care a rap for anybody
or anything.
M. Masson’s book on “ Napoleon and the
Fair Sex,” is a plain statement of facts reprinted
from Le Figaro. Napoleon and Josephme loved
each other, though their first acquaintance
was based upon the delusion of each one
that the other had money. At Milan, in
the first Italian campaign, the singer Grassnn
tried in vain to captivate Napoleon. He
was strictly faithful to Josephine. In Egypt he
formed a liaison with Madame Foures. She had
put on a Chasseur uniform and accompanied her
husband, a lieutenant, from Marseilles. Napoleon
sent him to France with despatches. The English
captured him and sent him back, having got the
wink from French officers. Josephme s son,
Eugene, was in Napoleon’s suite, and of course
knew all about it. When Napoleon was at
Milan the second time, after Marengo, Grassini
became his mistress. It was concealed from
Josephine. Grassini had a particular friend,
Madame Marchesi, another vocalist. Napoleon
issued a command for Grassini, Marchesi, and
Mrs. Billington, the English singer, to accom-
pany him from Milan to Paris. This was only a
blind to smuggle Grassini over. This woman,
however, fell out of favor through trying to inter-
fere in politics. She became the mistress of Wel-
lington, in Paris, in 1815. De Quincey records
the delight with which he used to hear her sing in
London at the Opera.
The Melbourne milk-carters are making a
stand against the sweating to which they have
been hitherto subjected with impunity because
they do not come under the action of the Shops
and Factories Act. It is asserted that, for a
seven-day week of work beginning at 3 to 4 o clock
in the morning, these helots get only from 30s.
down to as low as 15s. The Milkmen s Union
has published for public information a “white
list ” of dairymen who, in addition to paying
reasonably well, treat their carters fairly in the
matter of board and lodging.
After all the Jingo press yells about “ the
Boers without uniform not being entitled to
belligerent privileges,” it is interesting to note
Wellington’s views on the subject. During the
Peninsular War, Massena’s communications were
harassed by an un-uniformed Portuguese militia,
the members of which he threatened to shoot on
capture. Wellington wrote to him as follows :
The men whom you call “ peasants without uniform,”
“ assassins,” and “ highway robbers,” are a local militia,
which I have already informed you, are military corps,
commanded by officers and acting under military law.
You hold, it seems, that only those are entitled to
belligerent rights who are clothed in uniform; but you
should remember that you yourself have added to the
glory of the French army while in command of soldiers
who had no uniforms. — Wellington’s Despatches, vol. VI.,
p. 464.

“The Marshall-Hall broil no longer fizzles
noisily, but a sputter awaits the new man, Peter-
son, unless he’s extra careful. The latter’s brother
profs, seem prepared to cold-shoulder their coming
colleague, inferring that, as ‘ a man of honor,’ he
should have declined his appointment when in-
formed (by the Professorial staff) that Marshall-
Hall’s name had been withheld from the Selection
Committee.
“ Cynics diagnose this doctrine as esprit de
corps , in defence of that sacred institution, a Pro-
fessor’s tenure. The Dons’ motto seems to be,
‘ Not all the water in the rough, rude sea can
wash the balm from an anointed Professor,’
while opponents sum up the position of the
’Varsity staff in the words —‘ Scratch a Prof., and
all his mates will squeal.’
“ The few pious papas who upheld Marshall-
Hall for canny reasons connected with their
daughters’ musical progress now proffer quaint
explanations of their championship. Said a plump
proser, in a railway compartment, t’other day, ‘ I
loathe the man’s opinions, but I’m told on good
authority that when he’s wielded the baton for an
evening, there’s a pool of perspiration on the floor
all round where he’s been standing. Marvellous
energy! Marvellous!’ Speaker’s confidante, a
gushing matron, testified that at one time, guided
by hearsay, she herself regarded the hymner as
‘an unclean beast.’ ‘But,’ she went on, ‘l’ve
completely altered my opinion since the nurse
(such a sweet girl) who attended him when he was
so ill assured me that he has the most pure, lovely,
delicate mind conceivable ! ’
“The popular beauty of Melbourne and favor-
ite poseuse of the studios has contracted an
engagement with a naval officer, as milky-skinned
and handsome as she is olive and brilliant.
“ It is recorded of a gay young moth who flut-
ters foolishly round the stage candle, that he asked
a brilliant little foreign dancer why she brought
her husband out with her from Europe, as she
would have had a much rosier time without him.
‘Another time,’she lisped sweetly, with an acre
of promise in her dark eyes, ‘ when I learn
enough English.’ That was years ago, and ever
since the benedict has been pondering over the
reply.
“Palmists are rising to affluence in Melbourne.
One oracle has retired on her gilded laurels, and
others may be expected to follow suit. The most
swagger seer of all has rooms in the heart of town
where he gives audience to a host of the ourious
and gullible all day long. You have to wait hours
for your turn. But then he is the most up-to-date
of Prophets. He sits enthroned in a dark (of
course, dark) apartment in gorgeous apparel —full
Court-dress, with his long locks hanging aestheti-
cally over a lace ruffle in Charles 11. style. He
has the hands of a woman —long, white, _ slender
with filbert nails, and sparkling rings. His touch,
hysterical girls declare, is Magnetic. He is
gentle-voiced and persuasive. His charges to lift
the veil of the Past, or Past and Present, are half-
a-crown and five shillings. The Future goes into
a higher figure, and the whole entire complete
‘To-be,’ down to the virtues and vices of
the yet - unborn, costs the curious about a
guinea. And there are many ready to
prize the pearls of this Omniscient Charlatan m
ruffles, for such an exorbitant consideration.
Yet, if he tells you all that he professes to be able
to do, a thousand pounds would not requite so ex-
cellent a Daniel. The funny part of the business
is the diplomacy he shows in telling the half-crown
client just so much, and leaving off at a sensa-
tional point. Beyond that lies the five-shilling
province—and only the production of a twin coin
to the first will induce the Prophet to proceed
into that apartment. And the five-shilling client
has the same experience. The Seer stops at a
thrilling stage, and only an additional coin will
induce him to encroach on more uncharted ground.
And so on. Yet crowds rush him, and
every little suburban Sallie sells the bottles and
saves up her earnings to interview the wily wizard,
while sovereign Toorak openly stops its car-
riage at his sacred eyrie.
11 A good story is told in camera of a beauty
who a little while back started fast and furious
riding-parties. They promised to be the rage—
and the pretty Diana was to be canonised for her
clever project till one day an accident befell her—
and the new dissipation came to a sudden and
unexpected full-stop. Like the captain of a ship
in trouble, she was the last to leave after seeing
everyone else off. A Benedict (one of the
chaperones) remained behind as her escort. Then
the beauty gave the signal, and they were off too.
But her pony was fresh and played-up a bit. She
determined to take it out of the little wretch and
drove in the spurs. Then the pony fairly bolted.
Beauty lost her whip. A mere trifle. But next her
hat sped away in the wind—and horror ! a coil of
lovely chestnut hair rolled down into the dancing
dust; and that's why there have been no more of
these swagger summer riding-parties.
“ A Melb. girl—daughter of a politician—prizes
an autograph album in which tbe names of all the
notable Parliamentarians in Australia are in-
scribed with poetical and characteristic senti-
ments. The most extraordinary signature is that
of Sir Philip Fysh, which is written in one con-
tinuous string of pothooks, and has to be trans-
lated for everyone. Which recalls a letter once
received by Jennings-Carmichael from, it is be-
lieved, a well-known cleric who dabbled in verse
himself, and who writes a shocking hand. The
charming Jennings had an idea that the 4-pp. of
picturesque flourishes and strokes stood for some-
thing flattering, and carted the lengthy compli-
ment round to one and another of her friends,
none of whom could decipher a syllable beyond
the formal address. John Downer’s signature in
said book is weak, effeminate, scrawly, but he
atones by a very charming and witty impromptu
verse—in French. The K.C., in spite of platter
features, and all that, has a captivating way with
women.

“ The conceit of some Britishers would fluster a
snail. When the soldiers of the King were here,
a local rough was fined for assaulting one of them.
Our bad boy, whose ‘ tart ’ had ‘ chucked ’ him all
day for red-coat society, skulked into a pub., and,
in a surly way, demanded and paid for a‘beer.’
A by-standing Tommy, who had probably
quenched his thirst gratis and felt polite and high-
toned in consequence, struck in— ‘ That ain’t the
wy to arsk. Arslt civilly, my man,’ tapping the
beastly colonial with his little cane as he spoke.
Not to mention two lovely black eyes, the mentor
got enough of the Australian adjective to last him
for the rest of his life.
“Scribe hears of another rift within the lute,
whereon Old England twangs, tunes for Young
Australia to dance to. A Victorienne, whose
English fiance was deriding Australian brag and
self-satisfaction, interposed snappishly,, ‘ Yes ; I
admit we are proud of our country.’ ‘ You have
no country—it’s Ours /’ grunted John Bull, junior.
“ The Woman’s Column in the evening paper
has been encouraging housewives to supply
schedules of expenditure. Newspaper domestic
experts frequently accuse household fairies, who
reveal the cost of their kitchen magic, of ‘ spend-
ing too much on meat.’ The reason, I guess, why
unassisted females cultivate. carnivorous tastes
among their flock, is that life is too short to cater
for vegetarians. A man can’t or won’t live on
plain boiled cabbages and turnips. He can and
will submit complacently if for one dish of meat
you substitute several dainty delicious plats. (It
takes heaps of elaborate purges, omelettes, salads,
macaroni-cheese and such to placate a male per-
son like one simple joint or grill.) Said vegetable
placation lasts no longer than the meat, though it
costs the cook hours of vegetable-chopping, rub-
bing through sieves, cheese-grating, egg-whisking
and so on.
“At presentMelb. she-society parts its lips and
gazes unseeingly over the heads of nobodies in
imitation of the daughters of an eminent person-
age. The prettiest of these comely spinsters, her-
self an accomplished poseuse, need not complain
if plagiarists do appropriate her mannerisms, for
most of her own dainty glances and attitudes im-
press the observer as borrowed, owing their at-
home air to much practice in front of the looking-
glass. Testimony to said belle’s skill as a coun-
tenance-change artiste is supplied by a page of her
photos in a (Jollins-street window portraying the
charming sitter along with her brother, smiling,
laughing, dimpling, twinkling, dreaming, brood-
ing, pining—in fact, rehearsing the gamut of her
facial repertoire.


What Mrs Bowles Found Out.
A hard thump on one’s head may make it ache,
but most headaches come without the aid of
thumps or bumps. They are caused by poisons in
the blood acting on the nerves.
The same is true of pains and lameness in the
muscles of the back and sid ;s—including rheu-
matism and lumbago.
Mrs. Mary Bowles will tell US' about hers, and
how she got over them at last ; and a thankful
woman she is for it.
“About ten years ago,” she says in her letter,
“my back and sides used to ache so bad that I
was often perfectly helpless and prostrate. Some-
times for weeks continuously I could not raise my
hand to any kind of work.
For the last thirty-five years I have worked at
dairying and farming in this district. I am now
turned 76 years old and am well known through-
out the district.
“ When I first felt the pains in my back and
chest I went to the chemist, who gave me a plaster
to put on. This relieved me slightly for a day or
two ; then the pains were as bad as ever. I found
out that it was my liver and kidneys that caused
the trouble, and was prescribed for by three
doctors and a chemist ; but no good came of all
they did.
“ The pairs I suffered were terrible ; I could
hardly bear them. By this time I was so bad I
didn’t know what to do or where to turn for help
Wh,t would have become of me if it had not been
for Mother Seigel’s Syrup I dare not think.
“ But, as a kind Providence ordained it, I saw
an advertisment of a case like mine being cured
by that medicine, and I got a bottle from a
chemist in Muswellbrook. That single bottle
relieved me very much, and by continuing to use
the Syrup of the good Mother Seigel I got well.
“1 now attend to my work as I did before the
complaint came upon me—thanks to this remedy
that was brought from England to Australia,
where it does such a lot of good.
I keep it all the time in the house, and always
mean to. And when anybody is ill among my
acquaintances or friends, I tell them totakeMother
•IP?, 1 ' 8 ®-' ru P’ for anything will cure them that
will. —Mrs. Mary Bowles, Kayuga, near Muswell-
brook, N.S.W., Sept. 21st, 1899.
The character of Mrs. Bowles, and the truth of
of the above statement made by her, is vouched
for by Mr. 0. J. Spratt, Auctioneer for the Far-
mers’ Association, Muswellbrook—who says she
has resided in the district 35 years, and he has
known her personally for eight years.*

The younger of the beautiful Cornwallis
West girls is engaged to the 21-year-old Duke of
Vestminster. Her elder sister married Prince
Henry of Pless (whoever he may be), and the
handsome brother lately fell captive to Lord Ran-
dolph Churchill’s widow. In a matrimonial way
of speaking, Mrs. Cornwallis West has scored an
enormous success with her children, and if she
ever meets the faded Jersey Lily these times she
can afford to smile graciously. They were rival
professional beauties for a while. Each of them
had her photos, in the London shop-windows, and
Mrs. Langtry’s photos, outstayed the other
woman’s. But now the beauty of the Langtry is
merely a tradition, whereas the loveliness of Mrs.
West has living representatives in the Princess
and the Duchess-elect. What a fool is the pretty
woman of sassiety who doesn’t give herself a
chance to mellow into a distinguished mother-in-
law !
Related recently of the new King that
once, when, as Prince of Wales, he was presiding
at an agricultural dinner, a homely old farmer,
when asked by a waiter what wine he would take,
said, ‘A’d rayther hae a moog o’ale.” At this
several snobs laughed, but the Prince, who had
heard what passed, at once said, “Very good
idea, too! Waiter, bring me some ale, also.”
Ihen the laughter froze up suddenly.

A man from England says terrible things
about the alleged Highlander of to-day :
One interesting feature of the Boer war is the denosi-
tion of the Highlander from his place of pride in the
British army. The man in kilts has passed. The slump
began at Majuba Hill in 81 and ended at Magersfontein
Iho if pre “ ent war - Nearly every time they baVe met,
the Boer has succeeded in damaging the Hielan’man in
someway or another. Even after Elandslaagte, where
the Gordon Highlanders got in and used the bayonet with
deadly effect, it was told with bated breath how, when
the Boers made their last stand, the Highlanders threw
themselves flat on the ground and not till the charge was
blown the third time would they get up again And
then it was a man of the Imperial Light Horse, an irregu-
lar corps who led the way—and died. It was the sudden
panic and bolt of a company of Highlanders who
held al i outlying kopje from which the Boer storming
P art J 8 ? men could hay e been picked off, that led to
the at Majuba Hill in ’Bl. Then the whole force
rushed down the hill. Had they rushed in with the
bayonet (according to Gen. Butler) Majuba would have
been another story. At Magersfontein a whole Highland
Brigade bolted lgnominiously because about ten per cent
tell. Had they rushed in with the bayonet; as the
Guards did earlier in the same march, Magers-
fontein wa B won and the road to Kimberlev
open. But they rushed in the wrong direction.
The reputation of the Highlander has for a long time
rested on a false foundation. When he first blazed in the
British military firmament he was a sturdy individual
fresh from the soil. His charges were irresistible, his fire
fast and accurate (deer-stalking had made him proficient)
and the tighter the place the better he seemed to like it.
In a very little time he made a terrific reputation. Aristo'-
cracy took him up and he became a fashionable institu-
tion. He got away from the soil, and was regarded as a far
and away too valuable fighting-machine to be used up
in small affairs. He gravitated always to the reserve
and the despised line regiments were put in front
to sweat and bleed, and die till the enemy’s strength was
worn out, and then- the skirl of the pipes was heard,
there was a sound of rushing feet, and the wavering ex-
hausted enemy disappeared in a swirl of kilts. Next
morning or so the world learned over its breakfast that
the Highlanders had stormed the heights with their
characteristic dash and gallantry. The fact that a
despised line regiment had, in 99 cases out of every 100
perished in breaking the back of the job, was hardlv
worth cabling, and so the world never heard of it
unless it read the official despatches—and that was too
much to expect any public to do, even if it got hold of
them, which was only about once in a century. The
Highlander is not to blame for his decadence. He is a
good man who has been spoiled. In many cases he has
been spoiled right out of existence, and the alleged
Highlander is not really a Highlander at all, a purely
mdmduahstic Government having allowed the Highlands
of Scotland to be depopulated to make deer forests for
Yankee millionaires and British merchant princes who
never fought anything more terrible than turtle soup.

An ancient claimant at Bendigo Old Age
Pension Court astonished the magistrate with the
declaration : ‘ ‘ Behold in me the nephew
of William of Orange ! ” The P.M. gave orders
for a crown—to be paid weekly.
Writes a member of Queensland’s third
contingent: “ The result of our trip was 170
Boers captured, 14,000 cattle taken, and some 500
Boer farm-houses and farms utterly destroyed.”
Just think of the wholesale misery implied in that
“ 500 farms utterly destroyed ! ”
Brisbane Telegraph’s authoritative an-
nouncement of Foxton’s appointment to the
Federal Cabinet misled no end of Q. country
newspapers. Among others, the Capricornian,
an able, respectable journal, published an article
on Foxton, his merits, demerits, and fitness for
the position. Foxton must have got rather weary
of the congratulations.
“S.S.” : Be a par.— Bulletin, Feb. 16—
about a row at Cardiff Infirmary, raised by a good
Protestant whose leg had been interred with a
Catholic corpse, do you forget that this is exactly
the motif of an old Bulletin story, “ Morgan’s
Leg ” ? If the Cardiff yarn is not a steal, the
coincidence is peculiar.
Prof. Judd, of the British Geographical
Society, proceeds to Fiji to find out whether that
sultry land is really an island or not—a point
most people thought was settled. Judd really
wants to ascertain whether Fiji is of continental
origin or a product of ocean upheaval. Dick
Seddon believes it to be an appanage and offshoot
of his kingdom.
Was the usually-very-rational Wallington
giving the newspaper reporters a “ bit of kid ”
when he professed to think that,the Yorks
couldn’t come to Australia, and the honor of
opening the Federal Parliament would “inevit-
ably devolve ”on Hopetoun ? Or did the Queen’s
death so shock his faculties that he couldn’t think
straight? Which was what appeared to be the
matter with the dailies who surmised a visit from
the Queen’s son, Connaught, in lieu of her grand-
son.
Clement Scott, in his new paper, calls
attention to the treatment meted out to colonial
young men who receive commissionsin thelmperial
service. One young Canadian, it is stated, was
absolutely boycotted by his “brother” officers,
and was compelled to dine apart from the mess. I
The colonial officer, it appears, is at best just
tolerated, and no redress can be got from the War
Office, despite repeated complaints. By the way,
even the English-born officer who complains to
the authorities about his social regimental treat-
ment is generally ignored—and “ damned for the
service ” as well.
The Bulletin receives this story with an
assurance that it is perfectly true :
The flavour of the old Sydney Municipal Council lives
after it. About eight months ago ten sanitary inspectors
were advertised for, applications and testimonials to be
sent to the Town Clerk’s office. In reply the applicants
received a printed letter to the effect that the Council
had been graciously pleased to allow each applicant an
opportunity to qualify for the position. A preparatory
course of lectures was to be delivered at Sydney Technical
College between July and Nov., 1900, which it would be
necessary to attend, and for which each applicant
would have to pay 10s. 6d. At the end of the
course an examination would be held and the ten
men who were most successful would be chosen.
Meanwhile, the Council required its inspectors, and ten
mysterious strangers were appointed pro tern. These
fortunate gentlemen attended the lectures at the Tech-
nical College, in company with about 300 other applicants
who had not had any such luck. In addition to the Col-
lege lectures the acting-inspectors received privately, and
free of charge to themselves, additional lectures from a
high medical authority on the legal side of sanitary
science, as to what Acts of Parliament an inspector
required to know before he proceeded, in the execu-
tion of his duty, to mop up unclean premises. The
students at the Technical College received no such
training, and weren’t informed that it was neces-
sary. They learned all about the construction
of houses, prevention of smoke, systems of ven-
tilation and refuse - destructors, but as to what
legal authority to proceed under the lecturer main-
tained the dignified reserve of an oyster. Then came the
examination. Every branch of the exam, bar one, the
fifth, contained the words “ under what authority would
you proceed?” or “ how would you proceed and under
what authority would you act in the following cases ? ”
The 10 acting-inspectors were filled with this information,
while the other 300 had never had the subject mentioned
to them. When the numbers were up it was found that
“ a strange thing had happened.” The 10 gentlemen who
had received the private lectures—free of charge—were
the only ones who had passed the legal part of
the examination, and they were permanently appointed
to the positions they had filled for the previous six
months pro tem. Some of them failed, it is said, on the
scientific part of the examination, but their success in
stating the section of the Act under which to proceed,
carried them through. If there is anything in appearances
it simply amounts to this, that the Council picked out 10
of its proteges to fill 10 positions, and then for appear-
ance’s sake caused 300 outsiders to lose each 10s. 6d. andalot
of valuable time in competing for positions that they had
not the slightest chance of getting, because they were set
to study one set of subjects and then examined on quite a
different set—where they failed, as a matter of course.
337 Kent-street, Sydney,
Bth February, 1901.
rpo THE EDITOR
OF THE
SYDNEY MORNING HERALD.
Sir, —In your leading article of to-dav, you ask,
“After five years’ Freetrade in this State, have
our industries disappeared, are our manufacturers
ruined, and establishments deserted; are our
people without work and bread ? On the con-
trary, our manufacturing interests are sounder
than ever.”
Our extensive works are almost closed, and
most of the people dispersed to the other colonies;
our plant, amongst the most costly in N.S. Wales,
has been lying idle for over two years. The Syd-
ney merchants are well aware of this.
Until the jam, confectionery, and other indus-
tries had their present Protection granted to them
by Mr. Reid, they were in a similar fix, and we
are aware of their present difficulties at the pre-
sent moment.
How is it you claim success under Freetrade
when you are partly living on Projectionist duties,
to say nothing about making up the shortage of
revenue by using borrowed unproductive money,
and the proceeds of our national estate, which is
sold every year, and “called revenue?”
The A.K.O. Candle Works have been closed
for several years : also Kerosene Companies, and
other industries. The Herald makes a false
statement, and refuses to publish a correction.

Like a Decrepit and Infirm
Old Woman.”
A WONDERFUL RESUSCITATION
The Case of Mrs. M. H. EDMONDS,
(BY A LOCAL REPORTER.)
“Will you kindly oblige by giving me
some particulars regarding an extraordi*
nary statement which was lately made in
my presence ? ” The above question was
put by our reporter to Mrs. Margaret
Helen Edmonds, of 11 North-street,
Wellington.
“ Well, said Mrs. Edmonds, “ there are
so many sensational rumours going about
now-a-days that do not concern me at all,
but if you will tell me upon what subject
you are seeking information I will gladly
accede to your request if it lies in my
power.”
“ I desire to know the nature of the com-
plaint from which I hear you recently
suffered.”
“ Oh, yes ; but you will tire of standing
if I tell you in detail, so please take a seat.
My terrible sufferings started with an
attack of acute rheumatism, which first
showed itself by terrific pains in the knee
joints. Before this distinct and awful
agony appeared I felt sensations of chilliness
running through my body for a couple of
days, and I knew that something was going
Wrong. I also perspired very freely, my
skin being very hot. On the second night
after the commencement of these symptoms
I was attacked with frightfully severe pains
in my back. My muscles became so stiff
and ached so much that I was afraid to
stir, as whenever I moved I experienced the
greatest agony imaginable. Indeed, the
slightest movement increased the severity
of the tortures so greatly that I was
obliged to lie in a perfectly helpless con-
dition for hours. I was frightened to death
at the way my pulse was beating. The
blood was fairly bounding through my
veins, and I had attained a very high tem-
perature. It was only with great diffi-
culty and at the expense of much suffering
that I sometimes managed to hobble ab ut
the house like a decrepit and infirm old
woman. My hip joints were also affected
by the torture tlmt was daily drawing the
life out of me. 1 hit feverish and thirsty,
an imy tongue was quite wh'te. The
rheumatism in time spread all over my
body. There was not a limb or a muscle
which escaped the clutches of my dreadful
disease. I got so helpless that L could not
stand on my feet, and when I tried to do
so my legs gave way beneath me. My
'imbs were just as useless as though I weie
paralysed.”
“ Of course, you were under a doctor ?”
“ Certainly I was ; but his treatment was
without avail. Every day and every week
I sank lower. My doctor at last recom-
mended my removal to the hospital, but I
strongly objectedj as I have always looked
upon those institutions with detestation and
horror. Howe er a week or so later my
medical adviser told me that my system
was completely broken down, and that if I
did not them to take me to the
hospital i was sure to die. He gave me to
understand that I was near death’s door, and
that my only chance of pulling through was
to follow his advice. I became greatly
alarmed, as I really felt more dead than
alive, and I consented to go the hospital,
where I remained for a month. I cannot
speak too highly of the kindness and at-
tention shown to me whilst in the institu-
tion. Nobody could be better treated in re-
gard to the painstaking efforts of the
doctors; but still they did not succeed in
improving my pitiful condition. I became
impatient, and left the hospital against the
doctor’s will.”
“ It is a wonder you were able to leave
after so much suffering.”
“ You’re right. It took me all my time.
I was so weak that I frequently fainted
away without doing anything calculated to
produce exhaustion. I was unable to do
anything, yet I was always languid and
fatigued. My nerves were in a shocking
state. A knock at the door, or, in fact,
any unexpected sound, set me trembling
like a leaf, and when darkness came on I
was in a state of nervous terror till daylight
appeared. When I closed my eyes in the
hope, of falling asleep I had ugly images
forming themselves before my imaginative
vision. Fearful thoughts of coming troubles
continually haunted me, and as I could
never get to sleep to escape my mental
tortures I was almost driven crazy.”
“ What kind of diet were you taking ?”
“ Only the very lightest of invalid’s foods,
and very little of that. You can scarotly
credit the awfnl pains I felt in my stomach
after taking food, and behind my shoulders
a feeling was ever present, resembling the
stabbing of a knife. My chest felt as if it
was loaded with lead, and the oppressive
sensation was always accompanied with
great difficulty in breathing. Windy
spasms came cm very often, and besides
causing no end of violent pains in my
stomach my heart was set palpitating to
such an extent that I thought the rapid
healing would only cease when my heart
stopped for ever. My weight was reduced
to about one-half what it used to be before
my illness. Headaches were everlastingly
tormenting me, making me despondent and
miserable to such a degree that I yearned
for death to release me from my every-day
afflictions. Every morning, on arising from
bed, I was attacked with fits of retching
and vomiting. The disease had obtained
such a hold upon me that I despaired of
ever getting better, and I sank to the most
direful depths of misery. When the
doctors failed to cure me I tried many
advertised remedies, but they made me
worse, if possible, than before taking
them.”
“ But you ultimately regained your
health ? ”
“Of course I did, thank God ! But lam
sure I would never have got well had it not
been for Clements Tonic. A friend of mine
called to see me one day, little thinking to
find me so near my grave. After telling her
all about my long months of suffering she
told me she knew of a medicine which
would certainly cure me. I listened with
a feeling of indifference, as I thought there
was not a medicine in the world which I had
not given a trial. I did not even trouble to
inquire what the medicine was called, so
my friend, apparently disappointed at my
evident callousness, volunteered the infor-
mation that the physic she h»d so mu h
confidence in was called Clements Tonic.”
“ Yes, and did you give Clements Tonic a.
trial ? ”
“ I am overjoyed at having done so,
though it took a deal of persuasion, as I
had given up all hope. I sent for one
bottle of Clements Tonic to start with, and
I derived so much benefit from it that I
purchased more. My appetite returned
after several doses, and I was soon able to
eat solid food with a relish. The pains in
my stomach, back, and head quickly
vanished, and my food was no longer
vomited, but digested instead, and did me
good. My nerves were toned and stimu-
lated by Clements Tonic, which also brought
peaceful sleep. The rheumatic troubles
were daily decreased and finally removed
by Clements Tonic, my lost vigour and
strength meanwhile being restored. In
several weeks I was strong, active, and
happy, for which I am under a life-long
obligation to Clements Tonic, and my health
has been splendid since. I would not be
without Clements Tonic in my house for
the world.”
“ May I have your statements printed ? ” [
“Decidedly; publish these facts in anyj
way you deem fit.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Maroarkt Helen Edmonds, of 11 North-street,

do solemnly and sincerely declare that
I have carefully read the annextd document,

numbered from one to three, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of my illntss and cure
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full
permission to publish in any way my statements —
which I give volun'arily, without receiving any pay-
ment; and I make this solemn declaration conscien-
tiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue
of the provisions of an Act of the General Assembly
of New Zealand, intituled “The Justices Peace
Act, 1882."

From a Maoriland trooper’s letter : —
Every corps in S’Africa that I have seen reckon that
they are the smartest and best fighters. So they are, it
one could believe what the generals say. After every
fight the corps are formed up, and the general pulls their
leg for about a quarter of an hour.
British militarism at Pretoria finds the
local Kaffir hurtful to its feelings in a quite unex-
pected way. Officers die freely of enteric there
as elsewhere in the Boer country. They eat too
much meat and rich stuff, drink intoxicants, and
in fact court the cursed disease from which the
seasoned Dutchman religiously guards himself.
Consequently the swell military funeral with band
accompaniment is an every-week occurrence at
headquarters. The Kaffir, who has a good ear for
music, quickly “caught on” to the pretty celes-
tial part of Chopin’s “ Marche Funebre,” and the
result is painfully ludicrous. If a mayor, mourn-
ing the loss of a dear comrade, walks down the
main street of Pretoria he is bound to meet a dam
nigger whistling the favorite bit of Chopin. The
authorities thought to put a stop to this black
horror by substituting Beethoven for Chopin, but
the tuneful Kaffir caught on again, and when last
heard of he was whistling both funeral-marches
with all the joy in life.
Barton has gained a staunch supporter in
an unexpected locale. S.M. Herald’s pup-
reporter warns dog-owners against following the
advice of Dry-dog Reid to throw puppies into
water to teach them to swim, as that would scare
them from water for ever. A man lately gave £1
for a pup, and on his return home one evening he
found his youngest offspring had been experiment-
ing with Toby in the bath and had drowned it.
Three verses of an alleged poem on
Kitchener, solemnly published by Canterbury
(M.L.) Times
Towns of squalid misery,
Acres of barren soil,
By him have been transformed,
Into places for honest toil.
And if in the fulfilling
Of the call that sent him there,
He merited not the approval,
Of correspondents fair,
Should we, then, judge him harshly,
And his reputation kill,
To please the pique or fancy,
Of Winston L. Churchill.

Our New Selection.
IV.—DAD’S TROUBLES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Those days of toil and moil —that weary, up-hill
struggle of Shingle Hut —were only thought of
now in moments of merriment. Queer old days
wild old ways that all of us loved to remember
none of us wished to forget.
Farming was not the drag—the wretched, mur-
derous drudgery it used to be. We were improv-
ing every day—rapidly climbing to the lap of
comfort. The wheat turned out a success again,
and the profit made us all rejoice. Still we kept our
heads. The frequent want of a shilling had taught
us the’worth of one. We were not extravagant.
Mother in her thankfulness attributed our success
to the mercy and goodness of God. Dad reckoned
’twas all due to his own head.
“Well, yairs,” he would sometimes admit, upon
pressure, “th’ boys does a good deal, an’ the
women’s done a bit one way er another, too, but
all with th’ hands ; an’ where’d be th’ good o’ it if
there wer’ no head ? . . . Their hands, but my
head ! ”
On that point Dad was emphatic. From his
decision there was no appeal. . Dad was the
Judge, the Full Court, and the Privy Council, too,
at our selection.
Things were worked methodicallv —almost re-
duced to a science. To Dave was allotted a three-
furrow plough and a set of horses which none but
himself used; Joe had a double-furrow and
separate horses; Little Bill rose at cockcrow and
brought all horses fi'om the grass-paddock and
drove them back last thing at night—drove them
gently in obedience to Dad’s orders until he got
out of sight, when he rushed and raced them for
their lives, and flogged them through the. rails.
The horses did their best to get through without
maiming themselves, but the odds were always on
Little Bill.
Dad poked and pottered about—didn’tdomuch —
did very little in a most impressive way. He fed
the horses, and patched bags, and made a leg-rope
occasionally, and sooled the dog on the fowls if
ever they approached the cultivation ; and coo-eed
and shook his fist menacingly at Dave and Joe
when they sat on the ploughs," yarning; and fol-
lowed in the tracks of the men who pulled and
gathered the corn, and found cobs they missed,
and swore. He rarely remained longer than a
minute in one place; he was everywhere, warn-
ing and worrying everyone. He praised the
farm and explained things to anyone who called,
and in a lofty manner disregarded the solicitations
of travellers —he put them all off with an eloquent
wave of the hand. Travellers were not fond of
Dad.
• * * *
A bright, sunny day, after a heavy frost. Dave
and Joe following the ploughs—up and down, and
round and round. Joe came to a standstill and
stared across the field.
“What th’ deuce is he after now?” he said,
following the running form of Dad with his eyes.
Dave stopped his team and stared also.
Dad crossed the cultivation, entered the grass-
paddock and ran along a wide gully through some
thick timber. Three of the Regan boys—ranging
from 14 to 20, were there doing something with a
stout springy sapling; they had at bent, bow-
flshion to the ground, and kept in position by a
lever? A wire noose was fastened to the sapling.
It was an ingenious arrangement they had con-
ceived to catch dingoes in, and they were stand-
ing contemplating, its construction Dad came
with a rush, and tried to fall on one of them. But
the Regans were all runners. They decamped
“ If I don’t make yer eyes black, when I catches
yer, then damn me! ” Dad shouted after them.
They ran harder. . , ,
Dad turned to the bent sapling;—looked at it—
wondered what it meant. He kicked up leaves
and dirt in search of enlightenment. He kicked
his foot into the noose and tripped. The sapling-
left the lever and flew up, taking Dad with it.
Dad was enlightened. The sapling wasn t strong
enough to swing all of him m the air—it elevated
his heels till only his head and chest and arms
were in touch with the ground. He swung like
a slaughtered bullock partly hoisted to the
gallows. He swore lamentably—he roared and
wriggled, and kicked with the limb that was free,
and clutched at the grass. He bared a patch of
ground about his head and went through a series
of swimming movements with his hands. Yet he
swung. .
A pocket-knife and a two-shillmg piece fell out
of his trousers-pocket. He clutched them and
held them fast in his hand. Then he ceased
struggling and began to use his head.
Dave and Joe were still staring in the direction
of the gully and wondering. But they were
nearly a mile off.
* * * *
Regan’s dog, which had gone hunting through
the paddock on its own account while the Regan
boys were setting the snare, returned panting to
the place. It was a dog with a lot of the bull
breed in it—an ugly, surly, sulky dog with a thick
drooping under-lip. It had a bad name in the
district. Dad knew of its reputation. It trotted
up. Dad was hanging motionless, thinking.
He heard the noise. Feelings of hope and
thankfulness entered his soul. He strained, and
leaning on his hands turned his eyes up to welcome
his deliverer. He saw the dog and recognised it,
and groaned. The dog saw him and stood, staring.
It was surprised—astonished. It growled coarsely,
gruffly. A cold, creepy feeling passed all through
Dad. He glared at the brute with eyes of terror.
Then, spurred by desperation, he kicked
vigorously and howled out for help. The dog
sprang back, inclined to fly, but seeing that Dad
remained stationary it faced him and barked.
And such a bark !
Dad blackened in the face—his eyes threatened
to burst. He tried to throw the two-shilling
piece hard at the dog. It rolled short. He
opened the pocket-knife with his teeth. The dog
came closer. It trotted round Dad—drew still
nearer, barking, barking. Dad flinched, and
grasped the knife tighter. There was a pause-
on the dog’s part. Dad collected his thoughts.
He became resourceful. He softened his voice
and said affectionately:
“Poor old chap ! . . . poor old fellow! . . . .
poor — old — hoy ! ”
But Regan’s dog knew a thing or two ; besides
it had become conscious of Dad’s helplessness.
Meanwhile Dave and Joe, weary of wondering,
went on ploughing. But the Regan boys, attracted
by the barking of their dog, cautiously re-entered
the paddock and approached the gully. When in
sight of the snare they took in the situation and
ran up, shouting to the dog to desist. The sound
of their voices only emboldened the brute. It
misunderstood. Their shrieks it took for words
of encouragement, and, laying bare its teeth, it
rushed in and barked close against Dad’s ribs.
Dad shuddered and writhed. His flesh twitched.
He missed the dog’s nose with the pocket-knife.
In return the dog snatched a mouthful of his
pants and some of his thigh, and would have had
more only the Regan boys came up and beat it
off with sticks.
They released Dad in a hurry and ran away
again; there was nothing mean or mercenary
about them.
STEELE RUDD.
Not a Bad Sort—A Hobblede-
hoy’s Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAD only known Kellow half-an-hour when, as
we were lying on the grass, he asked me for a fill
as he saw me lighting my own pipe. I handed
him a new cake ; he cut the corner off it and gave
it (the corner) back to me, and after filling his
pipe, put the rest in his pocket. He was affable
enough in conversation, and I gathered that he
had a profound contempt for all chumps and
mugs. He asked me if I had anything particular
in view for next day. Told him I hadn’t. “Then
what say to a day’s fishing ? ” I acquiesced, and
he was grateful enough to tell me I could get the
lines and hooks, whilst he would provide the
sinkers. He got these at the blacksmith’s shop,
and I don’t know that the man of iron was any
the richer after it. As I could see no alternative,
I accordingly bought two lines and the necessary
hooks.
We met in the morning at the appointed time
and place, and proceeded to the rocks. On the
way, we had to pass a pub. Would he have a
drink? Well, he should smile ! I shouted, and
we went on. Twenty yards past the pub. he
stopped, and intimated that a bottle of schnapps
would no way interfere with the biting of the fish.
I also thought it wouldn’t. “I’ll hold the bag,
then,” said he, taking it from me ; and again had
I to bow to the inevitable, and go and get the
tanglefoot. I carried the bag, half-full of iron
sinkers, while he walked about four yards ahead
with the bottle.
Arrived at the rocks, he told me to take off my
boots and see about getting bait, and to lend him
my knife to cut off some cunji he could see on a
rock. As he was not over expert at cutting cunji,
he soon broke the knife. Then he looked up at
me in great disgust. I had kept that knife for
over five years, and valued it dearly. I explained
that the storekeeper guaranteed it to be of the
best. “ Yes,” said he ; “he knew when he was
dealing with a chump.” (Kellow himself evidently
did). One good blade still remained. “ Well, it’s
no use now,” he said as he put it in his pocket.
I never saw it again, except in Kellow’s hands.
On the first throw, my amiable friend’s line, cork
and all, went flying out to sea. Then he blas-
phemed and denounced me for not holding the
cork. He sat down and I fished. Presently I
got a good bite, and stood up to prepare. “ A
bite?” said my mate. I nodded. “Let’s feel,”
he said as he took the line and moved to another
part of the rock, and gave me to understand
that the day’s fishing, as far as I was
concerned, had terminated. I had to sit
down and feel interested in the horizon.
Presently, Rellow hauled in a nice schnapper.
I reckoned that it should be mine—and said so.
Rellow calmly ignored me, and threw out again.
Determined on retribution, I put myself outside
the schnapps and both our lunches on the sly.
Then I felt better, and, after a bit, the spirits
began to have due effect on me, so that, when
Rellow ordered me to climb down the.rock and
get a big crab' he had seen, I overbalanced,
and in I went. When I came to the sur-
face, the. “drawback” had me out about 10
ifards. I went down twice and then lost
Consciousness. When I came-to, Rellow —the
hateful Rellow, on whom I had resolved to
have revenge—was pulling me up on a low shelf
of rock, himself dripping wet and a bit scared.
“Wait till I get you the schnapps,” he said, and
ran for it. But, alas! Then he looked in the
lunch-basket. Alack !
Rellow said nothing, but his silence was elo-
quent. He heaved into the water all his line,
then the bait and bottle; the 61b. fish followed,
and then my bag, which had cost me ss. 6d. at
the saddler’s. Then he put on his boots, roared
“ Come on home! ” and led the way. Only once
he turned round and said, “Just don’t you ask me
to go fishing with you again ! ”
I still buy drinks for Rellow and give him cakes
of tobacco (of which I get a corner back), and ask
him to dinner on Sundays. He’s not a bad sort,
after all.
B. BLUEY.
About a hundred papers in different parts
of this population-hungry country have risen to
cordially approve of Chief-Justice Stout’s pro-
posal that Boers be invited to immigrate in large
numbers to Australia. Perth Morning Herald
goes so far as to say
It would be difficult to find a more desirable class of
immigrant than the Boer farmer. His industry is pro-
verbial, and he would prove in every way as estimable a
settler as, for instance, the German farmers in S.A.
Truly the allegedly dirty, lazy, shiftless, cowardly
Boer, who—so the frantic Jingoes of a few months
ago assured us—had all the bad qualities under
the sun, is beginning to look-up. He is now a
man whose industry is proverbial, and the best
possible man we could get to show the Australian
the way he should go. Yet there are still some
who stick to the old beliefs. For instance, “Ser-
geant-major H. T. Bead, N.S.W. Lancers,” writes
to “Mr. B. F. Evans,” of Sydney Sunday Times,
and B. F. publishes it in the kitchen-wenches
organ :
I have found out their (the Boers’) peculiarities to my
sorrow. I will sum them up in these few words— they
are a lying, thieving, vindictive, treacherous lot of
hounds; they are even treacherous to one another,
“ these poor, ignorant, God-fearing people,” as our pro-
Boer sympathisers would say. These are expressions
used by people who never lived with them. Their
religion ! God forbid that I should ever use religion for
so base an obiect, as the Boers only use it to cloak their
iniquities. I'cannot write too severely on this subject,
as I have studied their actions minutely. lam willing to
admit that there are a few good men among them, but
they are few and far between, and as to the women, they
are worse than the men. There is no such a thing as love
or sympathy in their composition. They even urge their
husbands to disregard their oaths and take up arms and
go on commando. I am thoroughly disgusted with the
race, for I did think there was some spark of honor and
gratitude in these people ; but I find they are devoid of
sentiment, conscientiousness, or truth.
“ A look from her would ripen fruit.” —
Balzac.

A Sydney Man.

Some parts are better than others ; but while working in the shafts one is continually damp, and this
affected my health. My trouble came with a bad cold in my kidneys, which caused terrible pains in
the back. I did my work, but it was most fearful torture for me to do so, as you can well understand.
I was exceedingly bad with these pains, when I saw Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills advertised, and it
struck me by the name of the remedy that it should be good for my complaint. I procured a box at

two doses a week. The nature of my work causes me to have slight returns of the pain ; but I take a
dose of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, which never fails to cure me. lam never without them in the
house.”
