Man and Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Love me ! ” she whispered, and I saw a fear
Crouch shuddering ’neath the portals of her eyes:
“ Love me for ever ! Call me always Dear ! ”
I kissed her lightly, and a burning tear
Scorched my false lips and shamed my feeble lies.
Ido not love her. Hush l <S he must not hear t
I caught her fiercely, pressed her face to mine.
“Love you, my dearest? Ay, till Death us part.”
(It sounded like an old-time echoed line,
Yet through the tears I saw those brave eyes
shine.)
With lies I soothed the storm-tossed woman-heart.
I cannot love / Not hers the fault / Nor mine /
So steals my lying soul through year to year ;
I loved her once—hut now—she must not hear.
V.
MATCHBOX.
An Hour in the Dead Country.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the home of one of the “wool-kings ” that
they have made books about. That is the wool-
king himself over there —browned, with close-
cropped grey beard, bared arms, slouch hat, and
leather-girdled waist. And this, who brings in
the chops, and is sorry there are no potatoes, is
the wool-king’s wife —the wool-queen. She also
cooked the chops.
“No, M‘Lean,” Martin, the wool-king is
saying—“no,” running his eye over a dozen
sheep, “I don’t think it’s worth while killing
them. We’ll have to see if we can’t pick out
something a bit better from another paddock.” It
is one of the “ great ” sheep-runs of the out-back;
yet they have to go from one paddock to another
to get a sheep fit to kill!
We join Martin and fill our pipes. Then we
look at the sky. First to the north. “The
January rains are late,” he says, and turns to the
south. No consolation either way. Last month
they talked of “the December rains.” Neither
useful December nor January rains have come
oftener than once in seven years; but they all
talk fondly of them, as children do of Christmas
—it is hope that they live upon.
We smoke in silence for ten minutes. Then Mar-
tin takes his pipe from his mouth, and has no idea
what a pitiful picture he makes as he looks at the
sky again. “ Well, you know,” he says with con-
viction, “it’s better in one way for keeping hot
like this —there's more chance of getting the fag-
end of the monsoon rains. •”
The prospect makes him almost cheerful; and
he talks of the awful past and present almost as if
they were buried. “ Our last rains came just a bit
more than 12 months ago—we got 2inches then.
Not much, but it gave us a lambing, the first we’d
had for five years. None this year, not a lamb.
Our best ? Well, it went up to 24,000 in my time.
“Yes—no, it isn’t a whirlwind ; a horseman, I
make-out.” After ten minutes of silence —
“Burton, one of Dalgety’s men. Came up in
August. Saw the shearing through, and then ran
out of water; he’s been sitting alongside ever
since, waiting for enough rain to wash the clip
with.”
Burton can’t stand it any more, and is making
down to Broken Hill to wait. It isn’t only the
being idle ; it’s the awfulness of being surrounded
by nothing but the dead and dying.
“Oh, they’ve taken the ladies away”—the part-
owner’s wife and the governess. “They’ve gone
out to the Blue Lake ; there’s a pretty fair ‘ soak ’
there; last for a couple of months, anyway. Yes,
they’re right back on the wells, too, now; not a
drop left in the tanks, and the stock are doing
very badly on the well-water. Well, they could
perhaps muster 16,000 if they got rain at once.”
That holding carried 110,000, and fattened them,
not so long ago.
“So I hear good old Jobson’s gone down,”
Martin says. “ Well, he made a good fight of it.
Close on 25 years, and hard graft all the time.
Still, he’s been weakening on it a good while.
The Govt, inspector was up before the last ap-
praisement. ‘ Your holding isn’t sufficiently im-
proved, Mr, Jobson,’ he says; ‘it would carry
more sheep if you had two 20,000-yard tanks.’
‘Yes, if sheep’d live on water,’ says Jobson.”
“ Paid ’em all off ? ” Burton asked—he had got
round to the station-hands and the question of ex-
penses.
“All but Ryan; he’s out at the Black Well.
Young Tom is working the Eaglehawk engine.
M‘Lean ?—he’s just waiting for the camels to
come with feed; he can’t get across without it,
and we’re just about out.”
It was time to be moving-on. I noticed the
“cheque” Martin paid me was an order on a
mortgage-co. “ Yes,” he said, “ it’s come to that
at last—orders now. And that’s what ‘killed’
poor old Jobson, between you and me.”
I left him looking again into the cloudless north
for the February rains.
P.X.
“ H.B.T.”about matters New Hebridean:
Such trade as exists at present from the N. Hebrides is
a monopoly in the hands of Burns, Philp and Co., who,
haveahold over the majority of white traders in the group.
The highness of freights for island-produce to Sydney
practically compels traders to sell their stocks on the
spot at such prices as it suits the buyers to offer. It’s “ Take
it or leave it.” Administration there is practically none.
A British man-of-war is on cruise amongst the islands
generally from May to Nov., during which period the
mission stations are visited, and any murders of whites
undergo a so-called investigation, which nearly always
results in the offending village being burnt. By the
way, the decisions in favor of the French, re-
cently given by the joint commission—which out
of six members included three officers of the
Ringarooma—have destroyed the belief of the natives
that the British would not see their land unjustly taken
from them. The animus between British and French
residents is increased by the unequal restrictions under
which they trade. The Britisher or Australian, being
forbidden to sell arms, ammunition, or liquor to the
natives, has, in order to compete with the unrestricted
Frenchman, to do so sub rosa —else why do the manifests
of island-traders from Sydney contain such large lines of
“split peas” and such an excessive number of “broom-
handle ” cases ? If this strategicallv-important group
is not soon to pass over to the French flag, this question
of dual control must be pushed home, and a permanent
settlement satisfactory to Australia arrived at. Delay
will only encourage France to further aggressions, besides
further weakening the belief of the native in the ability
of the British to ensure that his land will not be unjustly
alienated. Vila Harbor, Havannah Harbor, and Undine
Bay would make admirable naval stations, and doubtless
this is what the Frenchman has in mind, as a counter-
move to the establishment of an Anglo-Saxon nation in
the S. Pacific.
“Useless.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Desolation Gully lay amongst the hills. Be-
hind it was a ragged face of rock, noted for its
wallabies and prickly-pear. On either side the
rough, uneven hills were burdened with thorny
scrub, here and there dotted with grass-trees. In
front lay a small broken flat, mainly taken up by
a hopeless, straggling creek.
Sometimes in the early spring mornings, as the
sun came gently up over the eastern range, and
the mists broke back, softly and tenderly, from
scrub and rock, Desolation Gully was beautiful.
The four houses resting in the valley glowed green
and gold, the. parrots whirred and screamed
amongst the golden wattles, shaking down showers
of bloom, the dew lay light and graceful upon the
herbage, leaving dull lines of broken green where
the cows had trodden on their way to the yards.
But the dwellers in Desolation Gully knew
little of beauty; they were struggling with the
sordidness of poverty, and had no taste nor incli-
nation towards artistic feeling. While the scene
around them lay like a dream of the gods, the chil-
dren built their castles of cowdung. One amongst
them, a boy of ten, was different from the rest.
While the others played together he would wander
away alone, watching the play of the parrots and
dreaming over the soft-eyed wallabies as they sat
quietly on the rocks and wonderingly looked at
him with large, strange eyes. “Mooney,” the
other children called him. “Useless,” said his
mother. “Wait and see,” said the father;
“ some day if he is needed you will find the grit
that is in him.”
One day, when they had been out together split-
ting rails, he had learnt of what stuff the boy was
made. “ Tush ! ” said the mother when he men-
tioned it, “that does not bring grist to the mill.”
The father sighed and went back to his rail-split-
ting, leading the old horse that was to jink the
timber out of the hills. As they passed through
the scrub the thorns kept tearing at the old bag
collar. The wheat husks with which it was
stuffed began to trickle out. “ Hang it,” said the
man, “ I’ll have to go and get a bit of string to fix
the thing.” The dreamer stepped from behind
the bushes. “ I’U go,” he said, carelessly.
When he got back the men had gone on. Fol-
lowing the tracks, the boy came to where the man
had been at work. He called; no reply. A
wallaby sat out on a point of rock and looked at
him wonderingly. He called again softly, almost
fearfully. Must have gone to sleep, he thought,
and went on tracking the horse. After he had
gone a few yards he looked up, saw the horse, and
went over to him. A little way off his father lay,
apparently asleep, but looking again the boy saw
that a red-gum had caught him in its fall. In
dodging he had tripped on a stone, and now a
limb of the tree lay full across him. With a
slight gasp the boy set to work to try and drag it
off. He stood for a moment and thought. Taking
the water-bag, he went to a spring close by and
brought back some water. He went up to the
man, and made as if to pour some on his face.
“No,” he said, “may as well be dead till I get
the tree off.” He took up the axe, but it was too
heavy for him to use. Dropping it, he
brought the horse over. “It all depends on you,
Sleepy,” he said; “you’ll have to stick to yer
hold.” He hooked the horse on to the limb,
stroked his weary old head, and said, gently:
“Git, Sleepy!” The horse pulled, the branch
eased up off the man. “Hang to it!” said
the boy, and ran back behind the horse.
His father was a heavy man, he could
move him very little at a time. The
limb began to settle back. “Git, Sleepy ! ” called
the boy from his work. The old horse settled to
it again. Gradually the boy worked his father
clear. “Back, Sleepy !” he called. With a sigh
of relief, the poor old horse eased off. Taking up
the water-bag, the boy looked at the man.
“ Better come round now,” he said, “and tell me
what to do.” He dashed the water over the man’s
face and head.
The man eame-to. He looked up heavily.
“Eh! What is it, Tommy!” he asked,
dreamily. The boy poured some more water.
The man’s eyes opened again. He began to take
in the situation. “Will Igo and fetch ’em from
the house ? ” asked the boy. “ No,” answered the
man, “ you’ll do.” The boy sat and waited. He
was tired after his work. “ Get a sheet of bark
off that log,” said the man, nodding to the butt of
the freshly-fallen tree. The boy set-to, wondering
what it was for. The man’s eyes closed again.
The boy worked on. When he had got the bark
off he came and looked at the man. He
seemed to, be asleep. The boy thought a
little. Cutting two sticks, he fastened them on
to the bark. Hooking Sleepy on, he drew
the rough sled down below his father and prepared
to put him on. When he moved him the man
awoke. “ What’s up ? ” he asked sleepily, and was
off again. The boy placed him on the bark.
“ Steady, Sleepy ! ” he said, and they started
down-hill. Every now and then they stopped
while the boy moved a stone out of the way. Then
they went on again.
When they reached the edge of the scrub next
the house the boy unhooked Sleepy and went on.
“Mother,” he said, when he reached the house,
“ father’s got a bit of a knock. I’m bringing him
home.”
The woman moaned and sat down on a chair.
“I told 'im ’e’d be gettin’ ’urt, goin’ up there by
’isself,” she growled. “’E took no notice of me;
’e alius was pig-’eaded.” The boy went back to
his work.
When he got to the house the woman came and
looked at the man on the sled. “Fool!” she
said savagely, “’e’sdead, I alius Anew you was
useless !”
WILL M. FLRMING.
It has been formally resolved by Mel-
bourne’s City Council that Bard Fitzgibbon, of
the Metropolitan Board of Shirks, is making the
city ridiculous by his fulsome addresses and his
silly cablegrams to England whenever some royal
person is suffering from a severe corn. Also, the
Council formally requests the Government to try
if it can make the absurd Fitzgibbon attend to
business and leave off hysterics.
A very enthusiastic bigamist of many
aliases was brought to light in London the other
week. His system was to advertise for a wife
with £3O capital, and marry the confiding house-
maids, cooks, laundresses, &c., as they came along.
From January 6th to October 18th of last year he
is known to have acquired four little bits of capi-
tal with females attached, the shortest recorded
interval between honeymoons being four days.
But probably a dozen or two other pining spin-
sters and widows were duped and have said noth-
ing about it to the police. England, with its
many densely-populated towns and its London,
where any man can get lost whilst growing a
beard or a pair of new whiskers, is the paradise of
bigamists.
